MAP
Second Phase of the 380-kV Transmission System -(No. IBRD 12562R)



APPRAISAL OF THE
SECOND POWER TRANSMISSION PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        This report appraises a Power Project covering the 1977-1982 ex-
pansion (second phase) of the 380-kV transmission system of the Yugoslav
power organizations associated in the Union of Yugoslav Power Industry
(JUGEL), responsible for the generation and transmission of electricity
throughout the country.
ii.       The Project comprises about 1,000 km of lines, extension of 8 sub-
stations and construction of 16 new substations aggregating about 10,000
MVA in transformer capacity, and 9 dispatch centers. The total cost of the
Project is estimated at US$681 million including a foreign exchange component
of US$274 million. The Project forms a small part (about 5.7%) of Yugoslavia's
1976-1982 development program for generation and transmission, amounting to
US$10.8 billion.
iii.      A Bank loan of US$80 million is proposed, which would cover about
30% of the foreign exchange cost. Disbursements would be made for 100% of
the c.i.f. costs, or ex-factory costs of Yugoslav manufacturers, of equipment
under contracts to be awarded in accordance with the Bank Group Guidelines
for Procurement after international competitive bidding, and for consultant
services. No retroactive financing is proposed. Yugoslav manufacturers, who
would have a preference of 15% or applicable duties, whichever is the lesser,
are expected to be successful in being awarded the contracts for most of
equipment. To augment the proposed loan, JUGEL, together with a committee
of the Federal Secretariat for Finance, has solicited offers for financing
from most major international suppliers of transmission equipment and has
received letters of intent from about 100 foreign and Yugoslav firms. These
offers cover all equipment suitable for international bidding (up to 45% of
the foreign exchange costs). The balance of the foreign exchange costs, which
are essentially direct foreign costs associated with civil works, erection
costs and associated contingencies, would be funded from other sources. The
European Investment Bank is actively considering a loan of about US$30 mil-
lion. The local costs would be financed by internally generated funds and
local loans.
iv.       There would be a single loan agreement between the Bank, JUGEL,
the borrowers, and the composite organizations of Macedonia, Montenegro and
Serbia (which would guarantee the financial performance on behalf of their
constituent organizations). The six borrowers would be the composite organi-
zations for generation and transmission in three of the six republics (Bosnia-
Herzegovina, Croatia, Slovenia) and the three organizations responsible for
constructing and operating transmission facilities in Macedonia, Montenegro



- ii -
and Serbia (where the work organization for transmission also covers the auton-
omous provinces of Kosovo and Vojvodina). There would be 18 executing agencies
responsible for the technical implementation of the Project. The loan would
be guaranteed by the Federal Government and would be prorated to the six
borrowers according to the share of each republic of the total cost of the
Project.
v.        The Bank has made three loans for power in Yugoslavia.  The first
(277-YU) and second (318-YU) loans, each for US$30 million, were made re-
spectively for the 266-MW Senj hydro plant and 360-MW Bajina Basta hydro
plant which were both completed in 1969 and are operating satisfactorily.
The third loan (836-YU) of US$75 million was made in 1972 for the first phase
of the 380-kV transmission system comprising some 3,000 km of lines and 7,700
MVA of substation capacity; this project is nearing completion. A Bank loan
of US$70 million for the 450-MW Buk Bijela hydropower station in Bosnia-
Herzegovina failed to become effective because the Federal Government could
not obtain ratification of the Guarantee Agreement due to an inter-republican
riparian dispute over the use of the waters at the proposed site, which arose
subsequent to the Bank's appraisal and consideration of the loan by the Board.
A Bank sector mission carried out an extensive review of the Yugoslav energy
sector in October 1975.
vi.       Bank lending for the proposed Project would provide an opportunity
to continue the institution-building which was one of the main objectives
under the third power loan. While technical difficulties have not been beyond
those to be normally expected, institutional problems have been serious since
1965, when economic reform accelerated the policy of decentralization and
self-management, fragmenting the sector which, before 1965, was under strong
federal control. Some measure of integration through coordination has been
reinstituted, particularly following the constitutional changes in 1974, and
the process of integration and coordination in the planning and operation
of the sector is sought to be strengthened through the proposed lending
arrangements.
vii.      The proposed Project has a key role to play in achieving two major
goals of national energy policy, which are to end the perennial power deficits
by the end of this decade and to make maximum use of domestic energy resources
in achieving this. In accordance with the first objective, sales are projected
to grow at 10%/a on average and to double to 70 TWh by 1982, when installed
capacity would reach some 18,700 MW; in accordance with the second objective,
this expansion will be based essentially on generating plants utilizing the
country's main primary energy sources of hydropower and coal (mostly lignite).
The location of these generating plants is largely dictated by the location
of these primary energy resources, which are spread unevenly throughout the
country. The 380-kV system expansion under the proposed Project is the least-
cost means of connecting these dispersed sites and transmitting the energy
to the load centers. The estimated economic rate of return is at least 13%.
viii.     Under Loan 836-YU JUGEL undertook to engage management consultants
for a study of management procedures throughout the power system and prep-
aration of recommendations for improvements, and of the priorities and geo-
graphical locations of future power projects. The management consultants were



- iii -
engaged about three years late because the borrowers could not reach consensus
on the terms of reference, the amount of services and responsibilities of
local subcontracting consultants and the selection of the main consultant
responsible for the study. The study is now expected to be completed by
mid-1978.
ix.       Because a countrywide least-cost power development study is, in
principle, inconsistent with the Yugoslav constitution (each republic is
autonomous and its objective is to meet power requirements in the republic
at minimum cost to its own economy), a country-wide study which would provide
ranking of power plants in economic merit order was initiated only in November
1976. Although not financed by the Bank, the study is being executed in
accordance with terms of reference made available by the Bank. The Bank has
obtained assurances that the study would be completed satisfactorily since it
would provide guidance on the economic impact of republican decisions exer-
cised in accordance with each republic's autonomous procedures.
x.        The Bank has been urging for some time that a tariff study be made
because the tariff structure has not been changed since its implementation
in 1965 (although since 1970 annual increases have raised the levels by 180%
compared with a 160% increase in the cost of living since 1970) but this study
too was started only in November 1976. Again, no Bank financing was requested.
The Bank has obtained assurances that the study would be completed satisfac-
torily, including a marginal costing approach.
xi.       The implementation of the Project would largely be in accordance
with the procedures agreed upon for the first transmission project (Loan 836-YU)-
i.e. JUGEL would sign the Loan Agreement as coordinator with the assistance of
a coordinating committee representing the borrowers and the executing agencies
and would be responsible, without assuming any financial obligations, for all
actions to be taken for withdrawal of loan proceeds and repayment of the loan.
Since, however, the arrangement under Loan 836-YU failed in part to meet the
objectives of coordination because of JUGEL's lack of authority to act on
behalf of the borrowers, JUGEL and the borrowers have agreed to certain modifi-
cations in these procedures including establishment of a Project Unit and in-
creasing JUGEL's authority for Project execution. Agreement was also reached
on increasing JUGEL's responsibility for operation of the power system through
amending the agreement for dispatching throughout the country.
xii.      Under Loan 836-YU, although four of the 12 borrowers did not achieve
the average 35% cash contribution required as a minimum during 1971-75, aver-
age financial performance was satisfactory. A similar cash contribution
requirement (30% on an annual basis), would be agreed to by the borrowers
under the proposed loan. Improved financial performance is expected because
under the proposed loan financial performance would be undertaken by the
republican composite organizations, unlike the arrangement under Loan 836-YU
where the borrowers that undertook financial performance were, in effect, the
executing agencies. Net internal cash generation, together with funds to be
provided by special measures (such as surcharges on electricity consumption),
is expected to finance 38% of the 1976-82 investment program.



- iv -
xiii.     In view of the agreements reached on the matters outlined in Section
7, a Bank loan of US$80 million is recommended for a term of 15 years with a
3-year grace period. Completion of arrangements for local funds (iii), and
signature of an agreement among the borrowers covering coordination of project
execution and full operation of the Project Unit (xi) would be conditions of
effectiveness of the proposed loan, which could be effective within five
months of signing.



APPRAISAL OF THE
SECOND POWER TRANSMISSION PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
1. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The Federal Government of Yugoslavia, on behalf of the power orga-
nizations responsible for generation and transmission throughout the country,
which are all members of the Union of Yugoslav Power Industry (JUGEL), has
requested Bank financing for the further development of a 380-kV transmission
network estimated to cost US$681 million equivalent. The sum will constitute
5.7% of the country's 1976-1982 generation and transmission development
program. A Bank loan of US$80 million is proposed to finance part (30%) of
the foreign exchange cost of this Project. The Project comprises about 1,000
km of single circuit lines, 13 new substations and the extension of 8 substa-
tions aggregating about 10,000 MVA in transformer capacity, 3 switching
stations and 9 dispatch centers.
1.02      The Project is the second phase of a 380-kV national grid.  The con-
struction of the first phase started in 1970 and was accelerated by a 1972
Bank loan of US$75 million (836-YU). The first phase which consists of 3,000
km of lines and 7,700 MVA in transformer capacity is scheduled for completion
by the end of 1977. A third phase, which is scheduled for commissioning by
1985, will complete the 380-kV network and set the stage for the preparation
of the next (as yet undecided) higher voltage transmission network. The tech-
nical and feasibility studies for the three phases were prepared by JUGEL
in consultation with its power generation and transmission members.
1.03      Three loans for electric power development in Yugoslavia have been
made previously: Loan 277-YU (US$30 million; 1961) for the 266-MW Senj
hydro plant on the Lika/Gacka rivers in Croatia, Loan 318-YU (US$30 million;
1962) for the 360-MW Bajina Basta hydro station on the Drina in Serbia - both
stations were completed in 1969 and are presently operating satisfactorily -
and Loan 836-YU. A Bank loan of US$70 million, made in 1975 for the 450-MW
Buk Bijela hydropower station in Bosnia-Herzegovina, failed to become effective
because the Federal Government could not obtain ratification of the Guarantee
Agreement due to an inter-republican riparian dispute over the use of the
waters at the proposed station site, which arose subsequent to the Bank's
appraisal and consideration of the loan by the Board. A power component
(34-MW hydro) is also included in the Ibar multipurpose water project (Loan
777-YU; 1971) which is scheduled for completion in 1977. Also, a Bank sector
mission carried out an extensive review of the Yugoslav energy sector in
October 1975.
1.04      The main difficulties encountered by the Bank in the power subsector
have been institutional. In 1965 economic reform initiated a policy of decen-
tralization and self-management. As a result, the Yugoslav power sector
changed from a highly centralized federally controlled sector (before 1965)
to a totally decentralized form of organization. However, since 1970 (as



-2-
formulated by the constitutional changes of 1974) some measure of integration
through coordination was reintroduced principally at the republican level.
The Bank's consistent encouragement of more effective coordination in the
power sector may have assisted in the movement towards integration. The
proposed loan will promote strengthening of coordination in system planning,
financing and operations of the subsector.
1.05      This report was prepared by W.F. Kupper (Engineer), I. Mathai
(Financial Analyst), I. Elwan (Economist) and C. Warren (Young Professional).
It is based on JUGEL's feasibility study and the information obtained by Bank
missions which visited Yugoslavia in September/October and November 1976, com-
prising -- in addition to the above staff -- Messrs. P.A. Cordukes (Financial
Analyst) and A.E. Maffei (Legal).
2. THE ENERGY AND POWER SUBSECTOR
General
2.01      Yugoslavia is a federation of six republics and two autonomous
provinces and has an estimated population of 21.6 million (1974). The eco-
nomic system that has evolved in Yugoslavia resembles the market economies
of Western Europe and the United States in many aspects and is considerably
different from the centrally managed, government controlled economies of the
Eastern European countries. The central feature of the Yugoslav economy is
the concept of "social property", i.e. most of the means of production are
owned by society under management of workers that use them and are joined
together in "self management" organizations. The social property therefore
is not state owned, the workers are not government employees and their
organizations are not state agencies.  A private sector co-exists with the
social sector in the form of small farms and businesses that account for 15%
of GDP.
2.02      Economic decision-making in Yugoslavia is highly decentralized.  It
is largely outside the scope of Government control at any level. The basic
decision-making units in each subsector are called "Basic Organizations of
Associated Labor" (BOAL). In principle, these are technically identifiable
units that function independently at the lowest level of business activity.
BOALs can be integrated into "Work Organizations of Associated Labor" (WOAL).
At the republican level, several WOALs and/or BOALs may integrate into a
single body referred to as a "Composite Organization of Associated Labor"
(COAL). These organizations are governed at the various levels by councils
that are elected by their respective member workers. 1/ The organizations
at all levels evolve through a set of revocable contracts denoted as "self-
management agreements". The relationships within a sector and between sectors
as well as the authorities' limited jurisdiction over them are regulated by
"social agreements".
1/   See Annex 1, Attachment I for the 1974 constitutional definition
of the forms of organization.



2.03      Electric power is considered a social or public good.  Sectors
and/or subsectors that are engaged in the production of social goods are
regulated by bodies known as the "self-management communities of interest".
The communities are composed of the organizations of associated labor pro-
ducing the goods or services and the organizations representing the consumers.
Their principal function is to provide the framework for developing, financing
and regulating the activities of their respective sectors or subsectors.
The Energy Sector
2.04      Energy Resources. 1/  The following information summarizes the
findings of the energy sector review mission of October 1975 (1.03).
Yugoslavia's primary energy resources consist mainly of low grade coal,
lignite deposits, some hydrocarbons, and substantial hydropower potential.
The largest proportion of these resources are located in Bosnia-Herzegovina,
Croatia, Kosovo and Serbia. The country's reserves of hydrocarbons are not
significant when compared to hydro or coal and lignite. Exploitable reserves
of uranium exist and development of some oil-bearing shales may be possible
in the future. There are indications of geothermal energy, but no estimate
of potential is available.
2.05      Yugoslavia's coal reserves (approximately 22 billion tons) are
equivalent to several hundred years' supply at present consumption rates
(35-40 Mt/a). Coal production increased during the past few years because of
increased oil prices. Most of the coal, however, is of infe*ior quality and
export is uneconomic; its value is primarily for electricity generation.
Yugoslavia's hydropower potential is estimated as 65 TWh/a of which some 23
TWh/a has been developed (4,800 MW). A further 7 TWh/a (2,800 MW) is under
construction or planned. Of the remaining 35 TWh/a hydropower potential,
10-15 TWh/a are unsuitable for future development because the sites are
either located near densely populated valleys or in smaller catchment areas.
2.06      Based on 1974 figures, Yugoslavia's oil reserves of about 50 Mt
represent 15 years of production (3.5 Mt) compared with total country needs
of 10 Mt (imports accounting for the balance). Similarly the proven reserves
of natural gas of 47 billion m3 (Gm3) are sufficient to supply 30 years of
production (1.4 Gm3; no imports). Estimates of assured uranium reserves
(9,000 - 20,000 t) are adequate to fuel 1,500-3,300 MW of generating capacity
for 30 years. Currently, the first uranium mine is being developed to provide
fuel for the nuclear station (630 MW) under construction at Krsko in Slovenia.
2.07      Past Trends.  Since 1966, the demand for primary energy has been
growing at an average annual rate of 8%. Hydrocarbons showed the highest
growth rates (oil 16%/a and gas 18%/a). Hydropower production has also
increased but financing constraints have impeded construction. Yugoslavia's
oil imports increased from 10% of total energy requirements in 1966 to 32% in
1974. The growth of energy usage has been significantly above the growth rate
1/   See Annex 1, Section A.



- 4 -
of GNP (6.6%/a) with an energy coefficient (elasticity of energy growth to the
GNP growth) of about 1.2 for the same period.
2.08      Future Growth.  The 1976-1980 economic plan forecasts primary energy
requirements to grow at about 9%/a. This is optimistic because it implies a
higher energy coefficient (1.3) for the forecast period than the coefficient
of the past ten years. Coal production is projected to rise to some 58 Mt
in 1980, of which at least 21 Mt is in lignite required for power generation.
Demand for oil is expected similarly to rise to 18.5 Mt and for gas to 6
Gm3.
2.09      Policies and Problems.  Yugoslavia's energy plan aims at self-
sufficiency through intensive development of domestic resources. However,
an overall framework for ensuring proper coordination within and between the
energy subsectors has not been developed. Coordination is hampered by the
existing system of decentralized planning. At the same time, the federal
government's control of energy prices, coupled with the lack of overall policy
guidelines, has resulted in financial difficulties in the sector and economic
distortions (e.g. lack of funds for expansion and imbalanced allocation of
financial resources among subsectors). Additional sources of funds will be
required to finance the proposed energy development program. These could be
derived from surcharges on energy prices (similar to those in the power sub-
sector, see 2.22) to be allocated to energy projects according to their eco-
nomic merits. The creation of an "Energy Investment Bank" may be desirable
in order to administer these funds under the control of Federal and regional
authorities.
The Power Subsector
Historical Review
2.10      Organizational Changes:  The 1965 economic reform (1.04) accelerated
the move toward decentralization in the economy as a whole. In the power sub-
sector, this fragmented the subsector into autonomous organizations causing
a retardation in the construction of new generating facilities and a deteriora-
tion in the reliability of the system. The sector has been in the process of
reorganization since 1970, with the object of improving coordination. Legal
changes permitting this reorganization were formalized in the 1974 constitu-
tion. As a result, construction of electric plant accelerated again. A crash
program was initiated in 1972/74 which will result by 1978/79 in increasing
the installed generating capacity by 4.0-4.5 GW. The added capacity will
represent almost half of the 1975 total installed capacity.
2.11      Production and Consumption. 1/  The average annual consumption of
electricity in Yugoslavia increased by 12.5% between 1955 and 1975; however,
at the republican level, the growth rate varied widely. For instance,
Slovenia's consumption increased at an average annual rate of 7.8% as compared
1/   See Annex 1, Attachment 3.



to Montenegro's 17.6%. On the production side, Serbia's share of the total
power output increased from 20% to 44%, while Slovenia's share decreased from
35% to 17%.   Meanwhile, although national per capita consumption quadrupled
between 1960 and 1975 (from 405 kWh to 1,652 kWh), the disparities are still
considerable e.g. in 1975, from 900 kWh in Kosovo to 3,340 kWh in Slovenia.
This primarily reflects the differences in the stages of the republics'
economic development. The proportion of the population having access to
electricity has also increased substantially (from 55% in 1960 to 94% in
1975). Changes in the market shares of the customer classes were also sub-
stantial; the share of industry dropped from 72% in 1955 to 59% in 1975,
while the share of households increased from 17% in 1955 to 28% in 1975.
2.12      Power Exchange:  Power exchanges with neighboring countries (12
connections of which one is at the 380-kV level) have been small due to the
limited transport capacities and dispatch constraints. In 1975, power im-
ports met about 3% of Yugoslavia's energy requirement. These limitations
contributed to energy deficits which, since 1973, have been in the order of
0.7-1.1 TWh/a (some 2-3.5% of overall power requirements).
Current Aspects of the Power Subsector
2.13      Present Organization.  The organization of the power industry fol-
lows the Yugoslav principle of workers' self-management (2.01, 2.02) 1/. A
single power plant is typically operated by a BOAL. BOALs within a larger
geographic area may form a WOAL. In its general form, however, a WOAL may
include BOALs concerned with generating plants as well as transmission,
research and development and other supporting services. Distribution entities
are organized separately but along similar lines. The generation and transmis-
sion organizations in each republic have organized themselves into COALs. In
the case of Croatia, the COAL includes the distribution organizations. The
subsector presently consists of about 210 basic organizations, some of which
represent coal mining organizations.
2.14      The 32 generation and transmission organizations (WOALs and COALs)
are required by law to be members of JUGEL 2/. In addition, a combination of
9 power institutes and energy consulting firms are voluntary members of JUGEL.
While JUGEL coordinates the major aspects of power generation and transmission
in the country, it has no executive power over its member organizations. In
a similar manner, all 20 distribution organizations have their own associa-
tion, but 16 of these organizations have voluntarily joined JUGEL. The power
organizations and their customers comprise the power sector's "Communities of
Interest" (2.03). Some of these communities are still in the formative stage.
The communities of interest coordinate sectoral activities with the distribu-
tion organizations at the local level and with the generation and transmission
1/   See Annex 1, para 4, etc. for discussion of organization.
2/   See Annex 1, Attachment 2 for JUGEL's Membership Chart.



-6-
COALS at the level of the republic. They are responsible for determining
long-term development programs and their financing. In addition, they decide
on both the consumers' financial contributions to system expansion and the
level and structure of the tariffs. The communities are governed by an
assembly in which the power organizations and the customers have equal rep-
resentation. If the communities fail in carrying out their responsibilities,
matters are turned over to the republican assembly for final resolution.
2.15      Generation and Transmission Facilities.  The existing system was
essentially created after 1945. In 1939 total installed capacity in the
country was only 500 MW and production 1.2 TWh. By 1975 installed capacity
and production were approximately equal to 9 GW and 40 TWh respectively 1/.
The proportions of electricity generated hydraulically and thermally have
been almost equal since 1955. In the future, thermal generation is expected
to predominate. Wide disparities in the total capacity added since 1955 exist
between republics; 43% was installed in Serbia, 20% in Croatia, 17% in Bosnia-
Herzegovina, 12% in Slovenia, 5% in Macedonia and 3% in Montenegro. The
disparities reflect, in part, the different energy resource endowments of
these republics.
2.16      Data compiled by JUGEL show that the overall heat rate of generating
plant in the system has improved from 4,120 kcal/kWh in 1960 to 2,720 kcal/kWh
(10,800 Btu/kWh) in 1975 2/ which results in an average annual decrease of
2.7%. Yugoslavia's plant efficiency is sensitive to the assumptions made
about the average heat rate of lignite because it accounted for approximately
90% of the total heat used for generation in 1975. However, even with a more
conservative assumption about the heat rate of lignite, the data demonstrate
the effect of scale economies resulting from the installation of larger units.
Also, manpower data indicate that, in generation, workers input has been at an
acceptable level of about 2.06 man/MW for thermal plants and 0.90 man/MW for
hydro facilities. The capacity factor has increased from 48% in 1965 to 51%
in 1975 and the load factor from 61% to 65% 3/.  By the end of 1975, installed
capacity in JUGEL's system was 8,381 MW, of which 4,676 MW hydro and 3,705
thermal 4/ producing 37.4 TWh (18.8 TWh and 18.6 TWh respectively).
2.17      The transmission system has expanded in parallel with generating
capacity, from about 2,900 km (110-kV only) in 1955 to about 15,772 km (110-
kv, 210-kV, 380-kV) in 1975 1/. This created a national grid, yet the net-
work's reliability, stability and voltage conditions are not satisfactory
1/   See Annex 1, Attachment 4.
2/   See Annex 1, Attachment 5.
3/   See Annex 1, Attachment 6.
4/   See Annex 1, Attachment 7.



-7-
because several essential lines are rapidly reaching their rated capacity.
In addition, the republics' dispatch facilities are inadequate for the opera-
tion of the networks; this is particularly true under emergency conditions.
2.18      Under a dispatch agreement signed in 1971, JUGEL's function is
basically one of coordination. It monitors agreements between republics and
records departures from them; it also determines the magnitude of operational
penalties and bonuses. Yet, despite its functions, JUGEL has no executive
powers. Matters pertaining to inter-republic exchanges of energy, such as
schedules, requests for assistance and changes in switching conditions, are
provided by agreements among JUGEL's members. JUGEL's role under this agree-
ment, however, is inconsistent with the foreseen optimum use of the 380-kV
network to be completed in which generating plant would operate in accordance
with its incremental cost ranking. Its dispatching role will be enhanced with
the amendments planned for the agreement, which have the objective of ensuring
optimum operation of the national generation and transmission facilities
(4.10).
Tariffs
2.19      Tariff Structure.  The present bulk supply tariff structure, 1/
which applies throughout the country, was introduced in 1965. Periodically,
new maximum permitted levels, reached through agreements between the republics,
are promulgated by the Federal Government. Any republic may institute reduced
rates through its communities of interest but in practice the maximum rates
are usually sought. The tariff structure is cost-based in philosophy. The
rates are differentiated with respect to voltage levels, season and time of
use and allow two options (step 1 and 2) for selection by the consumer. The
differences between them is that the second step has a lower kW rate than the
first, but a higher kWh rate; they were introduced to allow for differences
in the pattern of customer consumption. Recently, a consensus was reached
between the power producers to remove the second step at the bulk level. The
change has already been made in Bosnia-Herzegovina and Croatia. The structure
of the distribution tariffs 2/ is similar to that of the bulk tariffs with the
exception that the second step still exists throughout the country.
2.20      Tariff Levels.  There were no changes in the level of the bulk sup-
ply rates between 1965 and 1970. Since 1970, bulk rates have been increased
annually. The most recent increase in bulk (and retail) tariffs was introduced
between March and August of 1976. At the moment their level is approximately
180% above their corresponding 1970 level. This compares with an increase in
the cost-of-living index of about 160% for the same period. Because the dis-
tribution organizations are autonomous and have only recently joined JUGEL,
details on the tariffs at the retail level were not available to the mission.
They are thought to have followed the changes in bulk tariffs.
1/   See Annex 1, Attachment 8.
2/   See Annex 1, Attachment 9.



-8-
2.21      Tariffs and Marginal-Cost Pricing.  The tariffs do not reflect
the pattern of consumer demand and current costs of supply (6.17), and the
financial requirements of the power subsector. At the Bank s request, JUGEL
contracted (in November 197C) ` lastituta Za Elektroprivreda of Zagreb for
the preparation of a tariff study to be completed within a year. The terms
of reference for the study have been amended - as requested by the Bank - so
that marginal cost pricing will also be studied to provide guidance on the
cross-subsidization and divergences, if any, from the economic cost of
supply. 1/
2.22      Consumers' Contributions Towards Investments.  The republics have
progressively introduced various measures for collecting supplementary funds
for their investment in system expansion 2/. These measures are in the form
of either repayable loans, e.g. contributions from industry, BOALs and WOALs,
or non-repayable levies e.g. connection charges, metering charges and special
surcharges (as high as 60% for domestic consumers in Slovenia). The contribu-
tions are not intended as permanent features but as a temporary expedient to
provide funds during the present investment-intensive period.
Bank's Experience in Lending for Power
2.23      The general role of the Bank in the subsector have been outlined
above (1.04). In particular, difficulties associated with the execution of
the first power transmission project can be summarized under the following
three major headings:
(a) Cost Overrun. The final cost of the project is expected to
be approximately US$425 million as compared to the original
estimate of US$225 million due to increases in equipment
prices and labor costs. The overruns are being financed
through loans secured from the Federal Republic of Germany,
some local loans and the borrowers' internal funds.
(b) Overall Coordination. The coordination objectives have not
been fully attained, e.g. major interrelated project compo-
nents are not expected to be simultaneously completed. In
addition, reporting is lagging and the management consultants
were engaged approximately 3 years behind schedule.
(c) JUGEL's Role. Despite agreement on comprehensive coordination
between JUGEL and the Republics, the Bank's expectations have
not been met satisfactorily in this regard due to JUGEL's lack
of authority to act on behalf of the borrowers and its lack of
expert staff required for adequate performance of its coor-
dinating duties (the authorization of the coordinating committee
is required for engagement of such staff).
1/   See Annex 3, Attachment 4.
2/   See Annex 1, Attachment 10.



- 9 -
JUGEL has recently engaged additional expert staff for the Project Unit and
agreements have been reached on other actions to be taken for enhancing
JUGEL's role as coordinator and supervisor for the first and second trans-
mission project and for improving coordination (3.20-3.23; 4.06-4.11).
Future Development 1/
2.24      Each republic prepares its own development plans which are con-
solidated by JUGEL to form the national development program. The objective
of each republican plan is to maintain an annual balance of capacities and
energies within its control, including that from plants that are shared
(through joint financing) and might be located in another republic. JUGEL
has no authority to determine independently that the collection of projects
identified by the republics constitutes an optimum development plan at the
national level. At the suggestion of the Bank, JUGEL was authorized by its
members (November 1976) to contract consultants (Energoprojekt and Nicola
Tesla of Belgrade and Milzaz Vidmarja of Ljubljana) for a least-cost develop-
ment (LCD) study to be completed within 18 months. The study is to be con-
ducted in a manner consistent with terms of reference provided by the Bank 2/.
Because these studies are financed locally, Bank financing (from the proposed
loan) was not requested. However, assurances have been obtained by the Bank
for satisfactory execution of this study (3.19).
2.25      Forecasts.  Forecasts of electric energy requirements are made by
each republic through models of varying degrees of sophistication. JUGEL
consolidates the forecasts into a national forecast. The appraisal mission
reduced the load forecast in order to put maximum energy requirements and
plant capabilities in approximate balance. Power import and/or export was
used to provide for the margin of uncertainty. 3/ Sales in the interconnected
system (excluding imports or exports) are assumed to grow at 12% in 1976,
11.5% in 1977, and 12% in 1978, with a gradual decline to 8% by 1982. The
average growth rate would be 10%, equal to the average 1971-75 growth rate,
and 1982 sales (70.3 TWh) would be double those in 1975. Maximum demand is
expected to grow at an average rate of 11%/a between 1976 and 1982. The
forecast appears reasonable because the economic recession of the last 2-3
years which reduced the growth rate (11.2% in 1972 to 5.7% in 1975) appears
to be tapering off.
2.26      The increase in the proportion of thermal plant generation with the
related station requirements is the main reason behind the estimated increase
in bulk system losses, i.e. from 7% in 1975 to 10% by 1982. The forecast
capacity and energy balances 4/ show that energy requirements could be met
throughout the period provided that water availability is about average, but
1/   For details see Annex 2.
2/   See Annex 3, Attachment 3.
3/   See Annex 2, Attachment 1.
4/   See Annex 2, Attachments 2 and 3.



- 10 -
the plant margin would not exceed 28%. This margin is rather low in a hydro-
thermal mix such as that of Yugoslavia where low winter water flow may reduce
hydro capacity by some 20-40% because most plants are run of the river type.
Therefore, the main problem is expected to be the system's ability to meet
demand rather than energy requirements.

2.29      Investment Program Cost 1/.  The cost of the 1976-1982 development
program for generation and transmission (including Croatia's distribution
network and including Kosovo's and Vojvodina's generating facilities) would
be about 220 billion dinars (US$12.2 billion). The average annual expenditure
throughout the period would be approximately 31 billion dinars (US$1.7 billion).
The program appears ambitious and, although investments in power have been
stepped-up considerably (Din 11 billion in 1975 and probably about double this
amount in 1976), financial constraints may cause some delays. The extent, and
effects, of these constraints (6.12) cannot be estimated with any degree of
certainty.
3. THE PROJECT
3.01      General Objectives.  The objectives of the Project are continuations
of those that were set for the first Power Transmission Project, i.e.:
(a) to strengthen sector coordination, particularly through
follow-up of the ongoing studies for the establishment of
procedures for the efficient operation and management of
the 380-kV transmission system and the generating facilities;
(b) to encourage better economic and financial planning and the
use of improved forecasting and budgeting procedures by the
power entities;
(c) to assist Yugoslavia in obtaining most of the needed foreign
finance for the Project from other sources; and
(d) to strengthen the role of JUGEL in the sector as coordinator,
dispatch agency, and as channel for a continuing dialogue
with the Bank.
3.02      Historically, the power entities in each republic balanced the
exchange of power with the other republics (2.27), to ensure their annual
supply of electricity. The new 380-kV system coupled with adequate dispatch
facilities will allow far greater flexibility in transferring power through-
out the country. Power exchange is desirable in economic terms because of
the benefits stemming from the scale of operation, reduced reserve margins
and fuel saving resulting from the economic loading of plants.
3.03      Specific Objectives.  The second phase (1977-1982) of the 380-kV
system is an extension of the first phase. It will provide the following
services:
(a) facilitate the process of meeting the demands in areas where
the existing 220-kV and 110-kV systems are rapidly approaching
their capacity constraint;
1/   See Annex 2, Attachment 6.



- 12 -
(b) improve the overall system reliability; and
(c) make possible the access of a number of larger cities and
rapidly expanding industrial areas to the 380-kV network.
3.04      Description.  The Project, covering this second phase, would com-
prise 1/ about 1,000 km of single circuit 380-kV transmission lines, 16 new
substations and the extension of 8 existing substations with a total trans-
former capacity of about 10,000 MVA, 3 switching stations, and 9 dispatch
centers (1 federal, 6 republican, and 1 for each of the two autonomous
provinces of Vojvodina and Kosovo). The proposed loan would also finance
consulting services for the dispatch facilities and management, tariffs,
and system development (3.20, 3.21).
3.05      Cost Estimate.  The estimated cost of the Project 2/ is 12,250 mil-
lion dinars (US$681 million), of which the main items are:

(b) prevailing ex-factory prices of similar equipment manu-
factured in Yugoslavia; and
(c) consultant's cost estimates for the dispatch equipment.
The estimate of the foreign exchange component was compiled from contract
information provided by the manufacturers of materials, equipment and services
used in Phase I. The foreign cost component would be about 40% of the Proj-
ect's total cost.
3.07      The physical contingency of 5% reflects the execution experience of
Phase I. The price contingency for local costs assumes an annual increase of:
13% for 1976, 12% for 1977 through 1979 and 10% for 1980 through 1982; and for
foreign costs 9% for 1976, 8% for 1977 through 1979 and 7% for 1980 through
1982, which are in line with expected increases in Yugoslavia. The final
(1981) estimated cost of the Project (MUS$681) compared with the (1975) base
cost (MUS$456) represents--over the 6 year period--a compounded annual increase
of about 7% over the base cost.
3.08      The final unit prices are expected to be US$35/kVA for the substa-
tions and US$190,000/km of line, both of which are reasonable. Compared with
the first project (1972), 1976 costs are approximately 2.5-3 times those of
1972. Final (1982) unit costs are expected to be 4 to 4.5 times those of 1972.
3.09      Procurement.  The proceeds of the proposed loan will be applied to
the cost of transformers, circuit breakers, isolator switches, metering trans-
formers, lightning arresters, shunt reactors and metering and protection
equipment, purchased in accordance with international competitive bidding con-
sistent with Bank Group Guidelines for Procurement. The number of items to
be submitted for tendering will be decided at the time of issue of the bid
documents (September 1977) taking into account the current estimate of prices,
the portion of the loan allocated to each republic and the number and type of
items of equipment in each republic. In view of the uncertainties in prices
and their increases in future years it is estimated that the Bank loan would
finance some 50-60% of the numbers required (i.e. an average of about 55% of
the 7 items of equipment listed above would be submitted for ICB under the
Guidelines). In assessment of bids, Yugoslav manufacturers would receive
a preference of 15% or applicable duties, whichever is the lesser. The
probability appears high that they will be successful in being awarded the
contracts for transformers, circuit breakers and isolators and part of the
metering and protection equipment because the Yugoslav manufacturing industry
has considerably increased its production capacities for these types of
equipment during the execution of Phase 1 of the 380-kV system.
3.10      Specific items are expected to be financed by an EIB loan (see 3.22)
in a similar fashion as the Bank financed portion under EIB guidelines for
procurement. A portion of the remaining equipment, unsuitable for interna-
tional bidding (due to standardization requirements, limited number required,
extension of an existing or new contract, proprietary nature of the equipment)
would be procured locally. For most other equipment, suppliers' and similar



- 14 -
credits will be sought. Bid documents would be issued before the end of 1977
except for the dispatch facilities which are expected to be submitted for
tendering before the end of 1978 (see 3.20). In view of the large sums of
financing that are required--in addition to the Bank loan--for the completion
of the Project, JUGEL, together with a committee of the Federal Secretariat
for Finance, solicited offers for financing from most major international
suppliers of transmission equipment. It has received letters of intent from
about 100 foreign (United States, Canada, Australia, Japan and Western and
Eastern Europe) and Yugoslav firms. Procurement financed by such credits
would be done under the same general principles of international competitive
bidding, among the countries and firms offering credits, as those followed for
the Bank items, except that credit terms would be taken into consideration
during bid evaluation. In order to assure timely completion of financing
arrangements, satisfactory information should reach the Bank at appropriate
times (3.22).
3.11      Disbursements.  The proposed loan would be disbursed 1/ against the
CIF costs of imported items, the ex-factory cost of items supplied by Yugoslav
manufacturers, and the costs of consulting services. It will finance 100% of
each eligible contract.
3.12      The transmission lines and substations included in the Project are
expected to be completed by the end of 1981 but the dispatch facilities prob-
ably not before the end of 1983. Because the proposed loan would not finance
part of the latter (except studies related to dispatch facilities, see 3.20)
the Bank has agreed to December 31, 1982, as the closing date to allow for
payment of retention moneys due after the guarantee period.
3.13      Amount and Allocation of the Proposed Loan.  A Bank loan of US$80
million which will finance 10.3% of the total cost of the Project or 29.2% of
its foreign cost, is proposed for a term of 15 years. The loan would have a
grace period of 3 years and an interest rate of 8.2% has been assumed. The
general principle of prorating the loan amongst the Borrowers to the cost of
the facilities in each republic, as adopted for the first project, was also
applied for the proposed loan 2/, noting that the facilities for Serbia also
includes those required for Vojvodina (none would be financed by the Bank
loan in Kosovo). The allocation of the loan between the republics would be
as follows:

3.14      Borrowers.  The composite organizations (COALs) in Bosnia-Herzegovina,
Croatia and Slovenia would be the borrowers for these republics. In Serbia
the borrower would be Elektroistok which is the work organization that con-
structs and operates all transmission facilities in both the republic, Kosovo
and Vojvodina. Special arrangement is required for Serbia because, while
Elektroistok is a member of the republic's composite organization (ZEP), the
power organizations in Kosovo or Vojvodina are not members of ZEP. Elektro-
prenos Stopje, the WOAL responsible for tranmission, would be the borrower
in Macedonia; Elektroprenos Titograd the WOAL for transmission, would be the
borrower in Montenegro. The composite organizations in these 2 republics
cannot be designated borrowers because the respective self-management agree-
ments do not provide for borrowing by the COALs (the number of organizations
associated within each COAL is small and the need for centralized borrowing
is not yet apparent). As under Loan 836-YU, JUGEL would be responsible for
all actions to be taken for withdrawal of loan proceeds, payment of interest
and repayment of the loan.
3.15      Engineering.  The plans for lines and substations were prepared
by the republican enterprises for the areas under their jurisdiction.  JUGEL
was assisted by the various enterprises and local consultants (Energoprojekt
and other technical institutes) in integrating the overall design. The local
consultants will continue to provide special services of singular nature
(factory and site tests, supervisory staff for particular erections) to JUGEL;
this arrangement worked well for Phase I and is expected to work well for
Phase II.
3.16      Coordination and Implementation.  The various transmission enter-
prises, composite organizations and JUGEL (the first for the republican dis-
patch centers in the case of Bosnia-Herzegovina, Serbia and Slovenia and the
latter for the federal dispatch center; see also 3.20) would be responsible
for the construction of the facilities. As for the first project, JUGEL and
the participating organizations would execute the proposed Project under coor-
dination guidelines which, in view of the performance difficulties under the
first project, have been revised and discussed during negotiations. 1/ JUGEL
would be again designated as coordinator of the Project responsible for coor-
dinating committee work, engineering, procurement, construction, studies and
1/   See Annex 3, Attachment 9.



- 16 -
relations with the Bank. A coordinating committee chaired by JUGEL would be
responsible for reaching agreement on all aspects of the Project execution.
JUGEL would--under the responsibility of the committee and subject to final
approval by the various organizations through the committee--prepare all bid
documents, issue bid documents, receive, review and evaluate bids, make pro-
posals for the award of contracts, and prepare final contracts between the
successful bidders and the relevant organizations. Bid documents, evaluation
of bids and proposals for award of contracts to be financed by the proposed
loan would be subject to Bank review. JUGEL would administer all contracts.
Construction and local supervision would be the responsibilty of each of the
executing agencies in accordance with common standards (including scheduling)
set by JUGEL and the committee, for which JUGEL would execute overall supervi-
sion. There would be a permanent operating staff, i.e. "Project Unit" (4.07),
within JUGEL for all day-to-day business with the various organizations and
the Bank. The guidelines covering these arrangements, acceptable to the Bank,
would be signed by the participating organizations as a condition of effective-
ness of the proposed loan.
3.17      Because under the first loan the objective of completing related
facilities at approximately the same date has not been met, various measures
have been agreed upon in order to meet this objective for the second phase:
(a) bid documents will indicate appropriate (substantial)
penalties--this is particularly needed for civil works and
erection work for the lines--to be included in contracts,
for not meeting the construction schedule;
(b) a construction schedule for all facilities has been
defined 1/; progress would be supervised by JUGEL using
the critical path method and the necessary actions by the
executing agencies would be initiated through local project
coordinators; and
(c) actions will be implemented for full right-of-way acquisi-
tion within 18 months after loan signing.
3.18      Dispatch System Design and Bid Documents.  A commission in charge
of dispatch facilities, composed of JUGEL's technical staff and experts
of the republican organizations, sets the overall design principles. The
generation and transmission enterprises in each republic prepared plans for
their dispatch facilities (some were partially assisted by Electricite de
France and Societe General pour l'Industrie of Switzerland). The final
designs are reviewed by the commission. However, the progress towards com-
pletion of the design varies widely between republics. The management consul-
tants (Systems Europe of Brussels) engaged under the first loan are studying
1/   See Annex 3, Attachment 1.



- 17 -
the procedures and facilities for system operation and energy management, of
which the dispatch system is a part. It is expected that their findings and
recommendations will be available before the end of 1977. The consultant's
final results will be integrated in the final bid documents for the dispat-
ching facilities.
3.19      Combination of bid requests for all dispatch facilities is highly
desirable because of the following advantages:
(a) the facilities' attractiveness under suppliers' credit
financing will be enhanced because of their commercially
attractive size, where in total they are expected to
cost US$57 million (1976 prices) with a foreign compo-
nent of some US$52 million, and
(b) standardization of the equipment and software used
throughout Yugoslavia.
In order to capture these advantages, JUGEL has agreed to engage consultants
by December 31, 1977, under conditions acceptable to the Bank, for the prepara-
tion of comprehensive bid documents for all the dispatch facilities. The docu-
ments would be guided by the recommendations of the management consultants
and be jointly prepared by the republican experts and the dispatch committee.
The bid documents would be completed within 1 year after the acquisition of
the consulting services. The task will require about 125 manweeks of con-
sulting services at a cost of about US$280,000 (including contingencies).
3.20      Studies.  The Bank has maintained a continuous dialogue with JUGEL
on the management study financed by Loan 836-YU, a least-cost development
study and a tariff study. These dialogues are expected to continue because
of their importance in improving coordination, management, planning and
operation of the Yugoslav system, and JUGEL has undertaken the following:
(a) The Bank will receive for its comments the final report per-
taining to the tariff study not later than June 30, 1978,
and the least cost development study not later than December
31, 1978; JUGEL will exchange views with the Bank within
3 months after these dates on the recommendations resulting
from these studies and on the scope of further work to be
carried out in connection with these studies. Because fur-
ther work has also to be executed for additional management
studies covering each republic (i.e. follow-up studies to
the basic management study financed by loan 836-YU; see 5.07)
the loan would finance US$720,000 for about 300 manweeks of
consulting services for the additional studies in the areas
of management, tariff and least cost development.
(b) The Bank would be given the opportunity to review regularly
the progress of the least-cost development and tariff studies
for discussion with JUGEL, receive all interim and final
reports, and would be informed by JUGEL of the actions that
would be taken as a result of these studies.

3.22      The entire local cost and some of the foreign cost (particularly in-
direct foreign cost) will be financed by internally generated funds ($251 mil-
lion) and local loans (US$191 million).  The European Investment Bank has
appraised the 380-kV development program and pending the outcome of approval
of the relevant loan a preliminary figure of 25 million units of account
(about US$30 million) has been assumed. Specific arrangements have not yet
been made for financing the remaining foreign costs estimated at some US$129
million. However, JUGEL, together with a committee of the Federal Secretary
for Finance, has requested proposals for co-financing of the Project and it
appears from the number (more than 100) of preliminary proposals received,
that commercial loans amounting to some US$59 million and suppliers credits
amounting to US$70 million can be obtained. It has been agreed that, except
for the financing of the dispatch facilities for which no contracts would
be signed before the end of 1978, the borrowers will provide to the Bank by
June 30, 1978, information that arrangements satisfactory to the Bank have
been made for making available to the executing organizations any remaining
external financing required for completing the individual parts of the Project.
The Bank would have the option of withholding further withdrawals from the
loan account in the event that the above undertaking is not met. Financing
arrangements for the dispatch facilities would be completed before June 30,
1979. Interest during construction on the Bank loan of about $17.5 million
is expected to be financed from the federally allocated foreign exchange
resources of the Borrower's basic organizations. Although all appropriate
commitments have been made for financing the local costs, the finalization
and signing of the local financing arrangements as well as the financing
guarantees to cover any cost overruns which have been given by local banks,
would be a condition of effectiveness of the proposed loan. It should be
noted that Yugoslavia's own investment regulations, which were agreed to
by all the republics and provinces and promulgated in 1975 as a part of
Yugoslavia's economic stabilization policy to control inflation, require
that all the financing for any large-scale investment be secured before
the executing enterprise is permitted to undertake the investment.
3.23      Environmental Impact.  As under the previous loan, much of the
right-of-way is in rough country and very little land will be taken out of
agricultural production. Agreements have to be reached with all persons and



- 19 -
institutions affected, under the various republican laws for expropriations
which aim at a fair indemnification so as to provide former owners with not
less than the same economic situation they had prior to the infringement on
their property. As in most European countries, opposition to transmission
lines is growing, although in Yugoslavia the situation is not critical and
extreme care is taken in constructing lines so as to cause minimum adverse
scenic impact.
4. THE PROJECT ENTITIES
4.01      Borrowers and Executing Agencies.  The 6 borrowers would be the com-
posite organizations for generation and transmission in Bosnia-Herzegovina,
Croatia and Slovenia and the organizations responsible for transmission (con-
struction and operation) in Macedonia, Montenegro and Serbia (serving also
Kosovo and Vojvodina) (3.14). There would be 18 executing agencies, 2 each
in Macedonia, Montenegro and Serbia, 3 in Bosnia-Herzegovina, 4 in Croatia,
and JUGEL (Federal dispatch); see Annex 4 for the names of the borrowers and
executing agencies. A single loan agreement, similar to the one for Loan
836-YU, would be signed with the 6 borrowers, 3 composite organizations in
Macedonia, Montenegro and Serbia, and JUGEL as Coordinator. A single project
agreement would be signed with the remaining 8 executing agencies.
4.02      Organizations.  At the Republican level, the basic management struc-
ture is common to all power organizations 1/. It comprises on the operating
technical side a director (or manager) and working units (or departments) for
steam stations, hydro stations, transmission, research and planning, and con-
struction. The budgeting/statistics and accounting/internal control units
are under a director of finance. A single administrative department under
its own director handles matters related to personnel, public relations,
archives and auxiliary staff. Depending on the size and composition of the
BOAL, WOAL or COAL, several departments may not be required, or may be com-
bined. Organizational differences between social entities may be quite sub-
stantial (less so for a COAL than for a BOAL). Each organization (whether
BOAL, WOAL or COAL) has its own workers' council, elected by its members
in accordance with the self-management agreements. The Council is the top
policy-making body in much the same fashion as the board of directors of
a corporation in non-socialist countries. It appoints the General Manager,
who is the chief executive. In general, his executive power is less and
his accountability (vis-a-vis the workers' council) greater than is usual
in non-socialist countries.
4.03      The republican organizations are at times extremely complicated 2/
because the types of constituent BOALs may differ widely (e.g. they may in-
clude organizations of cafeteria operators, mine workers, etc.). Their number
can also vary widely, e.g. 77 in Slovenia. The table below summarizes the
number of borrowers, executing agencies, organizations and workers in each
republic that will be involved with the proposed loan:
1/   See Annex 4, Attachment 1.
2/   Annex 1, Attachment 2, makes an effort to present the organizations
in common terms.

4.04      The 18 organizations executing the Project are capable of construct-
ing and operating their facilities because of the accumulated experience gained
in the process of expanding their respective systems.
4.05      Future changes in structure are expected due to the merger of organi-
zations. It appears that, in both Croatia and Bosnia-Herzegovina, the organi-
zations responsible for transmission (3 in Croatia and 2 in Bosnia-Herzegovina)
and in charge of executing the Project may merge to form a single work organi-
zation. Organizational merger does not automatically amend the commitments
of the member organizations originally involved in a Bank project and prior
approval of the Bank is required for such changes.
4.06      JUGEL's Organization.  JUGEL, which has a total staff of 154, has
five divisions of which three are under a director (Engineering and Power,
Economic and Finance, and Legal and Administration) and two are directly
under the General Manager (Services, Dispatch). 1/ This organization is
effective for normal daily business such as statistics, coordinating orga-
nizational activities at the inter-republican and federal level. Yet it
is weak in most other respects because it lacks the necessary substantive
authority. However, transfer of substantive authority at this stage, even
if it could be agreed, would be premature because, under Loan 836-YU, con-
sultants have only recently started their work on recommendations for
reorganization of JUGEL's structure and of its functions as the union of
practically all power organizations in Yugoslavia.
4.07      In order to gradually strengthen JUGEL's rule the following actions
have been taken and understandings reached:
(a) JUGEL has recently appointed an economist, a financial
analyst and a lawyer, awaiting further recommendations
for staffing expected to result from the ongoing manage-
ment study.
1/   See Annex 4, Attachment 3.



- 21 -
(b) JUGEL will, before December 31, 1977, review with the Bank
the consultants recommendations for its organization and
staffing as a result of the ongoing management study and
agreed changes will be implemented within 6 months.
(c) JUGEL will create a Project unit, incorporating the present
department working for the coordinating committee, organized
and staffed in a manner satisfactory to the Bank, under the
the chairman of the coordinating committee (JUGEL's General
Manager). The unit will be fully responsible for all matters
related to Phases I and II of the 380-kV transmission network
including supervision. In addition, it is to represent the
borrowers in all matters that pertain to any dealings with
the Bank (4.10). Recommendations for the organization and
staffing of such a unit will be made by the management con-
sultants, and full implementation of the Project Unit would
be a condition of effectiveness of the proposed loan.
4.08      Operations.  Because the present dispatch agreement (signed in 1971)
between JUGEL and the generation and transmission organizations is outdated
(2.18) the organizations have undertaken to amend the agreement in form and
substance satisfactory to the Bank before June 30, 1978. The new agreement
would take into account the recommendations by the management consultants
concerning JUGEL's dispatch functions and its organization and staffing for
the purpose. The amended agreement would inter alia accommodate the fol-
lowing:
(a) JUGEL would coordinate all aspects of dispatching within
the interconnected system, at 110-kV and above, that
would materially affect the reliability of supply and
connections with neighboring countries.
(b) JUGEL would monitor the operations of all facilities
within the 220-kV and 380-kV system, in particular with
respect to digressions from normal operations in which
case it initiates corrective actions.
(c) JUGEL would have executive control over the 380-kV system
and the 220-kV tie lines interconnecting republics and
connecting neighboring countries in order to maintain the
integrity of the system.
(d) JUGEL would have total emergency control in situations
where cascading outages could endanger the system.
(e) The generating and transmission organizations intend to
create an inter-republican power sale and purchase pool
to be administered by JUGEL.



- 22 -
4.09      JUGEL has undertaken to provide the Bank, as a part of each year's
last quarterly progress report, a set of updated tables including the following
data:
(a) an updated five year forecast of operation schedules and
cost data for the existing plants and the entire system;
(b) an updated five year forecast of balances of energy and
capacities; and
(c) an updated five year investment plan for the power
system development program.
4.10      Furthermore, JUGEL will provide the Bank, as part of each year's
second quarterly progress report, monitoring information 1/ covering all
aspects of operations.  Because, except for the "standard"' kW and kWh data
(e.g. installed capacities, demand, generation, sales, etc.), no statis-
tically consolidated figures for operational data were prepared in the past
(operating expenses, employment cost, receivables, etc.), the Bank intends
to discuss with JUGEL--as soon as possible after the accounts have been fully
consolidated (5.07)--a reduction of the information to be provided to the Bank
with the objective of limiting the monitoring data to a set significant for
measuring performance.
5. FINANCE
Highlights of Financial Position
5.01      The highlights of the financial position of the six composite orga-
nizations of the republics are as follows:
(a) past financial performance is considered to be satisfactory
even though 4 borrowers (out of 12) did not achieve the average
35% cash contribution to expansion required under Loan 836-YU
during 1971-1975 (5.04, 5.05);
(b) the composite organizations need to improve their financial
forecasting techniques and would focus greater attention on
this area through submission of forecast operating statements
for each year together with a financing plan for the year to
the Bank by the end of January of that year (5.08, 5.16);
(c) out of about $10,766 million equivalent required for imple-
menting the 1976-82 investment program, internal generation
of cash within the electricity sector including funds pro-
vided by special measures would amount to 38% of requirements;
including funds specially raised outside the sector for its
development, the total cash generation would amount to 45%
of requirements (5.12); and
1/   See Annex 4, Attachment 5.



- 23 -
(d) the composite organizations have agreed to achieve a minimum
cash generation level of 30% of expansion (5.16).
Accounting System and Financial Statements
5.02      The form of financial statements of all Yugoslav enterprises is
prescribed by the Federal Government. Bookkeeping follows the double-entry
system and the accrual system for recording transactions.  The fundamental
principle of the Yugoslav system is accounting for the use of assets which
are regarded as "social property" entrusted to the working community.  How-
ever, there are many deficiencies in the way these principles are imple-
mented in the power sector. The salient features of the Yugoslav power
sector accounting system are given in Annex 5, Attachment 1.
Revaluation of Assets
5.03      Since 1970 there have been two revaluations of assets for the power
enterprises in Yugoslavia, once in 1971 and again as of December 31, 1975
using coefficients established by the Federal Government for the whole of
Yugoslavia on the basis of statistics prepared by the Federal Institute for
Statistics for equipment, building materials and other construction costs.
The effect of the 1975 revaluation was to increase the value of gross fixed
assets and accumulated depreciation, in other words, of the net fixed assets
in service by almost 100%. For example, in Macedonia the effect was to raise
the net value of fixed assets by 93%. Since a federal law of 1975 also re-
quires a further revaluation whenever the prices used for revaluation increase
more than 10%, further revaluations are expected frequently, if not every
year, in the future. Since tariffs should cover the cost of service including
depreciation based on a realistic value of assets, the financial forecasts
used here assume future revaluations every two years beginning with 1977.
Financial Performance of the Borrowers Under Loan 836-YU
5.04      The Agreement under Loan 836-YU provides that rates for the sale of
power will be set at a level sufficient to cover in any given year operating
expenses including employee compensation, depreciation and interest, plus
debt amortization insofar as it exceeds depreciation, plus an average of not
less than 35% of investment in power facilities during any plan period of the
guarantor after payment of taxes and other contributions required by law,
if any. The Bank agreed that it would consider the following measures as
being satisfactory to meet the above requirements, i.e., special taxes, sur-
charges and levies on consumers and investors which are not repayable by the
borrowers and which are specifically earmarked for the expansion of power
generation and transmission facilities. Thus funds raised outside the elec-
tricity sector which are not related to the sale of electricity could also be
reckoned for computing the internal cash generation for investment (compare
the proposed financial covenant discussed in para 5.16). The actual self-
financing level of each of the borrowers in 1971-75 (average for 5 years) was
as follows:

-  Trebinje                          50%

5.05      The failure to fulfill the covenant in the case of Osijek and Zagreb
in Croatia, and Ljubljana and Maribor in Slovenia was mainly due to the sig-
nificantly higher investment program (for Zagreb and for the two borrowers in
Slovenia) and delayed implementation of the agreed special measures (connec-
tion charge) to raise additional revenues in Croatia. In the case of Croatia,
the borrowers have argued that the covenant would be fulfilled if all the
four borrowers in Croatia including Zagreb were considered together. There
is force in this argument since the additional measures were determined by
the republic on the basis of their impact on the entire sector in Croatia
and not on one particular enterprise; therefore it may be considered that
the intent of the covenant has been fulfilled.
Organizational Changes
5.06      Since 1972, the organization of the power sector has undergone
substantial changes in line with changes which have taken place in Yugoslavia
in accordance with the new constitution (2,10, 2.13 and 2.14). The 12 enter-
prises which were signatories to the loan agreement have become "basic or-
ganizations of associated labor" or have been split into more than one basic
organization in order to conform to the principles of self-management and
decentralization of decision-making which are basic to the constitutional
reorganization. Groups of basic organizations in each republic have estab-
lished "work organizations" through self-management agreements to coordinate
their principal functions. In each republic work organizations and basic
organizations have further joined together to form a single "composite
organization" for the whole republic. One of the principal functions of
these work organizations or composite organizations of associated labor,
which have also become members of JUtGEL, is to coordinate planning, con-
struction and financing of new investment programs in each republic. The
basic organizations have agreed to pool their free funds within the composite
(or coordinating) organization of associated labor in the respective republics.
The coordinating organization in each republic (in agreement with the respec-
tive power sector "communities of interest") has therefore become the main
planning organization with control over financial resources and the basic
organizations, although largely autonomous, are more and more only executing
agencies.



- 25 -
Financial Objectives of Proposed Loan
5.07      Because the BOALs have in the past functioned as autonomous enter-
prises and there was no coordination of financial planning or consolidation
of accounts at the Republican level, and since the composite organizations are
by and large still in the process of establishing themselves as coordinating
organizations for planning purposes, consolidated and reliable historical
financial information is still generally lacking. One major objective of the
proposed loan would be to strengthen the coordinating role of the composite
organizations and to enable them to fulfill their planning function effec-
tively. For this purpose, procedures should be developed for consolidation
of financial statements and management information systems need to be devel-
oped to provide management with prompt and regular information on operations
including financial results and implementation of the capital budget. All
the composite organizations acting through JUGEL have agreed to engage con-
sultants satisfactory to the Bank by December 31, 1977 to conduct a manage-
ment study and to make recommendations on organization, training, accounting
and financial planning procedures and on establishing adequate management
information systems and to assist in implementing the recommendations in con-
sultation with the Bank and in accordance with a timetable satisfactory to
the Bank. The study could be undertaken as an extension to the study already
being conducted under Loan 836-YU. An outline of the terms of reference for
the proposed study which was reviewed with the representatives of the composite
organizations and JUGEL during negotiations and which would serve as a guide
for the final terms of reference is given in Annex 3.
5.08      A second financial objective of this lending operation is to obtain
improvements in the financial forecasting techniques of the composite orga-
nizations and BOALs. Traditionally the enterprises in the Yugoslav power
sector have emphasized implementation of investment plans with comparatively
less emphasis on the proper planning and recording of operating results. The
forecasts of operating results which would now be reviewed in each January
(5.16) would enable the borrowers to focus their attention on this long-
neglected area.
5.09      A third objective to be achieved is in the area of cooperation
between republics for power sector planning. While the recent organizational
changes in the sector have enabled coordination of planning and mobilization
of resources at the republican level, there is considerable scope for inter-
regional coordination and cooperation in the event of implementation of a
least-cost project which could serve more than one Republic. Appropriate
procedures for providing funds and sharing costs would ensure expeditious
implementation of a least-cost development project especially if the project
were located in a region which is short of funds for investment. The matter
was reviewed with JUGEL and the composite organizations with a view to formally
establishing such procedures. The present practice of dealing with the matter
on a case by case basis would continue for the present. Formal procedures
would be established should the need arise in the future for such action.



- 26 -
Present Financial Position
5.10      The debt/equity ratio and the debt service coverage given below
in respect of the six composite organizations reflect the historical devel-
opment of the constituent units of these organizations and the terms on which
they were able to obtain loans in the past.

5.11      A high debt/equity ratio does not necessarily indicate a heavy debt
service burden because of the relatively soft terms of past loans (e.g., 50
years at 1-2% interest). The low debt service coverage in Slovenia reflects
the fact that electricity rates are not set to generate substantial surpluses
but are set only to meet the cost of operations. Any debt service commitment
which is not covered by internal cash generation would normally be met from
reserve funds or the pooled sources of the composite organizations. Therefore
a shortfall in a particular year caused by failure to achieve expected per-
formance, would not prejudice the financial position of a borrower. The future
financial position of these organizations would depend on the additional debts
they incur, the terms of these debts and the tariff levels.
Financing Plans
5.12      Annex 5 gives the financing plans of the six composite organizations
for the period 1976-1982 which assume significant tariff increases and other
measures. The following consolidated statement shows that for the sector as a
whole (including in some republics distribution activities also e.g. Croatia
but excluding the program in Kosovo and Vojvodina), the internal cash genera-
tion for expansion comes to 22% without the funds provided as grants through
special measures, and to 38% including such funds. This compares with the
minimum 35% self-financing level sought under Loan 836-YU. Borrowings would
meet 58% of the capital expansion requirements.
