5.13      Since the financing plan is dependent on adequate revisions of tariffs
for which the maximum levels are set by the Federal Government, Government would
revise such maximum levels suitably to enable bulk supply and retail tariffs to
be so fixed in each Republic and permit other measures to be taken to generate
the required internal funds for financing the sector development.
5.14      Some of the special measures which have been or would be implemented
to raise the required funds are as follows:



- 28 -
(a)  surcharges on new industrial investments;
(b)  special tax on retained earnings of industrial organizations;
(c)  surcharges on electricity consumption; and
(d)  connection charges based on electricity demand payable by
new consumers and existing consumers for increased demand.
However under the proposed loan, (a) and (b) would be excluded from reckoning
as internal cash generation for purposes of the proposed financial covenant
whereas (c) and (d) would be included (para 5.16).
Proposed Financial Covenant
5.15      The existing covenant under Loan 836-YU requires a cash generation
equivalent to an average of not less than 35% of the cost of investment in
power facilities during any plan period of the Guarantor. The plan period
of the Guarantor being 5 years, monitoring the borrowers' performance under
the covenant was difficult since the cash generation had to be related to
the average cost of investment over a 5-year period. A two-year period would
be more reasonable; however, a two-year period is not a planning cycle in
Yugoslavia and there are advantages in relating the covenant to the investment
in a planning cycle which is one year, five years or 15 years. On balance,
therefore, it has been agreed that the cash generation would be related to
the yearly investment in generation and transmission facilities.
5.16      The undertakings under Loan 836-YU provide for a cash generation
target of at least 35%. However funds raised outside the electricity sector
which are not related to the sale of electricity, e.g., taxes on investments
of enterprises could also be reckoned for computing the cash generation level.
In order to determine an appropriate cash generation level for the proposed
loan, the following factors are relevant:
(a)  Loan 836-YU requiring a minimum 35% cash generation was made
to transmission enterprises which had only some generation
whereas the proposed loan covers not only all transmission but
also the entire generation in each republic, a subsector where
cash generation has traditionally been lower;
(b)  it would be logical to exclude funds generated outside the
sector for computing internal cash generation; this would be a
significant change from Loan 836-YU;
(c)  the Federal Gazette of 1975 specifies about 30% as an appro-
priate target level of cash generation in Yugoslavia. Any
proposed covenant will have added strength if it is consonant
with Yugoslav legislation;



- 29 -
(d) the cash generation level should be regarded as a minimum and
it should be the constant endeavor of the composite organization
to achieve higher levels and thus reduce dependence on loans.
In view of the foregoing, agreement has been reached with the composite organi-
zations that they will achieve in each year beginning with 1978 cash genera-
tion of at least 30% of the yearly investment in generation and transmission
facilities, reckoning for this purpose in addition to electricity revenues,
funds related to the sale of electricity such as special taxes, connection
charges, customer contributions, surcharges and any other levies on customers,
which are non-repayable and are intended for the expansion of power facilities.
Contributions provided by law, e.g., to Collective Consumption and other Funds
would be deducted when computing the cash generation level. Undue fluctuations
in investment resulting in varying cash generation levels would be taken care
of by offsetting overruns or shortfalls in cash generation for a year against
shortfalls or overruns for the next year. Annex 5 shows the expected per-
formance of each of the Borrowers under the revised covenant. What the
covenant means in terms of a rate of return for each composite organization
is also discussed therein. To monitor this covenant, each Borrower would
furnish to the Bank by January 31 of each year starting with the year 1978
a forecast of the investment plan and the operating statement for the year,
setting out the various sources of financing for the planned investment during
that year and the measures to be taken to achieve the financial target. The
plan would be supported to the extent applicable by the agreement of the com-
munity of interest which has an important, if not a determining, voice in
the formulation of investment plans and their financing.
5.17      Since the legal status of many of the existing borrowers under
Loan 836-YU has changed and all borrowers have now agreed to pool their
financial resources at the respective republic levels (5.06), the existing
covenant on the contribution to investment is obsolete. Loan 836-YU will
be amended to conform to these proposed arrangements for meeting the cash
generation requirements, i.e. this would be the responsibility of the com-
posite organizations. However, responsibility for debt service under Loan
836-YU would remain with the existing borrowers under that loan.

5.18      The expected financial position of the composite organizations in

5.L9      Though the debt service coverage will generally have diminished
significantly in 1982 as compared to 1976, the financial condition of the
organizations would still be satisfactory as judged by a debt service cover-
age of 1.5 or more for all except EGS. In the case of EGS the low ratio
of coverage is deceptive inasmuch as it does not reflect huge accumulations
of funds which are not treated as operating revenues. Current policy in
all Republics is to ensure that tariffs are set to cover at least operating
costs including depreciation and interest charges. This policy is expected
to be maintained.
Audit
5.20      Annual financial statements in Yugoslavia are subject to audit by
the Social Accounting Service (SAS). The scope and quality of these audits
previously have been considered not adequate to meet the Bank's audit require-
ments. Currently, however, groups of SAS auditors are receiving training in
modern auditing methods under the direction of international auditors. This
training was commenced at the Bank's instigation, and has been closely moni-
tored by the Bank to create a Special Audit Group within SAS capable of carrying
out comprehensive audits acceptable to the Bank. About 70 staff members of the
Special Audit Group have so far started training and the program continues to
expand annually. The question of extending the Special Audit Group's audit
to the electricity enterprises' accounts has been discussed with SAS, but may
not be possible immediately because of SAS staffing constraints. For this
reason, such audits will have to be introduced gradually, starting with only
one or two borrowers in the first instance and this arrangement has already
been accepted by SAS in discussions with the Bank in the context of audit
of all Bank projects in Yugoslavia. The composite organizations would hold
discussions with SAS with a view to formulating by the end of 1977 a program
for the audit of their annual financial statements by the Special Audit Group
of SAS, in consultation with the Bank. Meanwhile, they would have their annual
consolidated accounts audited by SAS or other competent auditors acceptable to
the Bank. The timetable agreed by the composite organizations for submission
of their audited consolidated accounts to the Bank is that they would do so
within six months after the end of the year for 1977 and 1978 and within four
months after the end of the year for the years beginning with 1979. The
extended time limit of six months for the accounts of 1977 and 1978 is neces-
sary until the organizations are able to streamline procedures of consolida-
tion of accounts with the help of the management consultants (para 5.07).
Insurance
5.21      Arrangements for insurance of properties are made at two levels:
JUGEL first enters into a general agreement with the union of republican
insurance companies (one company for each republic) setting out the general
framework for insurance i.e. general insurance conditions and types of risks
covered and also the method of revaluation of assets for the purpose of
insurance but without mentioning specifically the premium amounts and risks
to be covered in each republic; each composite organization in a republic
then enters into a specific insurance agreement with the republican insurance
company within the general framework stipulating the risks covered and the
premium amounts. The risks covered generally are as follows:



- 31 -
(a) boiler explosion;
(b)  fire (general and electrical);
(c)  breakage and breakdown of plant and equipment;
(d)  third party liability;
(e)  workers' compensation;
(f)  natural calamities including earthquakes (a special risk
in Skopje).
5.22      There is no insurance against war or civil commotion.  Assets under
construction are insured by the contractors. Claims are generally met for
the full amounts though at times with some delay. Premium payments have
been generally more than the amount of claims submitted.
Cost Overruns
5.23      Under Loan 836-YU, the local financing institutions agreed to finance
cost overruns. For the proposed loan, the composite organizations have pro-
vided a guarantee from local financing institutions to the effect that they
would provide any additional funds required to cover possible cost overruns on
conditions satisfactory to them (financing institutions). It is expected that
the conditions would be more or less similar to existing lending terms.
6. JUSTIFICATION
General
6.01      Currently, consultants are formulating a least-cost system devel-
opment (LCD) plan for Yugoslavia's power sector which would rank power
projects in economic merit order. As a result, the 380-kv network in general
and the Project in particular cannot be shown to be a component in a least-cost
national development program. Consequently, the Project is considered in the
context of the Yugoslav setting and the approach to power system development
planning. The following issues were studied (see Annex 6):
A.   Justification of Project:  since no LCD-related alternative
can be identified, a general economic evaluation of the
380-kV network and its extensions is provided in addition
to the technical justification of each line and substation
in the Project.
B.   The nature and relative size of economic distortions that
may have been caused by the localized planning system and
methods.
C.   The rate of return on Yugoslavia's transmission and genera-
tion development program.



- 32 -
D.   The marginal cost of supply in Slovenia was compared with
the present tariff structure, which is about the same for
all republics.
6.02      It should be stressed that country-wide development plans, whether
for power or otherwise, and their evaluation (e.g. economic ranking irre-
spective of republic) are in principle inconsistent with the Yugoslav
constitution and socio-economic system. Each republic is autonomous and
the objective in each republic is to meet demands at minimum cost to its own
economy. In practice, both commercial (market) and social (equal spread of
benefits) considerations have introduced voluntary coordination measures. In
the power case, for instance, joint ownership of plant is an indication of
such coordination. Jointly owned plants are constructed in one republic, but
part is financed by other republic(s), e.g. the nuclear station at Krsko in
Slovenia, thermal plant at Tuzla in Bosnia-Herzegovina and in Kosovo, hydro
plant at Trebinje in Bosnia-Herzegovina. The effective transfer of electric
energy from republics richly endowed with energy resources to those poorer
in resources is therefore quite substantial (2.27) and benefits of scale are
obvious.
Justification of Project
6.03      Based on a set of cost relationships in the electricity supply
industry, one can show 1/ that the 380-kV network represents the optimum
network capacity to be used in the transmission of power in the country
because of the following factors that in particular pertain to Yugoslavia:
(a) the localized distribution of primary energy (i.e. hydropower
and lignite) while the load centers are dispersed around the
country;
(b) the deteriorating system reliability caused by the overload-
ing of the 220-kV transmission network;
(c) lignite will constitute the main source of primary energy and
is of poor quality not fit for export;
(d) if a new 220-kV instead of a 380-kV network is used both
capital and operating cost as well as the cost of acquiring the
right of way would be higher in providing the same service; and
(e) the grid presently used or being planned for by the neighboring
countries with whom Yugoslavia will largely trade power is at
380-kV.
6.04      Load flow studies show that 7 lines of the 13 included in the
Project are required because:
1/   See Annex 6.



- 33 -
(a) two lines have to be doubled because future normal load
of the existing lines would be in excess of their rated
capacity;
(b) five meshes should be created (rather than the more expensive
alternative of doubling existing lines) since existing lines
would be overloaded under certain outage conditions; and
(c) six new connections to power plants and international grids
are needed (see map).
For (a) and (b) above no alternatives are feasible (220-kV lines of the
required capacity would be more expensive and reach their loading capacity
earlier). Similarly, 4 lines pertaining to (c) are required to be executed
at 380-kV in view of loading conditions, i.e. one connection to Italy, one
connection to the 500 MW (first stage) Obrenovac B plant in Serbia, one
connection to the 420 MW (1980) plant at Bitola in Macedonia which would also
serve as the second connection with Greece (1983) and, finally, one line (to
Banja Luka in Bosnia-Herzegovina) would be the first line in a future (1985)
3-mesh system linking the eastern part of the country to the rest of the
country at considerably higher reliability than the two lines being completed
under the first phase. The first of the remaining two lines (to Pancevo - 450
MW by 1982 - near Belgrade) would constitute the initial line of a 380-kV ring
around Belgrade to be completed by 1985. For the remaining line (to Kranj
in Slovenia) there is some doubt that 380-kV would be the appropriate voltage,
but, since it is only 35 km long and additional 220-kV transformers at
Ljubljana would largely offset its additional cost, the difference would
be small, particularly in relation to the overall cost of the Project.
6.05      Under the first phase, only a small number of the larger towns are
being direclty connected to the 380-kV system. This is now corrected and
towns like Rijeka, Podlog, Split, Sarajevo, Titograd, Negotino, Subotica and
Pancevo will be connected; the substations would additionally connect the
existing 110-kV and 220-kV directly to the 380-kV system in order to unload
these systems and increase reliability of supply considerably. With the
additional transformer capacity, the total capacity at all transmission levels
(110-kV, 220-kV, and 380-kV) in relation to maximum demand would remain at
the level of about 2.5 MVA of capacity per MVA of demand which constitutes
a normal required average. The capacity appears on the high side in 1982
(at 2.8 MVA/MVA of maximum demand) but this is reasonable because of technical
considerations; transport difficulties of the 300 MVA and 400 MVA units are
major in the mountainous country and a temporary excess in reserves appears
justified.
6.06      Replacement of the present inadequate dispatch facilities (2.17) is
urgent, but up to recently the individual republican designs have not been suf-
ficiently coordinated to avoid redundancies. The ongoing study of procedures
and facilities for system operation and energy management (3.18) and the engage-
ment of consultants for preparation of bid documents (3.19) is expected to
lead to dispatch facilities adequately adapted to the needs in each republic
and the network as a whole in the context of least-cost dispatching.



- 34 -
Yugoslav Development Programs and Possible Planning Distortions
6.07      The optimality of the capacity selected does not imply that the in-
vestments over time in the Project follow an optimum pattern. Investments in
transmission will be allocated optimally over time if they are integrated with
the least-cost development plan of the generating capacities. If the least-
cost investment program in generation differs significantly from the Yugoslav
plan, it could alter (bring forward or retard some lines of the network) the
investment schedule of the transmission grid. Therefore, it is important to
determine possible sources of distortions and their relative significance.
Despite the lack of a comprehensive national plan, there appears to be little
distortion in the use of primary energy resources for generating plant. By
1982 the capacity and type of plant to be added is:
Plant Type                                            MW                 %
Hydro                                               1,696               16.4
Pumped Storage                                      1,114               10.8
Oil-fueled                                            740                7.2
Oil-fueled (also district heating)                    645                6.2
Coal-fueled                                           325                3.0
Lignite-fueled                                      4,922               47.6
Lignite-fueled (also district heating)                 50                0.5
Gas-turbines                                          183                 1.8
Nuclear                                               664                6.4
10,339              100.0
In view of Yugoslavia's perennial capacity shortages (rather than energy
shortages) the peaking capacity in gas turbine and pumped storage plant (13%
of total) appears still rather limited. Because lignite-fueled plants have
limited peaking possibilities (they generally require base loading not below
70-80% of capacity), the availability of some oil-fueled plant (8% only) with
better peaking and operational possibilities appears fully justified, the
more so because most of it is located in Croatia, where most of the oil is
presently produced.
6.08      The largest unit size (except the nuclear plant at Krsko of 664 MW,
50% of which pertains to Slovenia and 50% to Croatia) is now 310 MW or 4% of
peak demand, and the next step would be 500 MW. Since this would be lignite
plant, which is difficult to operate, use of proven and reliably designed
boilers is essential for reasonably high availability. No measurable economic
distortions appear present due to selection of unit sizes, the more so because
economies of scale are less marked for lignite-fueled plant than for oil-fueled
plant.
6.09      Some distortions may have been caused by the massive construction
of thermal plant initiated in 1973/74 rather than hydro plant, i.e. the plant
mix may not be optimum, but the main causes are: (a) the after-effects of the
1965 economic reform (2.10), (b) only thermal plant with a relatively short
lead time could be completed in time to meet system requirements; and (c)
financial constraints (see 6.11).



- 35 -
6.10      Most power plants are located near the sources of primary energy
and the few exceptions (e.g. the Montenegro and Macedonia oil-fired plants)
are probably justified in view of the necessity to spread reserve capacity
throughout the country. Thus no measurable distortion is apparent because
of inappropriate location of plant.
6.11      Republican selection and ranking of plant has been on a financial
basis (using a method assessing incremental operational cost including de-
preciation) rather than on an economic basis. The importance of any economic
distortions caused by this approach cannot be estimated with any degree of
certainty, but the introduction of least-cost development studies (2.25), both
at the republican and country level, will permit future ranking of plant on
economic rather than financial grounds. It will also allow measuring distor-
tions if republican decisions contravene country-wide considerations.
6.12      Financial constraints have probably been the largest cause of dis-
tortions. Borrowing for thermal plant has been relatively easy, but difficult
for hydro plant with its high local component. Since this is largely a coun-
try problem that only in part may be solved by borrowers coordinating their
search for funds (as is being done for the Project), pragmatic questions of
financing may continue to dictate departures from any preconceived program.
Rate of Return
6.13      The internal economic rate of return (IER) is the discount rate
which equates the present value of the cost and benefit streams during the
life of the Project under consideration. For this Project, although its cost
can be determined, isolation of its benefits (as approximated by the revenue
it generates) is impossible because the distribution entities in Yugoslavia
are not integrated (except for Croatia) with the generation and transmission
enterprises and consequently detailed data on investment in distribution
systems and retail tariffs are not available. Therefore, the rate of return
is calculated for the entire Yugoslav generation and transmission development
program between 1977 and 1982. The yearly average bulk rate per kWh that would
enable each borrower to meet the financial covenant of the Bank is used as a
proxy for the benefits. The cost of the program and the benefits are expressed
in 1977 dollars. The estimated rate of return is 13.6%. 1/
6.14      Assuming that the prices paid at the bulk level represent a minimum
value of the benefits, the above rate of return would represent a lower limit
for the return on the program and, by extension, the Project. Assuming 10%
higher capital costs the rate of return would be 12.3%. If electricity rates
were 10% higher, the rate of return would be 16.4%. The sensitivity tests
indicated that the rate of return is more sensitive to variations in elec-
tricity rates than to changes in capital costs. The rate of return is further
understated since only revenues resulting from direct charges for electricity
consumption are included; taxes on investments and similar charges designed
to meet sector financing needs have not been included (see Annex 5).
1/   See Annex 6, Attachment 1.



- 36 -
6.15      Because sufficient information is available on Croatia's investment
in distribution and average revenue at the retail level, the IER was similarly
calculated on the overall republican 1977-1982 development program. 1/ The
rate of return could not be used to draw any conclusion about the tariff
levels at the distribution level across all republics because of the signi-
ficant difference in the cost of production between Croatia and most of the
other republics (a difference attributable to Croatia's heavy dependence on
oil for power generation).
Evaluation of the Yugoslav Tariff System
6.16      Because tariffs and rates at the bulk and the retail level 2/ are
not too wide apart and information was the most complete for Slovenia, this
republic was selected for a comparison of its tariffs with a marginal-cost
based tariff covering the Project period (1977-82). This comparison (see
Annex 6) confirms the expectation that the present schedules do not reflect
actual cost of supply (2.21).  Some simplifications had to be made but the
differences with marginal costs are so pronounced that preliminary conclusions
are not materially affected:
(a) Bulk Tariffs. The present capacity charges (Din 199.951kW
per month in Slovenia) appear to be some 50% below the mar-
ginal kW cost and the present energy charges some 30-40%
below the marginal kWh cost. The distortions are probably
most marked in Bosnia-Herzegovina and Croatia (Din 125-120/kW
per month) because investment in new plant is higher than
in Slovenia 3/ and the energy charges probably exceed the
marginal kWh cost.
(b) Retail Tariffs. Both for domestic and non-domestic supply,
the present capacity charges appear to be only some 10% of
the marginal kW cost. The energy charges for domestic supply
appear to be on the order of marginal kWh costs, but for non-
domestic supply they exceed marginal kWh costs.
6.17      The present surcharges on each bill, 4/ varying for bulk supply from
0% (Montenegro, Serbia) to 24% (Slovenia) of the bill, identified as "contribu-
tion towards investments", in effect constitute a capacity charge which may or
may not bring the total up to about marginal kW cost (in Slovenia it probably
does), but this distinction probably is lost to the consumers, particularly
industrial consumers. They do not obtain any direct message with respect to
the effects of their consumption during the peak hours. In three of the four
1/   See Annex 6, Attachment 1.
2/   See Annex 1, Attachments 8 and 9.
3/   See Annex 2, Attachment 6.
4/   See Annex 1, Attachment 10.



- 37 -
republics where it is levied, the surcharge is expressed in para/kWh and
probably felt as an energy charge, further distorting the message to the
consumer.
6.18      For retail sales the surcharge is similar, either on a percentage
basis (48% for non-domestic and 60% for domestic in Slovenia) or on a para/kWh
basis (the differences between republics and domestic and non-domestic are
large), without any apparent tariff rationale.
6.19      By and large it appears that - at least in Slovenia - on the average,
taking into account the surcharges: (a) bulk tariffs do not provide revenue
in accordance with marginal cost; (b) retail revenue from domestic consumers
is in approximate accordance with marginal cost; and (c) non-domestic con-
sumers are over-charged somewhat. Whether this is also the case in the other
republics can only be learned from detailed studies. These preliminary con-
clusions will be discussed with the Government and the borrowers in connection
with the review of the results of the on-going tariff study (2.21).
7. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
7.01      The terms of reference for the tariff study now under way have been
amended to include marginal-cost pricing (2.21).
7.02      The borrower and executing agencies would sign the coordination
guidelines covering the execution of the Project as a condition of effective-
ness of the proposed loan (3.16).
7.03      The executing agencies agreed on a construction schedule and other
measures assuring concurrent completion of related facilities included in the
Project (3.17).
7.04      Consultants would be engaged by JUGEL by December 31, 1977 under con-
ditions and terms acceptable to the Bank, for preparation of bid documents for
the dispatch facilities (3.19).
7.05      JUGEL agreed to submit and discuss with the Bank the least-cost
development study and the tariff study, and the need for further work in these
areas (3.20).
7.06      The borrowers agreed to provide to the Bank by June 30, 1978, satis-
factory information concerning arrangements for remaining external financing
required for the Project; arrangements for financing the dispatch facilities
would be completed before June 30, 1979. Finalization of local financing
arrangements and financing guarantees in each republic to cover any cost
overruns would be a condition of effectiveness (3.22).
7.07      Changes in the borrowers organizational structure would require
Bank approval, including appropriate provision for assumption of commitments
under the loan agreement (4.05).



- 38 -
7.08      JUGEL agreed (4.07):
(a) to review with the Bank, before December 31, 1977, the
consultant's recommendations for JUGEL's organization and
staffing, and that changes would be implemented within 6
months;
(b) to create a Project Unit for Phases I and II of the 380-kV
network, organized and staffed in a manner satisfactory to
the Bank, which would represent the borrowers in all matters
that pertain to any dealings with the Bank concerning the
Project. The Project Unit is to be fully operational as a
condition of effectiveness of the proposed loan.
7.09      By June 30, 1978, the generation and transmission organizations
would sign an amending agreement on the operation of the interconnected system
which will assign functional control of the 380-kV network to JUGEL and create
a transmission pool for regulation and administering of all inter-republic
purchases and sales of power (4.08).
7.10      JUGEL would provide to the Bank as part of each year's last quarterly
progress report, data for existing and new plant, actual forecast balances of
energy and capacities, and the investment program (4.09); and as part of each
year's second quarterly report,. specific monitoring information (4.10).
7.11      The composite organizations will:
(a) by December 31, 1977 engage consultants satisfactory to the
Bank to conduct a management study and to make recommendations
on organization, training, accounting and financial planning
procedures and on establishing adequate management informa-
tion systems and to assist in implementing the recommendations
in consultation with the Bank and in accordance with a time-
table satisfactory to the Bank (5.07);
(b) achieve a cash generation level of a minimum 30% of the cost
of construction of power facilities during each year (5.16);
(c) furnish to the Bank by January 31 of each year a plan of
their operations and investments during the year, setting
out the various sources of financing for the planned
investments during that year and supported to the extent
applicable by the agreement of the community of interest
(5.16);
(d) submit their audited consolidated accounts to the Bank within
six months after the end of the year for 1977 and 1978 and
within four months after the end of the year for the years
from 1979, and hold discussions with SAS with a view to
formulating by the end of 1977 a program for the audit of
their annual financial statements by the Special Audit Group
of SAS, in consultation with the Bank (5.20).



- 39 -
7.12      The Federal Government will revise maximum levels of tariffs suitably
to enable bulk supply and retail tariffs to be so fixed, and other measures
to be taken, in each Republic as to generate the required internal funds for
financing the sector development (5.13).
7.13      A Loan Assumption Agreement amending Loan 836-YU to conform to the
arrangements under the proposed loan would be entered into between the Bank,
the borrowers and the relevant signatories (5.17).
7.14      Subject to the foregoing, a Bank loan of US$80 million equivalent
is recommended for a term of 15 years including 3 years' grace. Signature of
guidelines covering coordination of project execution (7.02), completion of
arrangements for local funds (7.06), and full operation of the Project Unit
(7.08) would be conditions of effectiveness. The loan could be effective
within five months of signature.

ANNEX 1
Page 1 of 14
YUGOSLAVIA
SECOND POWER TRANSMISSION PROJECT
Past Developments
A.   Primary Energy Resources and Sector
General
1.        Yugoslavia is relatively rich in primary energy resources; they
consist predominantly (about 90% in terms of heat equivalent) of low-grade
coal (lignite and brown coal), located mainly in Kosovo, Serbia and Bosnia-
Herzegovina. There is a sizeable hydropower potential, most of it in Bosnia-
Herzegovina and Serbia. Known hydrocarbon reserves (oil and natural gas)
are relatively small and are mostly located in Serbia and Croatia. Other
potential energy resources exist (nuclear, geothermal and oil shales) but
have not yet been accurately evaluated. A Bank mission carried out an ex-
tensive review of the Yugoslav energy sector in October 1975 and the following
summarizes its findings.
Coal
2.        Commercially utilizable coal reserves total some 22 billion tons
(Gt), equivalent to several hundred years' supply at current rate of consump-
tion (35-40 Mt). The largest part (83%) in terms of heat content consist of
l-ignite, with an average calorific value of 2,300 kcal/kg and high moisture
and ash content. Brown coal (average calorific value 3,900 kcal/kg) repres-
ents 16% of total coal reserves, and hard coal (average calorific value 6,200
kcal/kg) accounts for the remaining 1%. Most of the lignite deposits are
suitable for openpit mining, but the brown and hard coal reserves mostly
require underground mining techniques.
Hydrocarbons
3.        Proven recoverable reserves of crude oil (all onshore) are about
50 Mt, equivalent to about 15 years' supply at the 1974 level of production
(3.5 Mt), covering about 30% of domestic requirements for that year. The
known oil fields are relatively shallow (2,500 meters) and there are indica-
tions that deeper exploration (below 3,000 m) could result in a doubling or
trebling of recoverable reserves. Proven reserves of natural gas are estimated
at 47 Gm3, equivalent to some 30 years' supply at the 1974 production rate
(1.4 Gm3), plus a further 53 Gm3 of probable reserves.
4.        Offshore exploration for oil and gas began relatively recently.
There have been no commercial discoveries so far, but the indications are
promising. An extensive offshore program in collaboration with foreign
partners is being prepared, and it is expected that work will start as soon
as a law on offshore development has been approved.



ANiNEX 1
Page 2 of 14
Hydropower
5.        Yugoslavia is one of the most richly endowed countries in Europe
in hydropower. The total exploitable energy potential is estimated at about
65 billion kWh (TWh), with a power capacity of 17,000 MrW. About 22 TWh has
been developed to date (4,800 MW) and a further 7 TWh (2,800 MW) is under
construction or planned, leaving 35 TWh (about 9,000 MW) still be to devel-
oped, although some of this (up to 13 TWh or some 3,000 MW, according to one
estimate), at sites located in densely populated river valleys or in smaller
catchment areas, may not be suitable for development.
Other Energy Resources
6.        Yugoslavia has exploitable reserves of uranium (U308) and the first
uranium mine is being developed to provide fuel for the first nuclear power
station now under construction in Krsko in Slovenia. Estimates of assured
reserves range from 10,000-22,000 short tons U308, sufficient to fuel 1,500-
3,300 MW of nuclear generating capacity for 30 years, assuming an average
annual plant factor of 68%. Total reserves, including "probable" and
"possible", may be several times higher than the figures quoted.
7.        Oil-bearing shales are known to exist in Croatia, Macedonia and
Serbia. No detailed geological exploration has been undertaken to establish
their extent and oil content but, according to one estimate, the main source
so far identified (Aleksinac in Serbia) has about 2 billion tons of shale
with an average oil content of some 10%. Exploitation would depend on the
development of a suitable technology for extracting the oil economically.
8.        Although there are geothermal indications, no estimates are avail-
able of potential geothermal reserves. Solar energy could be another primary
energy source in the longer term, but, as with oil shale, this is likely to
depend on technological developments outside of Yugoslavia.
Past Trends
9.        Primary energy requirements have been growing at over 8%/a, rising
from some 15 million tons oil equivalent (toe) 1/ in 1966 to about 28 Mtoe
in 1974. These global figures conceal wide divergencies in the trends for
the different primary energy sources. The main feature, as in other countries,
has been the much more rapid growth of demand for hydrocarbons (oil over 16%/a,
7
1/   Taking 1 toe as equal to 10  kcal.



ANNEX 1
Page 3 of 14
gas over 18%/a) because of their cheapness (over most of the period) and con-
venience, compared with the virtual stagnation in coal demand (under 2% annual
growth).  Hydropower production grew at about the same rate as total energy
demand.  As a result of these different trends, the share of coal in total
primary energy supplies fell from 55% to 34%, while the share of hydrocarbons
rose from 27% to 47% and that of hydropower from 18% to 19%. In view of
Yugoslavia's limited domestic hydrocarbon resources, the rapid growth of
demand for oil has led to greatly increased dependence on imports for meeting
total energy requirements, the share of imports rising from 10% in 1966 to 32%
in 1974.
10.       The 8% growth rate of energy usage has been significantly above the
rate for GNP (6.6%/a), resulting in an energy coefficient (elasticity of energy
growth to GNP growth) of about 1.2, which is high compared with most high-
income developing countries (about 1.0) and developed countries (0.6-1.0).
Energy Policy and Problems
11.       The present energy policy of Yugoslavia has been developed in
response to the drastic change in the world energy scene following the oil
price rises of 1973/74. Its main features are the maximum development of
domestic resources to meet energy requirements, close integration of the coal
and electric power industries in view of the projected growth of coal-fired
generating capacity, and efficient use of energy. These are sound objectives
but a recent Bank review of the energy sector identified a number of problems
which stand in the way of their successful achievement. One of the most
serious problems is the apparent lack of any effective coordination of
domestic energy resource exploitation to ensure optimum development from
the national point of view and appropriate allocation of investment funds
to achieve this. An associated problem is the lack of agreed countrywide
economic and financial criteria to evaluate alternative development plans
and projects in the energy sector. Other problem areas are energy conser-
vation, where little appears to have been done so far to implement the
declared objective of increased efficiency in energy use, environmental
protection and energy pricing. The last is particularly important, since
it impinges on many other objectives, such as the promotion of more effi-
cient use of energy, conservation and the generation of funds to finance
the ambitious investment program proposed for the energy sector. At present
primary energy prices are below international levels and - even with the
recent price increases - may still not adequately reflect costs. The Govern-
ment has been reluctant to raise them on the ground that this would increase
inflation and damage the competitiveness of export industries. However, any
such adverse effects are likely to be relatively insignificant and short-
term and would be greatly outweighed by the gains in elimination of waste
and improved resource allocation resulting from the adoption of prices for
energy resources based on their long-term marginal costs.



ANNEX 1
Page 4 of 14
12.       Discussions are under way with the Yugoslav authorities concerned
on these and other problems of the energy sector. It is hoped that, as a
result, solutions can be found, including possible institutional changes,
that are consistent with the Yugoslav system of social planning based on
self-management and social compacts.
B.   The Power Subsector
History
13.        The years 1945-1955 were marked by reconstruction of facilities
damaged during the war although by the end of the period some new hydro and
thermal stations were added and installed capacity reached about 1,200 MW
(1939: 500 MW). Until the early fifties, however, power supply was substan-
tially regional. Rapid development took place from 1955-1960 when the in-
stalled capacity doubled and the 110-kV system, which was interconnected
throughout the country, reached a length of some 5,500 km. Transmission
capacity, however, was limited and in the early sixties construction of a
220-kV system commenced. The work on the 380-kV system, which was initiated
in 1970 to connect the 1,024 MW Djerdap (Iron Gate on the Danube) hydro sta-
tion to Belgrade, accelerated in 1972 with Bank financing of about 3000 km
of line and 7,700 MVA in substation facilities. A noticeable trend has been
the increasing share of the public power entities in the country's electricity
production which rose from 81% in 1955 to 92% in 1975, with consequent econ-
omies of production. By the end of 1975 installed capacity in the country was
about 9 MW and production 40 TWh (in the public system 8.3 GW, producing 18.6
TWh and imports 1.2 TWh), transmission lines aggregated almost 16,000 km
(110 kV: 10,650 km; 220 kV:  4,550 km; 380 kV:  570 km) and substation capacity
reached about 18,000 MVA.
14.       The operation and management of the power subsector was under strong
federal control until 1965, when economic reform accelerated the policy of
decentralization and self-management for the sector, through the Electricity
Act of the same year, which still governs the sector - although in recent
years the tendency for improved coordination on the republican level has
accelerated.
15.       The decentralized nature of the sector led to a lack of technical
cooperation and difficulties in coordinating investment allocations; deci-
sions were made on a local basis without due regard to overall republican
and country planning for power development. This, together with lack of
funds, has caused perennial power shortages since 1970. During 1973, an
extremely dry year, conditions were particularly severe, when an estimated
5-6% of total electricity requirements could not be met. In subsequent
years, some 1-2% of total electricity requirements could not be met although
hydro conditions were generally good. In 1973/74 crash construction programs
were instituted for new power stations, particularly thermal, aggregating
some 4,000 MW (almost 50% of the 1973 installed capacity in the country)
but the situation is not expected to improve before 1978 at the earliest,
when most of these stations will be commissioned.



ANNEX 1
Page 5 of 14
Organization
16.       On the basis of the 1974 constitution, the sector is strictly
regionalized in republics and split into Distribution, Transmission and
Generation. The workers' position is central; an appropriate number organize
themselves under a "Self-Management Agreement" into a "Basic Organization of
Associated Labor" (BOAL). For power this enterprise might be a single power
plant or distribution undertaking. BOALs may enter into self-management
agreements with other BOALs to form a joint enterprise, known as a "Work
Organization of Associated Labor" (WOAL) which could comprise, for example,
BOALs concerned with transmission, research and development, and support
services, as well as generating plants. The work organizations may then
enter into self-management agreements with other work organizations to form
a "Composite Organization of Associated Labor" (COAL or composite organiza-
tion). Although they achieved this status at different times - and in some
instances have not yet fully achieved it - all transmission and generating
enterprises in each republic are now combined into composite organizations.
In some instances distribution enterprises have also joined, a tendency that
is expected to continue. The subsector presently comprises about 210 basic
organizations with some 47,000 workers. Attachment 1 summarizes the defini-
tion of the various labor organizations as provided in the constitution.
17.       All organizations for generation and transmission, presently number-
ing 32 (which can each be either a COAL, WOAL, or BOAL, or any combination of
these three) are required by law to be associates (i.e. members) of the Union
of Yugoslav Electric Power Industry (JUGEL) for which the organizational
membership chart is shown in Attachment 2. Distribution organizations,
which have their own separate association, are not obliged to be members
of JUGEL, but 16 of them (out of a total of 20 in the country) have so far
joined and the rest are expected to do so shortly. In addition, JUGEL includes
among its members 7 research institutes and 2 firms of consulting engineers.
JUGEL is supposed to be responsible for coordinating planning and operations
(i.e. dispatch) in the interconnected system but, in fact, it is a rather
loose confederation or association rather than a union because no executive
authority has been delegated to it over its members (except to a limited
degree in the execution of its dispatch function) and has to arrive at deci-
sions through a complicated system of voting or, in the case of important
matters, through consensus. It is also responsible for the preparation
of tariff proposal through a special committee, for technical regulations,
collection of statistics and promotion of research. Its governing body is
the Assembly, which appoints a Board of Management and the General Manager,
subject to federal approval.
18.       A recent development is the creation of "Self-Management Communities
of Interest"i (see Attachment 1). These communities of interest, some of which
are still in the formative stage, are required to be established by the organ-
izations of associated labor and the beneficiaries of their services. In the
case of power they are responsible for (a) determining long-term development



ANNEX 1
Page 6 of 14
programs, (b) determining financing of the programs, (c) coordinating devel-
opment programs for distribution with those for transmission and generation,
(d) determining connection charges for new consumers, and (e) establishing
final tariffs for distribution consumers. The communities of interest can
be organized on a regional basis (for power they correspond to the distri-
bution BOALs) in which case they combine at the republican level, by means
of a self-management agreement, involving (for power) the composite organ-
izations for generation and transmission. This is the case in all republics
except Croatia where only a single power community of interest has been
created at the republican level. Communities of interest are governed by an
Assembly on which the power industry and consumers have equal representation.
In the first instance decisions are made by the delegates of the power in-
dustry voting separately; a decision will, however, be accepted only if
supported by both the majority of the power industry delegates and the
majority of the consumers' delegates. If the communities of interest fail
to provide all the necessary funds for financing the relevant development
program, this has to be resolved by republican law. Similarly, if the com-
munity fails to fulfill its obligation regarding any of the other matters for
which it is responsible, these are determined at the political level by the
Republican Assembly.
Bank Lending for Power and Role in the Subsector
19.       Three power loans have been made previously to Yugoslavia:  Loan
277-YU (MUS$30; 1961) for the 266-MW Senj hydro plant on the Lika and Gacka
rivers in Croatia, Loan 318-YU (MUS$30; 1962) for the 36--MW Bajina Basta
hydro station on the Drina river in Serbia, both completed in 1969 and
operating satisfactorily, and Loan 836-YU (MUS$75; 1972) for the first phase
of the 380-kY transmission system comprising about 3,000 km of lines and
7,600 MVA in substation facilities, interconnecting the whole of the country.
Construction of the latter project is about one year behind schedule. A
power component (34-MW hydro) is also included in the Ibar Multipurpose Water
Project (Loan 777, 1971), which is expected to be operational in 1977. A
Bank loan of US$70 million, made in 1975 for the 450-MW Buk Bijela hydropower
station in Bosnia-Herzegovina, failed to become effective because the Federal
Government could not obtain ratification of the Guarantee Agreement due to an
inter-republican riparian dispute over the use of the waters at the proposed
station site, which arose subsequent to the Bank's appraisal and consideration
of the loan by the Board. A power component (34-MW hydro) is also included in
the Ibar multipurpose water project (Loan 777-YU; 1971) which is scheduled for
completion in 1977. Also, a Bank sector mission carried out an extensive
review of the Yugoslav energy sector in October 1975.
20.       The first 380-kV power transmission project has met with some
difficulties:



ANNEX 1
Page 7 of 14
(a) Cost overruns (which have been met by loans from the Federal
Republic of Germany, some local loans and the borrowers' own
resources) are expected to be in the order of MUS$200 (original
cost estimate MUS$225) or about 90%, due to the rapid rise in
both labor wages and equipment prices since 1973.
-(b) The Bank's objective of improved coordination has been only
partly fulfilled. As a result there were continuing power
shortages, lack of standardization of equipment, and poor
coordination of construction (for instance not all major
interrelated items are completed concurrently).
(c) Consultants, who would have provided a diagnostic review
of the ills and recommendations for improvements, were not
engaged before the middle of 1976, some 3-1/2 years late.
(d) Reporting is lagging; it has been deficient especially on
financial matters because the organization of the sector
is not conducive to the preparation of consolidated fund
statements and technical and financial forecasts, while
expertise in these fields was lacking.
(e) Performance of the committee coordinating the works was not
satisfactory, primarily because of lack of an adequate number
of staff in JUGEL assisting the coordinating committee that
could act authoritatively - by virtue of its expertise - in
dealing with republican experts, local and foreign consultants,
and the Bank.
Planning
21.       There is at present no system planning in the sense of a single
coordinated development plan for the whole of the subsector, based on an
objective evaluation of the feasibile alternatives in terms of plant and
development strategies in order to determine the least-cost means, from the
point of view of the national economy, of meeting future requirements. The
procedure has been for each republic to make its own plans for satisfying
forecast demands. In doing this it gives automatic priority to the construc-
tion of plants on its own territory if it has unexploited hydro potential or
fuel resources suitable for development. Failing this, it may enter into
joint projects for the production of power with other republics which have
available energy resources.
22.       The republican plans are consolidated by JUGEL to form the national
development program but JUGEL has no authority to take an independent initia-
tive to identify alternatives or to satisfy itself that the resulting collec-
tion of projects represents the optimum development program from the view-
point of the national economy. A study was completed by JUGEL in 1972 and
updated in 1976 on the development of the interconnected system up to 1980
but this also took the republican programs as given, but the study did not



ANNEX 1
Page 8 of 14
include establishment of economic merit order of projects or evaluation of
alternative development strategies. Consequently, there can, as yet, be no
firm assurance that the proposed expansion program represents the least-cost
means of meeting the forecast requirements.
23.       Because of the Bank's concern with the evaluation of the republican
development programs in comparison with an overall program from the stand-
point of the national economy and with a view to modernizing the sector
evaluating procedures, JUGEL has been authorized in November 1976 to sign
contracts with a combination of three institutions (Energoproject of Beograd,
Nicola Tesla of Beograd and Milana Vidnarja of Ljubljana) for the execution
of a least-cost development study in general accordance with terms of refer-
ence provided by the Bank. The study is expected to be completed in 18
months.
Market Aspects and Production
24.       Historically, the market has been substantially defined by the
policy of decentralization resulting in the particular physical and organ-
izational characteristics of the various sectors, including power. The power
responsibilities are physically delineated because each republic is autonomous
and tries to balance its power capacities and energies within the market re-
quirements of its own area (the regional delineation within each republic
that has persisted for many years has largely disappeared). Organizationally,
power responsibilities within each republic have been further delineated by
the split into distribution and generation/transmission while, on the market
side, because of similar self-management of other sectors (particularly in-
dustry) and lack of coordination between sectors, each sector has developed
to some extent independently from supply. This has caused power shortages
and possibly also economic distortions. It appears justified to assume that
normal market forces have forced the supply side - through improved coordina-
tion - to meet requirements in the future more effectively and equitably.
This has not yet led to similar actions on an inter-republican level except
for the construction of the 380-kV system and the possibility for a republic,
by financing part of a power station in another republic, to obtain the
exclusive right to the capacity and energy involved. The objectives of an
annual balance of capacities and energies at the republican level, however,
still remain unchanged, even to the extent that the capacity of a plant
financed in another republic is included in the balance of the republic that
financed the plant, a policy that is not followed in this report.
25.       The 1955-1975 development is shown in Attachment 3.  Growth rates
of production (i.e. generation from plant within the republic) have varied
widely, from under 8%/a in Slovenia to 18%/a in Montenegro (Table 1). As a
result there have been significant changes between republics; Serbia's share
in production rose from 20% to 44% and Bosnia-Herzegovina's from 14% to 20%,
while Slovenia's share decreased from 39% to 17% and Croatia's from 22% to 15%.



ANNEX I
Page 9 of 14
26.       Broken down in 5 year periods (Table 2), consumption has also
followed divergent trends. National consumption increased from over 12%/a
in 1961-65 to nearly 16%/a in 1966-70, but declined to 10%/a in 1971-75.
Only 2 republics show an acceleration in growth for the period: Bosnia-
Herzegovina from about 11%/a in 1965-66 to 26%/a in 1971-74, and Montenegro
from 23%/a in 1961-65 to 27%/a in 1971-74. The tendency in other republics
is downward, particularly in Slovenia (10%/a to 6%/a).
27.       The most notable change in the market shares of the various con-
sumer categories (Table 3) is the substantial shift from industry to house-
holds, which for 1960-1975 is summarized as follows:
Industry        Households           Other
1960    1975    1960       1975   1960      1975
---------------% of Consumption---------------
Yugoslavia, total                  70      59       17         28     13        13
Bosnia-Herzegovina                 68      63       11         26     21        11
Croatia                            58      56       26         27     16        17
Macedonia                          74       75      19         20      7         5
Montenegro                         72      79       17         13     11         8
Serbia                             60      48       21         42     15        16
Slovenia                           75      67       16         20      9        13
The above table brings out the wide range of variation: in 1975 industry con-
stituted a low of 48% of the market in Serbia and a high of 79% in Montenegro;
conversely, households constituted a low of 13% in Montenegro and a high 42%
in Serbia.
28.       Per capita consumption nearly quadrupled between 1960 and 1974
(Attachment 3, Table 4), rising from 396 to 1,652 kWh. Wide disparities
exist, varying (1975) from 900 kWh/c in Kosovo to 3,340 kWh/c in Slovenia.
The rate of increase in per capita consumption has been most marked in
Montenegro - from 245 kWh (1960) to 2,705 kWh (1975) - and Macedonia from 264
to 1,714 kWh. The relatively high figure for Montenegro reflects the estab-
lishment of a highly power-intensive industry in recent years (aluminum
smelting).
29.       It is estimated that presently about 94% of the population has
access to electricity, compared with 55% in 1960. Rural electrification has
been rapid; 36% only of all rural dwellings were connected in 1960, the pre-
sent estimate is about 80%.
30.       Power exchanges with neighboring countries have been relatively
insignificant except in 1975, when imports represented about 3% of produc-
tion in the interconnected system, indicating increased import possibilities
due to new connections (9 in 1970 to which 3 were added at 220 kV and one at
380 kV in 1975) to meet part of Yugoslavia's power deficit.



ANNEX 1
Page 10 of 14
Generation and Transmission Facilities
31.       As already indicated above (para 13) the existing system was essen-
tially created after 1945. In 1939 total installed generating capacity in the
country was only 505 MW and production 1,200 GWh. By 1975 (see Attachment 4)
installed capacity was 9,043 MW (Table 1) and production 40 TWh (Table 4), of
which 8,381 MW was in the interconnected (JUGEL) system, producing 37.4 TWh.
The rapid increase since 1955 resulted from the central place given to elec-
t:rification in the development plans; the annual additionally installed
capacity rose from an average of 250 MW in 1956-60 to 640 MW in 1966-70. This
was followed by a substantial slowing-down to about 430 MW during 1971-75.
There has been relatively little change in the ratio of hydro to thermal
capacity which was 52:48 in 1955 and 48:52 in 1975. Hydropower predominated
until recently but a rapid change (thermal capacity predominating) is expected
for the future.
32.       The distribution of the new generating capacities amongst Republics
has been uneven (Table 2). Of the total capacity of 7,820 MW added between
1955 and 1975, 3,440 MW (43%) was installed in Serbia (including the auto-
nomous provinces of Kosovo and Vojvodina), 1,293 MW (17%) in Bosnia-Herzegovina,
1,551 MW (20%) in Croatia and 968 MW (12%) in Slovenia; Macedonia acquired
only 366 MW (5%) and Montenegro 205 MW (3%). These disparities reflect partly
the different primary energy resource endowments of the republics and partly
differences in their stages of economic development.
33.       The transmission system has expanded in parallel with generating
capacity (Table 3). Again the distribution was uneven. Of the 12,888 km
added, the respective shares were: Serbia 37%, Croatia 25%, Bosnia-Herzegovina
18%, Slovenia 7%, Macedonia 7%, and Montenegro 6%. This big expansion created
a national grid; in 1958 there were still 10 independent systems but by 1960
all were interconnected. Even now, however, the security, stability, and
voltage conditions of the network are unsatisfactory because of lagging
construction of 220-kV and 380-kV lines and only one 380-kV link with neigh-
boring countries exists (Bulgaria). Many lines are rapidly reaching their
rated capacity.
Dispatch
34.       The present dispatch centers are wholly inadequate for operating
the large Yugoslav network under emergency conditions. They were built in
accordance with individual design concepts and neither equipment nor methods
are standardized (except frequency control). Lack of financing and coordinated
planning caused the various centres to vary widely in level of control pos-
sibilities which is largely by mimic boards and telephone. In part, the
federal dispatch center (JUGEL; this is combined with the Serbia dispatch
center) is an exception. It was financed by a Bank loan (277-YU of 1961 for



ANNEX 1
Page 11 of 14
the Senj hydro plant) but, although repeatedly expanded to meet needs not
provided by the republican systems - centralized dialing, frequency control,
monitoring of net interchanges, status and metering indications, computer for
data acquisition and display - it represents basically the mid-sixties level
of technology and has not been extended for the control of the 380-kV system.
Temporary additions will be made which, however, will not raise the quality
of control above the present level. Replacement of practically all dispatch
facilities throughout the network is overdue.
35.       Under an agreement, dating from July 1971, JUGEL's dispatch function
is basically one of coordination and guardian of the agreement (recording
departures, operational penalties and bonuses, etc.). JUGEL has no executive
powers under this agreement (the members have to agree amongst themselves
on important matters, exchange of energy including schedules, requests for
assistance, change in switching conditions, etc.) except that it is authorized
to carry out exchange of power with foreign countries. Because, in practice,
due to the increased complexity of the system, JUGEL's dispatch role has grown
beyond the simple assumptions of the agreement - and it is expected to further
expand in the future - the present agreement is clearly out of date and will
be amended appropriately.
Some Indicators
36.       Several other indicators for the JUGEL system are of interest (see
Attachment 5).
(a) Coal consumption (Table 1) reflects the big increases in thermal
generation. Between 1960 (3.5 Mt) and 1975 (20.9 Mt) it mul-
tiplied by a factor 6. Inmproved use of higher graded coal
apparently decreased the relative amount of coal required from
1.79 kg/kWh in 1960 to 1.12 kg/kWh in 1975. The percentage
increase of oil consumption was even higher, since it started
from a negligible base (5,000 t) in 1960, but was still under
0.5 Mt in 1975. In terms of heat value, however, oil's share
rose from 0.5% to about 9%.
(b) Thermal efficiency (Table 1) reflects the increasing generating
unit sizes, i.e. benefits of the economies of scale. The heat
rate declined from 4,120 kcal/kWh (16,340 Btu/kWh) in 1960 to
2,720 kcal/kWh (10,800 Btu/kWh) in 1975. Assumptions for the
heat rates - kcal/kg - of coal used appear rather optimistic;
lignite plant of the average size used in Yugoslavia should
show an average heat rate of some 2,900-3,100 kcal/kWh.
(c) Except for generation, no detailed statistical data on manpower
are available (and would be largely unsuitable for meaningful
monitoring in view of differences in organizations, e.g.



ANNEX 1
Page 12 of 14
whether coal mine BOALs are associated with the power organiza-
tions in the composite organization). In 1975 (see Table 2)
the total workers employed in power plants, i.e. excluding
head office and special services, amounted to 11,572. The
numbers of workers operating generating plant per MW of plant
(hydro and thermal) is relatively consistent and appears
normal. It varies for hydro plant from a low 0.72/MW in
Serbia to a high 1.92/MW in Macedonia and for thermal plant
from 1.93/MW in Serbia to 2.17/MW in Slovenia.
(d) Investment in the interconnected system showed a strong upward
trend (Table 3) during 1966-70, averaging some MDin 2,600/a,
representing 2.3% of national income and 10% of total invest-
ment. During 1970-74 investments in power stagnated around
the 1966-70 level, its share of national income falling to
1.1-1.3% and of total investment to 6%. A strong upsurge
occurred in 1975 when investment in generation and trans-
mission alone rose to some MDin 11,000 (MUS$611) i.e. some
4.5 times the 1966-70 level. The distribution of investment
amongst republics has been very uneven and variable (Table 4).
The share of Serbia has declined from 53% in 1966-70 to 35% in
1973. Slovenia's share decreased also (from 11% to 5%). These
decreases were offset by rises in Croatia (from 14% to 25%)
and Montenegro (from 2% to 16%), while Bosnia-Herzegovina and
Macedonia showed little change.
(e) Expansion during 1970-1973 was mainly financed (Table 5) by
banks and internal cash generation, banks decreasing from
about 86% in 1970 to 66% in 1973; self-financing rose rapidly
from 7% (1970) to 25% (1973) because since 1973 the power
entities are required to generate internally an average of
30% of construction requirements. This requirement was
reconfirmed in the 1975 Social Agreement between republics
on Power Development until 1980.
Statistics 1965-1975
37.       The detailed 1965-1975 sectorial and JUGEL's data with respect to
sales, losses, generation and capacities are shown in Attachment 6. The
overall plant factor in the interconnected system has increased from an
average of about 47% to about 52% in 1974/75 due to the lack of reserve
capacity. Similarly the load factor has increased from an average of about
61% to 65%. This may in part be attributed to load curtailments during
the evening peak hours. Attachment 7 shows the generating plant (type,
commissioning dates, unit sizes) in operation at the end of 1975.  Except for
some small plants in Croatia and Slovenia, all plants have been commissioned
after 1950.



ANNEX 1
Page 13 of 14
Tariffs
38.       The Federal Government introduced in 1965 a tariff structure for
bulk supply from the interconnected system, applicable throughout the country.
Changes in the rates were made from time to time after the republics reached
consensus on the maximum level (individually the republics could apply rates
below the maximum); since 1973 the basic structure and maximum rates were then
published in the Federal Gazette. With the creation of the communities of
interest a slight change was introduced. The structure and maximum level is
still a matter of consensus, but the communities of interest decide on the
absolute level of the rates.
39.       The structure reflects the philosophy that electricity rates should
reflect the cost of supply which, given the mixed hydro/thermal character of
the system, can vary widely according to the season and time of the day, the
voltage level of supply and location. All rates were at two options (Step 1
and Step 2) for selection by the consumer. Step 2 has a lower kW rate than
Step 1, but a higher kWh rate. In practice, a special committee of JUGEL
makes recommendations for any changes. The power entities have recently
reached consensus on removing the second rate step and this change has already
been introduced in Bosnia-Herzegovina and Croatia. The present structure and
the rates for the bulk tariffs in each republic are shown in Attachment 8.
40.       The structure of the distribution tariffs is similar to that of
the bulk tariff (see Attachment 9). The distribution tariffs are, however,
far from unified and the value of the official schedules (Attachment 9) is
minor because it appears that, for social and other reasons, most customers
or groups of customers (new housing development) are supplied under special
contracts. The rates are now supposed to be set by the basic communities
of interest, but it appears improbable that the schedules will be made more
uniform in the near future and consistently applied (with a relatively small
number of contracts for particular cases) once the system of communities of
interest has become fully operational. A tariff study, which has been con-
tracted in November 1976, will address the problem.  No information is avail-
able (except for Croatia) on the average prices for consumer classes but it
is expected that more comprehensive data will be collected by JUGEL in the
future since most of the distribution entities are members of JUGEL.
41.       Between 1965 and 1970, there were no changes in the bulk supply
rates. Because the general price level, as measured by the cost-of-living
index, rose by 64% over this period, there was a significant decline in
the real cost of electricity to consumers. Starting in 1970, however, there
have been annual increases in rates, ranging from 10% in 1970 to 30% in
1974 and some 17% in mid 1975. As a result, bulk supply rates in 1975 were
180% above the early 1970 level, compared with a rise of some 160% in the
cost-of-living index; over this period electricity rates have kept somewhat
ahead of general inflation. The percentage increases in bulk rates have
been aplied across the board (no information is available on the details of



ANNEX 1
Page 14 of 14
the average increases in distribution rates, but the increases followed a
pattern similar to that of the bulk rates) so that relativities have remained
unchanged amongst the parts of the tariff schedule. Because the tariff
structure has also not been basically changed since its introduction in 1965
(except for the decision to remove the second step), it is unlikely that the
present schedule reflects the cost of supply to the various categories of
consumers, particularly in view of the marked changes in the pattern of con-
sumption. The tariff study now underway is expected to assess the present
distortions and to make recommendations for the necessary adjustments. For
preliminary evaluation of the tariffs in comparison with marginal costs,
reference is made to Annex 6.
Consumers' Contributions towards Investments
42.       In order to raise additional funds for investments, numerous measures
have been introduced in the various republics, as shown in Attachment 10.
They are either repayable, such as the contributions by industry, BOALs
and WOALs, or non-repayable and then take the form of a connection charge,
a surcharge on each bill (as high as 60% of the bill in Slovenia for domestic
consumers), or a metering charge. These contributions are not intended as a
permanent feature but as a temporary expedient to provide funds during the
present intensive investment period. Nevertheless in view of the magnitude
of the Yugoslav power development program, these special measures are expected
to remain in effect for a long time unless the tariffs and rates are adjusted
to reflect the cost of supply and financial requirements.

YUGOSLAVIA
SECOND POWER TRANSMISSION PROJECT
Definitions from the Constitution of the Socialist
Federal Republic of Yugoslavia (1974 ed)
1.        Associated Labor - is a Yugoslav economic and legal category denoting
economic and non-economic activities performed with socially-owned resources
and organized on the principles of self-management.
2.        Organization of Associated Labor - is a generic term for those
economic and non-economic organizations which carry out their activities with
socially-owned resources and are organized on a self-management basis.  This
is in fact what was earlier referred to as an "enterprise" (for the economic
sector) and an "institution" (for the non-economic sector).
3.        Basic Organization of Associated Labor - is a component part of an
organization of associated labor which makes up a technologically rounded whole,
an independent economic and self-managing unit which can have the character of
a legal entity. (Basic organizations of associated labor were earlier referred
to as work units, plants, or independent departments in non-economic organizations).
4.        Self-Management Agreements - are self-management enactments adopted
on terms of equality by workers in work organizations and working people in local
communities, communities of interest and other self-managing organizations, with
a view to regulating and adjusting their interests (a more rational division
of labor, pooling of resources for the pursuance of common aims, regulal:ion
of mutual relations in this cooperation, etc.). In this way the regulative
and intermediary role of the state concerning relations among working people
is diminished. A self-management agreement is only binding on those who
have signed or acceded to it.
5.        Self-Managing Communities of Interest - are communities formed by
working people directly or through their self-managing organizations and
communities for the purpose of satisfaction of specific common needs. Their
aim is to link the interests of those who render specific public services
with those who use these services. Under the Constitution, the assemblies
of self-managing communities of interest in the spheres of education, science,
culture, health and welfare are authorized to decide, together and on equal
footing with the competent assemblies of the socio-political communities
(communes, provinces, republics), on matters falling within these spheres.
There are a]Lso communities of interests in the fields of housing construction,
power production, water management, transport, etc.



ANNEX 1
Attachment 1
Page 2 of 2
6.        Work Organization - is an independent self-managing organization
of workers linked in labor by common interests and organized in basic
organizations of associated labor of which the work organization is composed
or workers linked together through the unity of labor process.
7.        Composite Organizations of Associated Labor - are a form of
organization of associated labor established through the merger of several
work organizations or basic organizations of associated labor operating
within them. Composite organizations of associated labor are also referred to
as associated enterprises.
December 1976
