ANNEX 2
Page 1 of 5
YUGOSLAVIA
SECOND POWER TRANSMISSION PROJECT
Future Development
Objectives
1.        Energy is the priority sector in the 1976-80 Development Plan and
electric power is the major component of the energy sector. The basic prin-
ciple of national policy for energy development, as enunciated in an official
statement in October 1975, is that demand should be satisfied, to the greatest
extent possible, from indigenous resources. The policy statement emphasizes
the need for coordinated development and financing of the power sector in
order to ensure economic and reliable supply of electricity throughout the
country. Completion of the 380-kV system is a key element in achieving this
objective.
Forecasts of Requirements
2.        Projected Growth of Total Energy Requirements:  According to the
1976-80 Social Plan, total primary energy requirements are expected to in-
crease at about 9%/a., or somewhat above the level of the recent past, com-
pared with a GNP growth rate of 7%/a. This implies an energy coefficient
of about 1.3, which is also rather higher than the past figure. A large
expansion of coal output is planned (from 34 Mt in 1974 to some 58 Mt in 1980),
most of it (23-25 Mt or more) in the form of lignite for thermal power genera-
tion. Demand for oil is also projected to increase sharply to 18.5 Mt in 1980
compared with 12 Mt in 1974; the great bulk of the additional requirements will
have to be met by imports, since domestic production is planned to rise by
only about I Mt (from 3.5 to 4.6 Mt). Natural gas consumption is projected
to quadruple in 1980 compared with 1974 (6 billion m3 against 1.4 billion m3);
half the 1980 requirement will be met by imports. Hydropower generation is
projected to reach 25 TWh in 1980 compared with its 1974 level of about 21 TWh.
3.        On these projections the contribution of coal to total 1980 energy
supplies (in terms of heat value) remains at the 1974 level of about 34%; the
share of oil will fall from 43% to 42% but the share of hydrocarbons as a
whole will rise from 47% to 52% because of a big increase in the share of
natural gas (from 4% to 11%). The contribution of hydropower to meeting
primary energy requirements declines from 19% to 14%. Although the share
of imported oil remains virtually unchanged (30.6% in 1980, 30.4% in 1974),
the projections imply increased dependence on imports overall, the percentage
rising from 36% to 42% because of the projected imports of natural gas, which
are expected to account for half the total 1980 natural gas requirement.



ANNEX 2
Page 2 of 5
4.        Projected Growth of Electricity Requirements:  Forecasts of elec-
tricity requirements in the interconnected system are made for the individual
republics by the JUGEL member organizations and consolidated by JUGEL in a
single national forecast. A variety of methods is used at various degrees
of sophistication involving various models for trend calculations and taking
into account forecasts for large industrial users. The forecasts are normally
made for the next 10 years but with a perspective towards the year 1990 or
even 2000. For operational and planning purposes, JUGEL is most interested
in the forecast system requirements for the next 5 and 10 years, particularly
in the former since it provides the basis for the development and investment
program embodied in the "social agreeement" for the 1976-80 five-year national
plan. JUGEL prepares two alternative forecasts, the first one by simply adding
the individual republican forecasts and the second by making an independent
forecast from the national perspective (using an exponental trend model for
the first 5 years and assuming about 8%/a for 1981-85). The first forecast
of course emphasizes the prospects in each republic and may provide some
built-in protection against excessive over or under estimates because errors
in the individual forecasts would not necessarily be in the same direction.
On the other hand each republic may be tempted to pitch its forecast on the
high side to obtain a larger share in the investment program.
5.        The republican forecasts of sales in the interconnected system,
which were amended after the preparation of the feasibility study, are shown
in Attachment 1. They constitute a considerable reduction compared with those
presented in the feasibility study but correlate reasonably with the last 5
year trends and the capabilities of the new plant to be constructed. The
forecast was reviewed and slightly reduced in cooperation with JUGEL because:
(a) it appears prudent to assume that a country forecast should be somewhat
below the sum of the republican forecasts; and (b) the use of historic plant
loading trends indicate that the incremental requirements can probably not
be met by the new plant to be constructed under adverse conditions of water
availability. For this reason, in the appraisal forecast (also shown in
Attachment 1) power import and/or export was used to provide the margin of
uncertainty (which arises because of divergencies between forecast and actual
requirements and plant construction delays).
6.        The forecast appears reasonable, taking into account (a) during
1971-74 generation did not meet requirements, (b) the economic slump of the
last 2-3 years appears to alleviate, (c) although the average growth rate of
consumption (including imports) during 1971-75 was 10%, a strong recovery
over 1976 is already apparent (+11% over the same 9 months period). A gradual
reduction in growth rate (from a maximum of 12% in 1978 to 8% by 1982) was
assumed. The average growth rate (exluding import/exports from/to other
countries) until 1982 would be 10%, i.e. equal to the 1971-75 average, and
1982 sales (70.3 TWh) would be double those in 1975 (35 TWh). Total losses in
transmission and station supply requirement of thermal plant would increase
from about 7% in 1975 to 10% by 1982 in view of the considerable increase in
station requirements of thermal plant (up to 10% of generation in lignite-
fuelled power stations) which would, by 1982, meet 65% of the energy require-
ments, compared with 43% in 1975.   The load factor would continue to be



ANNEX 2
Page 3 of 5
relatively high (66%) until 1979 because curtailments of demand are expected
to be necessary until that year; imports from other countries would be re-
quired until 1978. Exports were assumed for 1979 through 1982; in effect they
constitute a contingency in the overall energy balance which may easily be
upset by adverse hydro conditions and other causes (para. 2 above).
Balances of Energy and Capacities
7.        The forecast balances of energy and of capacities are shown in
Attachment 2 and Attachment 3 respectively. They take into account the
republican construction schedules for new generating stations, slightly ad-
justed for those plants for which delays are already now apparent. While
the energy balance appears reasonably secure, the capacity balance shows
that until 1982 the capacity margin (installed capacity less maximum demand
as the percentage of installed capacity) would remain rather low for an about
evenly mixed hydro/thermal system at an average of about 28% (1981). This
margin should allow for (a) reduction up to some 40% in head of hydro plant
(and consequent reduced capability) during the winter peak months, (b) spinning
reserve, (c) normal maintenance, and (d) forced outages. No substantial reduc-
tion of the present problems (the 1975 plant margin was 20%, hydraulicity
extremely good but nevertheless deficits occurred) can be expected until 1982
when installed capacity would more than double to 17.5 GW (8.3 GW in 1975).
Thermal plant would represent 57% of this capacity (10 GW; compared with 3.6
GW or 43% in 1975) and hydro plant 43% (7.5% GW; compared with 4.7% or 57% in
1975). The narrow margin of capabilities reiterates the importance of the
380-kV system which would provide adequate means of sharing reserves, allow
exchange of energy between republics and imports (and shared reserves) from
neighboring countries, in order to reduce deficits to a minimum.
8.        An approximate breakdown of energy (sales, generation), and maximum
demand is provided in Attachment 4. The main feature of the information is
that Serbia and Bosnia-Herzegovina will be the main producers until 1982,
Slovenia may contribute from 1978 onward, the other republics depending on
their supply to meet requirements. In order to give an idea of the magnitude
of the interrepublican exchange of energy shown in the following table, they
are also expressed as a percentage of the bulk sales in the interconnected
system in each republic.

The requirements of the republics not having sufficient own generating plant
will, in part, be met by the plant financed by these republics in other repub-
lics (i.e. under firm contractual conditions) but a portion of the republican
deficits would have to be met from the overall "pool". The actual t"pool"
contribution, i.e. the estimated "other" sales, year by year, from one republic
to the others, are difficult to calculate, because import/exports also play a
role. The above table, however, clearly indicates the interdependence of the
republics on each other and the future 380-kV system.
Facilities to be Added
9.        Attachment 5 shows all generating facilities expected to be con-
structed before 1985, comprising about 10 GW in thermal capacity and 6 GW
in hydro capacity. During the period of completion of the Project (i.e.
up to and including 1982) about 7.5 GW of the thermal plant and 2.8 GW of
hydro plant is scheduled to come on stream, i.e. more than doubling the
installed capacity (8.3 GW in 1975) to 18.7 GW.
10.       About 1,100 km of 380-kV lines and 9,600 MVA in substations is
expected to be added during 1977-1982 (the Project). Expansion in 220-kV
lines would be rather limited, some 2,000 km is expected to be added, includ-
ing reinforcement of existing lines as back-up for 380-kV lines; substation
capacity would increase by about 5,000 MW. The 110-kV system would expand
considerably by about 5,500 km of lines and 7,000 MVA in substation capacity
due to the growth of the larger cities, requiring ring systems and additional
points of supply.

Page 5 of 5
Investment Program Cost
11.       The expenditure schedule per republic, both at end-1975 prices
and taking into account price contingencies, is shown in Attachment 6. For
calculating price contingencies the following annual percentage cost increases
were taken into account.

Contingency factor       0.045    0.13    0.22    0.33    0.42    0.52    0.63
No price contingency was applied to the foreign cost of ongoing works under
firm contract prices. The overall 1976-82 investment program also shows
investments in generation in Kosovo and Vojvodina. The autonomous provinces
have their own power organizations, except for transmission, which are not
members of the Serbian composite organizations. However, the Serbian invest-
ment program includes the transmission facilities required for the autonomous
provinces. These will be constructed and owned by Elektroistok, the Serbian
work organization for transmission. Not shown are developments in distribu-
tion on which no information was available, except for Slovenia and Croatia,
the latter being only republic where the composite organization is responsible
for the development program of the whole sector.
12.       The 1976-82 country-wide power development program is estimated to
cost about GDin 200 (US$12.2 billion) representing GDin 154 billion (US$8.5
billion) at 1975 base prices and an average price contingency of 43% on the
base prices. Annual expenditures would rise from about Din 11 billion (about
MUS$610) in 1975 to some Din 39 billion (US$2.2 billion) in 1982 and average
almost Din 32 billion (US$1.8 billion) over the period.

ANNEX 3
Page 1 of 7
YUGOSLAVIA
Second 380-kV Transmission Project
Description of Project, Cost Estimates,
Allocation of Loan Amount, Implementation
Introduction
1.        The second phase (1977-1982) of the 380-kV system comprises the
necessary extensions of the first phase to meet new demands, unload existing
110-kV and 220-kV facilities which are reaching their transport capacity,
connect larger cities directly to 380 kV, strengthen the system for connecting
generating plant under construction, and increase reliability.
2.        The Project involves the construction of (a) about 1,000 km of
single circuit lines, (b) 13 new substations and the extension of 8 existing
substations (presently in construction) aggregating about 10,000 MVA in
transformer capacity, and 3 switching stations, and (c) 6 republican dispatch
centers, 1 national dispatch center (JUGEL) and 2 satellite dispatch centers
for the two autonomous provinces Vojvodina and Kosovo.
3.        The individual lines and substations are shown on the map and Attach-
ment 1. The latter provides information on the length of the individual lines,
transformer capacity to be installed in the various substations, the technical
justification (based on detailed load flow studies) and the construction sched-
ule for all individual facilities.
Lines
4.        Line towers will be of the galvanized lattice steel type, with an
average weight of about 7 t each allowing a span of 300-500 m. Phase conductors
will be ACSR 490/65 mm2, one pair per bundle. Two alumoweld shield conductors
will be used with a cross-section of 126.1 mm2. All hardware will be of the
standard type and 17 suspension insulators per string will be used (400-kV IEC
voltage level).
5.        Although originally included in the feasibility study, the 380-kV
line Obrenovac - Tuzla (125 km) has not been included in the Project because
it was considered not required before 1983 in view of delays occurring in the
construction of an aluminium plant at Zvornik. It appears probable that this
decision may have to be reconsidered, even without counting on the supply to
the aluminium plant decision in view of the overall network reliability since
with a forced outage of the Obrenovac - Ernestinovo line the connection to the
western half of the country would be lost; which is serious in view of the
fact that Obrenovac and Tuzla together will form one of the largest blocks
of power in the country (exceeding some 2,000 MW by 1980). The feasibility



ANNEX 3
Page 2 of 7
study's load flow diagram for 1980 clearly indicates the need for this line,
which would carry some 215 MW in case of a forced outage of the Obrenovac -
Ernestinovo line (as the only forced outage in the system including generation).
Substations
6.        Most substations (open air) comprise a 380-kV section and either a
220-kV or 110-kV section, except Sarajevo, Pancevo, Rijeka and Split which has
all 3 sections, and the switching stations Divaca, Obrenovac and Ravna Rijeka
which have a 380-kV section only. They will be executed in painted construction-
steel portals. The 30 transformers required are of the oil immersed forced-
oiL-cooled/forced-air-cooled, autotransformer with secondary winding (YYO d5),
executed with non-load tap changer and 3 with on-load tap changers. The main
design specifications, location and number of transformers at each location is
shown in Attachment 2.
Dispatch
7.        The present dispatch centers would be completely replaced by up-to-
date control and monitoring facilites. Three levels of dispatch are envisaged:
Area: The type of center would cover particular areas (initially
only both autonomous provinces Vojvodina and Kosovo, but similar centers
would be built in the future for regulation of many hydro plants in a single
river basin, e.g. the Drina). They would supervise and control 110-kV networks
having little effect on, or requiring continuous inputs from, the republican
or national dispatch centers.
Republican: These centers are mainly of operational importance
under normal load and operational conditions within the limits of the current
inter-republican supply agreements. They would normally control, evaluate
security and stability, and monitor the 220-kV republican network not used
for interconnection with either neighboring countries or other republics, and
the complete 110-kV networks. They would also supervise, control and monitor
all plants within each of the republican system (maintaining the necessary
spinning reserve) and regulate their own generation system but with respect
to frequency depends on the federal automatic load-frequence control system.
Federal (JUGEL): The character of this center - under normal
operating conditions - would be of a supervisory and coordinating character,
with only few operational functions of particular importance, such as the
frequency control and optimization of operation of the power system as a
whole. The entire 380-kV network and 220-kV tie lines between republics and
with neighboring countries would be controlled by the federal center. The
center would also evaluate security and stability for the 380-kV network and
initiate the necesary measures for republican action in this respect whenever
required. The center would coordinate the generating contribution of each
republic, the schedules for power exchanges between republics and between
Yugoslavia and adjacent systems, in order to ensure maximum security of supply
and optimum operation.



ANNEX 3
Page 3 of 7
8.        The facilities required for the above functions in each of the
centers would comprise the following sub-systems:
(a) Data acquisition and transfer;
(b) Communications;
(c) Real-time and off-line data processing;
(d) Data display (man-machine interface);
(e) Load and frequency control;
(f) Multi-channel carrier and microwave radiolinks;
(g) Auxiliary and emergency power.
Consulting Services
9.        Under the first transmission project, management consultants are
studying the procedures and facilities for system operation and energy manage-
ment of which the dispatch system is a part. Although (see para 18) the
republics have been assisted in designing their systems by the consultants,
and guided by a committee under the chairmanship of JUGEL, the preparation
of bid documents was largely an individual affair implying the possibility
of different solutions between republics and digression from common norms
and standard with consequent redundant cost. This policy has been changed
recently and consultants will be appointed who, in cooperation with the
committee and republican experts and taking into account the management
consultants' observations, are to prepare a comprehensive set of bid docu-
ments for all dispatch centers combined, suitable for obtaining all inclusive
bids and financing proposals from manufacturers. The cost is estimated at
US$280,000 (including contingencies) of which 90% in foreign currency, rep-
resenting about 125 man weeks.
10.       On November 24, 1976, JUGEL signed contracts for a country least-cost
power development study, to be completed in 18 months, with three Yugoslav
consultants: Energoprojekt of Belgrade (and technical consultant study coor-
dinator), Nicola Tesla of Belgrade (load-flow studies), Milaza Vidmarja of
Ljubljana (economic consultant) and Institut za Elektroprivreda of Zagreb.
The study is to be executed in accordance with terms of reference provided
by the Bank (see Attachment 3). Because the 3 consultants under the Manage-
ment Study financed by the first power transmission project are already engaged
(together with Systems Europe of Brussels, the main consultant) in setting up
an energy model for the country (including power), the present study would in
part duplicate work and the estimated cost of NDin 7.3 (US$405,000) has been
reduced by US$160,000 equivalent and MDin 4.4 would be financed by JUGEL. No
request has been submitted to the Bank for financing the additional cost of
this study by the proposed new loan. Although the consultant may request



ANNEX 3
Page 4 of 7
assistance from the present management consultant (Systems Europe of Belgium)
or another internationally reputable consultant, they have - up to now - no
experience in executing a study of similar nature. However, JUGEL will dis-
cuss with the Bank the need for further work related to the least cost devel-
opment study and also for further work in the area of the ongoing basic
management study financed by Loan 836-YU. This would include an extension of
this study for conducting a management study for each of the composite organi-
zations (see para 5.07 of the main text). Outline terms of reference for
that extension are given in Attachment 4. Similarly (see next paragraph) some
additional work may be required for further work in the area of tariffs. The
cost of the additional work in the areas of least cost development, management
and tariffs is estimated at US$720,000 for about 300 man weeks of consulting
services.
11.       Also on November 24, 1976, JUGEL signed a contract with the Instituta
Za Elektroprivredu of Zagreb for the execution of a tariff study, to be com-
p:Leted within 12 months, estimated to cost MDin 1.25 (US$69,000; similarly
reduced by US$24,000 because the institute under the first power transmission
project provides general services related to tariffs, particularly bulk
tariffs in the interconnected system). No request has been made to finance
this study by the proposed new loan. The terms of reference (Attachment 5),
however, have been amended to make specific reference to the requirement that
in executing the study the marginal costing method should also be studied.
JUGEL will discuss with the Bank the need for further work in the area of
tariffs upon completion of the study and it has been assumed that about
US$50,000 - representing about 40 man weeks would be required for further work
in this field. This amount has been included in the sum mentioned above (para
10).
Project Cost Estimate
12.       The estimated cost of the Project, as shown in Attachment 6, would
amount to Din 12.3 billion (MUS$681) including a price contingency of Din 4
billion (MUS$226) or about 50% of the base (1975) cost. The estimated foreign
exchange component of Din 4.9 billion (MUS$274) is based on contract informa-
tion for the first project when equipment contracts were largely won by
Yugoslav manufacturers and may be understated if large amounts of co-financing
- mostly in the form of suppliers' credits - are obtained, thus reducing
participation of local manufactures.
13.       The physical contingency of 5% reflects the experience during exe-
cution of the first 380-kV transmission project. The price contingencies
assumed for the overall investment program (see Annex 2, para 11) were also
applied to the Project.
14.       The expected final unit prices resulting from the above estimates
are US$35/kVA for the substations and US$190,000/km of line (varying between
US$175,000/km in Macedonia to US$200,000/km in Croatia). The substation's bay
costs would average approximately as follows:

Compared with the first project cost estimates, 1976 average unit costs are
some 2.5-3 times those in 1972 and final unit costs would be 4-4.5 times
those of 1972.
Expenditures Schedule
15.       The assumed expenditure schedule and the calculation of the price
contingency depending on this schedule are shown in Attachment 7. Initial
expenditures (1977) are expected to be low, because in accordance with
Yugoslav regulations the Project cannot be initiated before financing has
been assured, i.e. probably not before the middle of 1977.
Amount and Allocation of the Loan
16.       A Bank loan of MUS$80 has been assumed.  As for the previous loan,
the allocation of the loan to the 6 borrowers would be in accordance with the
percentage of total cost that is represented by the cost of facilities in each
republic except that, upon request of the other republics, MUS$1 has been
first allocated to Slovenia because this republic--as requested by the other
republics under the first phase--has doubled the Krsko Zagreb line to allow
for higher reliability of supply to the eastern part of the country, thus
incurring extra cost. Each republic would share the cost of the federal
(JUGEL) dispatch center pro-rata to the overall cost in each republic of the
first and second project. Attachment 8 shows the cost of facilities in each
republic, as well as the calculation of the allocation of the loan amount.

Implementation
17.       Engineering:  The arrangements for the first project are assumed
to be continued for the proposed Project. The republican enterprises have
prepared the plans for lines and substations in their own part of the Project
in accordance with the principles developed during the first project. An
overall design was done by JUGEL assisted by technical staff of the various
enterprises and consultants such as Energoprojekt of Belgrade and several
of the technical institutes that are members of JUGEL, which also made the
necessary load flow, short circuit and stability studies. The consultants
will also continue to provide - in the implementation stage - special services
of a singular nature (factory and site tests, supervisory staff for particular
erections). These arrangements worked well for the first project and are
expected to work well for the proposed Project.
18.       The republican generation and transmission enterprises, have indi-
vidually prepared plans for their dispatch facilities. Some of them were
originally obtaining assistance from Electricite de France and Societe General
pour l'Industrie (Switzerland) for generation and transmission separately.
Subsequently a commission of JUGEL and republican experts was formed which
prepared overall design in accordance with common principles. Detailed
designs were made by the republican enterprises and reviewed by the commis-
sion. The status of final design in the various-republics, however, varies
widely (from Croatia, which has already received bids on its facilities, to
Macedonia, which is in the preliminary design stage). As indicated above
(para. 9) the management consultants financed by the first loan are expected
to make recommendations on the dispatch procedures and overall design of
dispatch facilities and the measures to be taken for compatibility of the
system to be constructed in each of the republics.
19.       Requests for bids of all dispatch facilities combined is highly
desirable in view of (i) the commercially attractive aspects of this large
aggregate program (expected to cost MUS$57 with a foreign component of some
MUIS$52 at 1975 prices) for which suppliers' credits will be sought and, (ii)
standardization of equipment and methods. It is expected that JUGEL will
engage consultants for assistance in the preparation of a comprehensive
set of bid documents to assure coordination of the various plans and use of
similar and compatible equipment, covering all of the dispatch facilities
in the various republics.   This would allow the simultaneous issue of bid
documents in accordance with the above objectives by the majority, if not all,
of the republics. This task will require about 120-man weeks of consulting
services at a cost of US$280,000.
20.       Construction:  The various transmission enterprises in each republic
would be responsible for the construction of the facilities in their operating
areas, using Yugoslav construction firms or force account, as appropriate.
Most of the work under the first loan is being executed by Yugoslav firms but
some of the delays in completing the first phase are caused by unwillingness
of these firms to expand personnel which, after completion of works, cannot



ANNEX 3
Page 7 of 7
be dismissed. JUGEL and the republican enterprises responsible for the exe-
cution of the works are expected to implement adequate contractual obligations
to enforce completion in accordance with agreed schedules (see Attachment 1).
Part of the completion delays were also due to late acquisition of right-of-
way for lines. It is expected that acquisition for the full 1977-1981 program
will be initiated immediately after loan signing, if not earlier, in order to
avoid similar delays for the proposed Project.
21.       Coordination Guidelines:  As under the first power transmission
project, JUGEL and the republican enterprises would execute the proposed
Project under a set of coordinating guidelines (Attachment 10). Several
amendments have been introduced in light of past experience to allow for
improvement of the guidelines agreed to for Loan 836-YU of which the most
important are:
(a) Creation within JUGEL of a "Project Unit" responsible for
all aspects of the first and second project including all
the dealings with the Bank. The unit is expected to be
staffed with an adequate number of qualified staff (para. 6).
(b) Scheduling to ensure that line sections and substations are
completed concurrently; JUGEL to prepare critical path
diagram (para. 13).
(c) Appointment of Project Coordinator in each republic; regular
visits of JUGEL project unit chief or representative (para.
15).
(d) Time schedules and decision-making procedures for studies
(para. 18).
(e) Program reporting scheduling (para. 19).
(f) Provision of information to the Bank (para. 20).

YUGOSLAVIA
SECOND POWER TRANSMISSION PROJECT
Terms of reference for a study to determine the least-
cost power development program for the country
1.        The study should be based on the study "Development of Electric
Power Production and Consumption in Yugoslavia", taking into account the
latest available information on the subjects covered in that study (see
also World Bank letter to Federal Secretariat for Finance and JUGEL, dated
February 11, 1974). The main text should be kept as short and concise as
possible, including only such information as is necessary for clear under-
standing. Other supporting information and appropriate statistical data
together with detailed arguments and justification should be included in
an annex volume to the main report.
2.        Forecast electricity demand (MW) and energy requirements (GWh)
in the various republics and provinces, for the period 1974-1985, should
be critically examined and justified, to the extent possible, after being
brought to a common basis. These forecasts should take into account past
trends for the various consumer categories and future growth prospects as
reflected in economic development plans, allowing for possible delays for
technical or financial reasons. Amongst the factors to be considered are
trends in population, urbanization and industrial development, extensions
of public electric supplies throughout each region, and the influence of
electricity prices on demand (kW and kWh). To allow for uncertainties in
the data a probabilistic approach should be adopted, resulting in minimum,
maximum (at 95% and 5% probability respectively) and most likely forecasts.
An attempt should also be made to establish correlations that may exist
between the demand for electricity in each region and other time series
in the regional economies.
3.        The regional forecasts should be statistically combined in global
forecasts for the country (minimum, maximum and most likely).
4s.       The study should review the existing electrical facilities in the
country and those under construction (including those definitely contracted
but not yet in physical execution), including a general description of the
equipment, available capacities, load flow, voltage level, short circuit and
stability considerations, etc. Completion of the 380-kV transmission system
under World Bank Loan 836-YU should be taken into account.
5.        The next step should be the establishment of a suitable number
cf feasible alternative development programs (including transmission) to
meet with equal reliability and to a given standard of risk, in combination
with existing plant and plant under construction, the most likely power



ANNEX 3
Attachment 3
Page 2 of 4
demand requirements as projected under para 3 above. As a first stage the
combination of programs defined in the various regions may be used. This
combination, however, may cause either too high or too low overall reliability
(e.g. show excess capacities and energies) and, if so, a further alternative
should be established which - within the general (but not detailed) scope of
the regional programs - would meet the forecast demand at an adequate level of
reliability, which should be defined and justified; this reliability standard
should be used throughout the study. The alternative thus obtained should be
designated Alternative 1 (basic development program). Further alternative
development programs should be established, taking into account the feasible
technical options, on the basis of-sound engineering judgment but ignoring
purely regional considerations or local preferences. The installation of ad-
ditional nuclear plant should be considered in establishing the alternatives,
and pumped storage plant may also be inserted at different points of time in
various alternatives as appropriate. The method to be used for the assess-
ment of the capacity and energy balances of the various alternatives is left
to the discretion of the consultants but a concise outline of the method and
the computer program used should be annexed to the study.
6.        The capital cost estimates should be based on the prevailing market
conditions, with due regard for local conditions in respect of transport,
available materials, labor and services. Local taxes (including taxes on
income) and import duties as well as subsidies should be excluded from the
estimates, but indicated separately. For each of the main items in the alter-
native development programs the following is to be included in the annex to
the main report: (a) a technical description of the item together with a
rough outline or single line diagram of the facility, on which the basic
parameters of the facility are also stated; (b) a summarized cost estimate
(excluding interest during construction and contingencies for future price
rises, but including physical contingencies), separated into (i) civil works,
(ii) electro-mechanical works, (iii) engineering, consultants, and admin-
istration (i.e., company cost for design and construction), (iv) contingen-
cies, and, separately, (v) taxes and duties. Cost estimates prepared by an
enterprise should be reviewed by the consultant for a cross-check of prices
and amended as appropriate.
7.        The above approach assumes that after 1985 further system develop-
ment will be the same for all alternatives. If this approximation is ex-
pected to introduce appreciable errors (e.g., because the plant mix might vary
considerably after 1985 for the different alternatives), the forecast period
should be extended to 1990, assuming a reasonable growth rate not exceeding
10%. The various alternative power developments should then be extended to
meet the additional requirements in such a way that the plant mix by 1990
should be approximately the same for all alternatives.
8.        The different programs (Alternative 1, Alternative 2, etc., or
similar designations) thus established should be compared with each other
using the discounted cash flow method to determine the least cost (capital
and recurrent) development program for meeting the forecast requirements.



ANNEX 3
Attachment 3
Page 3 of 4
Recurrent operational and maintenance costs - except fuel - not common to all
alternatives should be estimated (and justified) for each of the alternative
development schemes using cost levels at the date used for assessing the
capital cost, not taking into account any expected future increases due to
cost of living or price increases, and excluding taxes and interest during
construction. For fuel, the cost to the economy should be used, i.e., taxes
should be deducted from, and any direct or indirect subsidies should be added
to, the normal price paid for fuel by the enterprises.
9.        For the conversion of the foreign costs (capital and recurrent)
associated with each alternative development it is suggested that an exchange
rate of Din 15 = US$1 should be used 1/.
10.       For the discounted cash flow comparison present values should be
calculated using discount rates in the 6-15% range (see sensitivity considera-
tions below in paragraph 12). Residual values of capital costs, using sinking
fund assessment of the residual values at the end of the discount period,
should be deducted to obtain the true present values of each alternative devel-
opment scheme. For purposes of decision-making the opportunity cost of capital
should be assumed to be 10%. Although the presentation of the present values
is to be left to the discretion of the consultant, a useful way of presenting
the result of the comparisons would be to calculate net present values (the
present values at the various discount rates of Alternative 2, Alternative 3,
etc., are each subtracted from those of Alternative 1), and provide a set of
graphs in which the net present value is shown on the vertical axes and the
discount rate on the horizontal axes. In these graphs the basic alternative,
which would have zero present value at all discount rates, would be represented
by the horizontal axes.
11.       The process of "planting up", in accordance with sound engineering
juLdgment, is now repeated for both the "maximum" and the "minimum" forecasts,
assuming that the "initial" plant decision (i.e., the next step in planting
up for a group of plants beyond plant already contracted) cannot be reversed.
Beyond the next step further variations within an alternative could be envis-
aged (including advancing or postponing plant included in the alternative; the
variatios could be designated Alternative la - maximum forecast -, Alternative
lb - minimum forecast - etc., or similar). Additional sets of true present
values, net present values, and graphical presentations of the latter are
obtained, indicating the sensitivity to "growth rate".
1/   A rate of exchange, appropriate at the time of creating the study, will
be used.



ANNEX 3
Attachment 3
Page 4 of 4
12.       Further sensitivity analysis would be required with respect to:
(a) The foreign exchange rate (present rate plus and minus 10%).
(b) Capital cost of steam plant, reflecting the uncertainties
in the estimate of physical contingencies.
(c) Capital cost of hydro plant, reflecting the uncertainties
in the estimate of physical contingencies.
(d) Fuel cost (especially fuel oil, for which a range of values
from, say, US$40 to US$80 per ton could be used in view of
the uncertainties).
(e) Total steam operating and maintenance cost (as estimated
by consultants).
(f) Suitable and various combinations of (a) through (e).
13.       The consultants should make recommendations based on the study,
with due allowance for the results of the sensitivity analysis, regarding
the least-cost power development program, particularly with respect to the
first group of plants to be completed as the "next stage" in this program.
Because several alternatives may be extremely close together in present value,
the relevant group of plants should be considered in the light of the regional
decisions already made with respect to plant construction. Justified slight
adjustments of the "least cost-scheme" to take account of these decisions
should be recommended, if necessary, in order to obtain a development scheme
that not only constitutes the least-cost development in power for Yugoslavia
but would also adhere (so far as can be justified within the uncertainties
of the data on which the study was made) to the existing regional development
schemes.
14.       The consultants should prepare file documentation in such a way that
quarterly updating of the information (and refinement of the input data) would
be a routine matter, facilitating annual revision of the recommendations.

YUGOSLAVIA
Outline of Terms of Reference for Extension of Management Study
Note:     These terms of reference should be considered as preliminary.  Under
Loan 836-YU the management consultants appointed by JUGE1 would study and make
recommendations for the management including information systems, administra-
tion and accounting procedures of the 12 borrowers. One of the 12 borrowers,
namely Elektrostopanstvo, Skopje, would undertake financial performance under
the proposed loan also and the remaining 11 are constituent organizations of
the composite organizations in other republics. In order to avoid any dupli-
cation, it would be necessary to treat the proposed management study as an
extension to the study under Loan 836-YU, building on the earlier study and
retaining all its essential features.
Given below is a broad list of tasks for the proposed study, each
of which has two phases. During the first phase, the current systems and
procedures would be reviewed, problem areas identified, and modified or new
systems and procedures would be developed. During the second phase, the
modified or new systems would be implemented and training programs would be
developed and initiated. For training purposes, the consultants should
identify specific objectives of training, field of training for each level
and category of staff, with estimated numbers of trainees; develop training
guidelines, schedules, and course materials for the entire program of training;
estimate costs of training; and prepare a time schedule for implementation.
Task I
Review existing organization, identify problem areas and make recom-
mendations for an appropriate organization structure in which the constituent
organizations are suitably integrated in the composite organization in such
a way that the composite organization is enabled to fulfill its designated
role of centralized planning including financial planning while at the same
time the constituent organizations retain their decentralized functions
and freedom of operations in accordance with the Self Management Agreement.
Assist the composite organizations to establish the recommended organization
structure, and initiate a suitable program of staff training.
Task II
Review present accounting systems including practices related to
closing of accounts and preparation of consolidated accounts and identify
problems areas. Develop modified accounting systems geared to the timely
preparation of consolidated accounts of the composite organization which
would form the basis for a sound management information system. The



ANNEX 3
Attachment 4
Page 2 of 2
consolidated accounts will comprise the accounts of all entities in the group
and will eliminate all interorganization transactions. Assist in the prepara-
tion of procedure manuals and forms and in the implementation of modified
accounting systems and procedure. Initiate program of training supervisory
level personnel and all members of accounting staff in the details of the
new systems.
Task III
Review financial reporting and budgetary control procedures and
develop and implement new or modified financial reporting and budgeting
control systems and procedures in tespect of both investment (construction)
accounting, budgeting and reporting and operation accounting, budgeting and
reporting. The management information systems should be such as to facilitate
proper financial planning of investment and operations, timely review of
performance against budgetary allocations and timely initiation of corrective
measures to secure the full implementation of the plan of investment and
operations. Train staff during the implementation of the new system and get
under way a continuing plan for training of staff.
May 1977

UNION OF YUGOSLAV ELECTRIC POWER INDUSTRY
Terms of Reference for the Study
"Basic Principles of the Tariff System
for Electricity sales in Yugoslavia"
I.
The Assembly of the Union of Yugoslav Electric Power Industry has
decided to engage a research institute to elaborate the basic principles of
the tariff system for electricity sales. The purpose of the Study is to give
solutions which will indicate the necessary conditions for the uniform devel-
opment of the market, the rational use of the existing electric power facili-
ties and the construction of new facilities, covering all costs that are
incurred. The Study should define the basic principles of the tariff system
for the sales of electricity, promoting rational usage and providing reliable
supply of higher quality than at present, including proposals for a tariff
structure and rates for the power industry in each of the republics and
autonomous provinces and the power industry in the country as a whole, based
on principles of marginal costing as set forth in the attached outline of
terms of reference pages 7, 8 and 9.
II.
The Study should analytically elaborate the following points:
1.        Review briefly historic development of consumption of electricity
for 1958-1974 and determine the consumption characteristics with respect to:
-    annual trends;
-    annual structural changes;
-    hourly demand (billed);
-    utiliztion of billed demand;
-    analysis of the influence of the customers operation mode and
possibilities of their adjustment to power system generation
possibilities and constant annual consumption;
-    daily load diagrams (for the third Wednesday in each month
and the first Sunday after the third Wednesday);
-    seasonal variations of characteristic consumption, par-
ticularly of:
(a) directly connected electricity consumers;
(b) consumers on the distribution network.
The analysis has to cover all Republics individually and the country as a whole.



ANNEX 3
Attachment 5
Page 2 of 9
2.        The Study should give analysis and critical assessment of the devel-
opment of electricity prices for characteristic consumers such as:
-    consumers directly connected to the transmission network;
-    consumers in distribution networks:
(a)  for consumers at the high voltage (10 kV) with
installed power of 200 kW and annual utilization
of 2,400, 3,600, 4,800 4nd 6,000 hours;
(b) for domestic consumption with an annual consumption
of 600, 1,200, 8,000 and 12,000 kWh.
For all of the above-mentioned consumers, except domestic with consumption of
600 and 1,200 kWh/year, average prices should be assessed according to the
ratios: VT:NT 1/ - 60:40, 50:50 and 40:60. Ratios between higher and lower
seasons should be taken in proportion to the month of seasonal application.
3.        In the Study, the analysis should be made of the historic structural
pattern of electric energy sales for the years under review according to the
following elements:
-    power demand
-    active energy
-    reactive energy
The analysis should be made separately for direct consumers and bulk distribu-
tion, and according to groups of consumers for distribution enterprises
indicated in Section II/I.
(Data elaboration and comparative analysis should be made for the years:
1958, 1964, 1966 and 1975).
4.        In addition to the above, the analytical part of the Study, should
give a brief survey of the development of the tariff system for sale of
electricity during 1957-1975 and, based on the above analyses determine the
degree of influence of the tariff system on consumption and its efficiency in
achieving its objectives during this period, particularly for consumption on
the transmission network and in distribution.
1/   VT = high tariff rate) in existing tariff schedules.
NT = low tariff rate)



ANNEX 3
Attachment 5
Page 3 of 9
The analysis should be made for the following distribution enter-
prises:
S.R. Bosnia and Herzeogina  -  Valter Peric Sarajevo
S.R. Montenegro              -  SRCG - Titograd
S.R. Croatia                 -  Zagreb and Split
S.R. Macedonia               -  Elektromakedonija, Skopje
S.R. Slovenia                -  DES, Ljubljana
S.R. Serbia                  -  Belgrade and Elektrovojvodina, N. Sad
A.P. Kosovo                   -  Elektro Kosovo - Pristina
III.
5.        The Study shall make a comparative survey and analysis of the tariff
systems, the approach to tariff framing and the price levels in relation to
consumer groups and power supply components (production, transmission, distri-
bution) in several countries in Europe e.g. France, Italy, Austria, England,
Sweden, Poland, CSSR etc.
IV.
In view of the importance of the study and the necessity to clarify
individual theoretical questions, the Study should determine (in a chapter on
the Theoretical and Economic Basis of Tariff Systems for the Sale of Elec-
tricity):
-    the role and tasks of the tariff system as a stimulating economic
mechanism encouraging rational construction and use of the power
facilities (generation, transmission and distribution);
-    the characteristics of electricity cost components with respect
to their division into fixed and variable costs, as well as
demand (kW), all expenses, including expenses for labor, power
operation, particularly with respect to application of the
Yugoslav power system;
-    interrelationship of rates for kW, kWh and kWhr within the
framework of the total expense for electricity particularly
with respect to application in the Yugoslav power system;
-    methods of electricity costs allocation according to voltage
levels;



ANNEX 3
Attachment 5
Page 4 of 9
methods and criteria for customer groupings in view of the
cost of further development and the use of electricity
(taking into account technological characteristics and
demand characteristics of consumers);
tariffication according to the time of day, seasons and
level of consumption.
(Particularly, reference is to be made to the relation between tariff steps as
well as degressive and progressive trends in the system of block rates).
V.
6.        Based on the forecast sales, the characeristics of these sales, the
consumption patterns and the planned developments in generation, the Study
should determine and elaborate the following aspects:
-    objectives of a tariff policy based on the characteristics
of the prospective development of consumption and generation;
-    a basis for the tarriff policy for rational use of electricity
in the period 1976-1985.
-    proposal of a rational structured model of the tariff system
that will ensure realization of the objectives of the tariff
policy.
The tariff system model must particularly give answers to the following ques-
tions:
(a) selection of a tariff system taking into account optimal
operation of the power system;
(b) consumers' grouping according to voltage levels, purpose
of consumption, or combination of these;
(c) assessment of billed peak demand (duration of peak load,
peak period during the day, month and the year to be billed,
manner of payment etc.);
(d) methods of allocation of electricity capacity expansion
expenses to voltage levels, demand (kW), active (kWh) and
reactive energy (kVArh);
(e) determination of tariff ratios in the tariff steps for the
different seasons of the year and times of day;



ANNEX 3
Attachment 5
Page 5 of 9
(f) active energy tariffication (according to the time of day,
seasons, zones and level of consumption);
(g) reactive energy tariffication (reactive power, determination
of reactive energy in relation to utilization time, total
quantities or excessive consumption, monthly or at any given
moment, etc.);
(h) tariffs for special large consumers, for consumers able to
adapt their consumption to the system requirements, for
domestic consumption, transportation, public lighting and
agriculture;
(i) tariff measures in the event of electricity shortages
(expected);
(j) give practical examples indicating effects as well as costs,
both from the standpoint of the power system and of consumers.
7.        JUGEL and its members will make available to the institute by a cer-
tain date, to be defined after contract signing, all data which the institute
requires for elaborating:
-    power and financial data for annual electricity sales on
the transmission network and in distribution for the
required years including losses and station supply (kW,
kWh, in HT and LT, Din and para/kwh);
-    monthly electricity sales for the required years (kW and
kWh);
-    daily load diagrams for characteristic days in a month for
all the months in the required years:
(a) for selected direct consumers (individually and
totals);
(b) for selected distribution enterprises;
-    average bulk electricity prices for:
(a) direct consumers;
(b) distribution enterprises;
-    average electricity prices for selected characteristic consumption
groups on the distribution network;

level of electricity prices in selected foreign power
companies;
forecast power and technical data for the years 1976-1980
and preliminary forecasts up to 1985 for the planned genera-
tion status according to type and planned generation and
consumption patterns. For 1985 global data will be provided.

OUTLINE OF TERMS OF REFERENCE FOR A STUDY OF MARGINAL COST
STRUCTURE TARIFF AND RATES
Objective
1.        The purpose of this outline is to propose an approach for the
derivation of a tariff structure which reflects as closely as possible the
costs to the economy of meeting the demand for electricity subject to any
constraints imposed by the revenue requirements of the utility to meet the
financial objectives and by any income distribution objectives (e.g. subsi-
dizing poorer consumers) which the electricity tariffs may be required to
serve.
2.        Suggested guidelines for the execution of the study are as follows:
Analysis of Cost Structure
(a) Definition of Cost. The relevant costs are not the financial
costs to the utility of expanding and operating its system to
meet the demand but the costs to the economy, as measured in
terms of the opportunity cost of the resources allocated to
the production of electricity. Therefore, shadow prices
rather than actual prices to the utility should be used where
necessary as a measure of cost. In addition, any taxes on the
utility's inputs should be deducted and subsidies added back.
(b) Relevant Costs. The costs to be considered by the tariff
designer are the marginal costs associated with the expansion
of the generation, transmission and distribution capacities and
the operation of existing and planned capacities during the
planning period (between 5-10 years). These costs should be
further defined according to geographical location, time of
day and season and voltage levels. This would require due
attention to the daily and seasonal variations in forecast
system demand and, to the extent possible, in forecast demands
of various consumer classes. Much of the required information
for this purpose may have to be specially collected e.g. by
taking substation readings, by enquiring about shift-working,
seasonal work-patterns, etc. and by statistical analysis of
available load curves. Estimates of marginal costs should
be based on the development program for the period considered
and the proposed operating regime for the existing and planned
capacities.
(c) Energy Cost. When demand does not come up against the system
capacity constraint (allowing for the reserve margins set to
maintain security of supply) marginal costs would be simply
marginal running costs grossed up to allow for losses at the



ANNEX 3
Attachment 5
Page 8 of 9
different voltage levels and, where relevant, in different
regions. The relevant losses are incremental losses, even
if they can be estimated only approximately, not average
losses.
(d) Capacity Cost. At periods when an increase in generation
would bring the system up against the security constraint,
the marginal cost of meeting demand would include the addi-
tion to all system costs resulting from adding to generation
capacity and/or storage, transmission and distribution in
order to provide the increased supply with an unchanged
probability of failure.
(e) Adjustment for Power Factors. It would also be necessary to
study the incremental costs attributable to poor power factors
for those type of consumer (the larger ones) who can be ex-
pected to improve their power factor in response to suitable
tariff incentives. The most important cost to concentrate on
is the extra MVA capacity required to cater for these poor
power factors.
(f) Evaluation of Existing Tariffs. The existing tariff structure
and rate and any alternatively designed tariff structure and
rates should be compared with the marginal cost-based tariffs.
Large differences between these are an indication that the
existing system or any alternatively designed system is given
the wrong price signals to consumers. Particular attention
should be paid to the types of metering in use and the quality
of meter maintenance, since this would provide some guidance
to what types of tariffs are available. In addition, the mode
by which the gap between the existing and proposed tariffs
should be addressed. That is, the tariff designer must deter-
mine whether the existing tariffs are difficult to administer,
conducive to disputes or conducive to fraud.
(g) Proposed Marginal Cost Based Tariffs. With the information thus
collected, it should be possible to make a first set of proposals
for a marginal cost based tariff system and to assess the effects
of changing the existing tariff system to such a system so that
the incentives (and disincentives) it provides to consumers
correspond more closely to the schedule of marginal costs derived.
These proposals would consist of a classification of consumers
and a tariff (or set of tariffs) for each class, together with
any connection charges and charges for reactive power which can
be justified. They would have to be modified as necessary to
take account of the following:



ANNEX 3
Attachment 5
Page 9 of 9
(i) Any strong arguments for slanting electricity tariffs
because of price distortions (e.g. of substitutes for
public electricity supply) elsewhere in the economy which
are likely to affect electricity sales in the absence of
such slanting.
(ii) The need for the utility to secure satisfactory financial
performance. This would generally mean, if the revenues
based on marginal cost pricing are insufficient, raising
those elements of the proposed tariffs where demand is
least specific.
(iii) Any income and redistribution objectives (e.g. through
subsidizing poorer consumers) to which electricity tariffs
may be required to contribute.
(iv) Practicality and cost. There is a trade-off between the
cost of administering any tariff structure (which depends
largely on the cost of metering and billing) and the
extent to which it can reflect the structure of marginal
costs. Complex metering, for example, is likely to be
worthwhile only for larger consumers, since the response
of small consumers to the extra incentives (or disincen-
tives) it offers would not justify the expense.
(h) Modifications of the strict "marginal cost" tariffs should be
designed to cause the least possible disturbance to the incen-
tives they offer to consumers in relation to the costs of
supplying them.
(i) Any new tariff schedule should provide for the automatic
adjustment of rates in the event of increases in the price
of fuel for the utility's steam-electric generating plant.
The price level above which this fuel-adjustment clause comes
into effect should be defined.
Implementation of New Tariffs
(j) The consultant should give careful consideration to the problem
of transition from the old to any alternative new tariffs pro-
posed in order to make the changeover more acceptable. He
should prepare a plan, in close consultation with the power
utility officials for the implementation of the new tariff
structures in stages, possibly introducing some of the parts
of the new tariffs as options in the first instance. The plan
should include provision for publicity, metering acquisition
and installation, meter reading and billing, and all necessary
staff training to ensure a smooth transmission to the new
system.
May 1977

YUGOSLAVIA
Second Power Transmission Project
Coordination Guidelines
General
1.        Coordinating Activities.  The republican power organizations (enter-
prises) executing the 380-kV transmission system, directly and through their
respective republican Composite Organization of Associated Labor (republican
coordinating organization) have designated JUGEL as coordinator of the Proj-
ect, responsible for coordinating the following activities
committee work
engineering
procurement
construction
studies
relations with the Bank
2.        Executing Agencies, Ownership, General Consultancy.  Fifteen enter-
prises are involved in the Project as executing agencies for the construc-
tion on behalf of six borrowers (one for each Republic). The transmission
facilities will be located in their respective operating areas and each
enterprise will construct and own the facilities located in its area. Each
enterprise, through JUGEL, will contract with suppliers for equipment and
materials which are to be located in its operating area. JUGEL will contract
for consultant services of common nature on behalf of the enterprises involved.
3.        Standardization.  Characteristics of transmission equipment and
facilities should be common (identical to those used in the first phase, but
not necessarily identical among the enterprises) in all enterprises in the
interest of uniform operation, standardization, economy and efficient main-
tenance.
4.        Loan Actions.  JUGEL would also be the agency for disbursement
actions and repayment of the Bank loan.
Coordination Committee
5.        Organization.  The enterprises, either individually or as groups,
have agreed to continue to provide a qualified representative to the co-
ordinating committee formed during the execution of the first phase of the



ANNEX 3
Attachment 10
Page 2 of 5
transmission project. This committee will continue to operate at least through-
out the construction period of the Project. The committee will be chaired by
JUGEL and would meet as frequently as necessary.
6.        JUGEL Project Unit.  There would be a permanent operating staff, i.e.
"Project Unit", within JUGEL for all day-to-day business on behalf of the exe-
cuting agencies and the borrowers and for all aspects of the relationship with
the Bank under the first and second transmission projects. The Chief of the
Project Unit would act as coordinator vis-a-vis the Bank under the overall
responsibility of JUGEL's General Manager. The Project Unit's organization,
number of staff, and qualifications of staff should be such that, to the satis-
faction of the Bank, all activities - organizational, technical administrative,
financial, statistical study and legal - are executed promptly and compre-
hensively.
Engineering
8.        Design.  The committee would establish the basic parameters for the
design of the transmission facilities after taking into account the wishes and
interest of the enterprises. To the extent that the enterprises and JUGEL do
not have the expert personnel needed for this purpose, they would seek the
assistance of qualified consultants whose experience would be reviewed by the
Bank.
Procurement
9.        Bid Documents.  The bid documents for materials and equipment, which
are subject to final approval by the Coordinating Committee would be prepared
by JUGEL, if necesary assisted by staff of the enterprises, and qualified
consultants whose experience would be reviewed by the Bank. The general terms
of the documents would be prepared to reflect Yugoslav practices and inter-
national practice based on the principle that equipment will be purchased on a
competitive international basis; 1/ to the extent necessary the general terms
would be amended if - for equipment not to be financed by the Bank - restricted
bidding or direct procurement would be required. In any event, however, docu-
ments would stipulate that the basic unit prices would be the same for all
enterprises with variations being allowed only for delivery and similar
expenses.
10.       Quantities.  The general principle would be that, in the interest of
simplicity and economy, a single order would be placed for the needs of all
1/   The Bank's "Guidelines for Procurement" and the Bank supervision over
bidding documents, award of contracts, etc. will apply for the materials
and equipment to be financed by the Bank loan.



ANNEX 3
Attachment 10
Page 3 of 5
enterprises. However, the enterprises under the coordination of JUGEL after
determining the required delivery schedules, would have to ascertain that
there would be adequate competition for such large orders to be delivered on
time, and use more than one order if there is reasonable doubt. The Coordi-
nating Committee, under the direction of JUGEL, would verify the individual
quantities required by each enterprise and the aggregate amount. Having
obtained the agreement of the enterprises, appropriate bids 1/ would then
be called. The bidding documents would sitpulate the portion of the total
quantity being purchased by each enterprise, and that the respective enter-
prise would itself, through JUGEL, make a contract with the supplier for its
portion of the total amount necessary and allowed for under the bidding docu-
ments. JUGEL would coordinate the signing of the individual contracts on
the same date. The bidding documents would also stipulate that each enter-
prise would be responsible for payment of its portion under the general and
particular terms of payments applicable.
11.       Bid Evaluation.  Bids would be evaluated by the Coordinating Com-
mittee in accordance with the provisions to that effect in the bid docu-
ments 1/ with resulting recommendations to the enterprises. The Coordi-
nating Committee would review the enterprises' proposals about making the
award. 1/ 2/ JUGEL would advise the firm winning the award and arrange that
appropriate contracts would be entered between the winner and the enterprises.
12.       Contract Administration.  The contracts so awarded would be admin-
istered by JUGEL on behalf of the Coordinating Committee and the enterprises
insofar as technical matters, schedules and the like are concerned. JUGEL
would also be responsible for pre-award communications with prospective sup-
pliers on matters concerning technical and other interpretations of the
contract. Post-award communications concerning contract formallities, in-
cluding payment, would be carried out by JUGEL on behalf of the enterprises.
Construction
13.       Scheduling.  The Coordinating Committee and the borrowers will be
responsible for preparing a schedule for the Project in its entirety and by
enterprise. All actions to be taken and their timing will be detailed in
this schedule. The schedule should ensure that line sections of a single
1/   For the materials and equipment to be financed by the Bank, see note 1/
of page 3.
2/   Proposals for awards for Bank financed materials and equipment would be
forwarded to the Bank for its concurrence prior to notifying the firm
winning the award.



ANNEX 3
Attachment 10
Page 4 of 5
line will be completed at the same time together with substations to be
supplied by (or supplying to) these lines. For this purpose priority ranking
would be reflected in the schedule at its inception. JUGEL would (a) prepare
a critical path diagram reflecting the agreed schedule, (b) coordinate with
the various suppliers schedule of deliveries to the various enterprises, con-
sistent with the overall schedule, and (c) update the critical path diagram
not: less than once in 3 months. For this, standardized information sheets
will be introduced for monthly completion by the executing agencies through
their respective coordinator for the Project.
14.       Execution.  Each enterprise is responsible for the execution of all
works within its service territory. The plans of each enterprise for con-
struction and necessary changes would be reviewed by JUGEL and the Coordinating
Committee in the interest of proper coordination and adherence to the schedules.
15.       Supervision.  Supervision of construction would be the responsibility
of each of the enterprises in accordance with common standards reached by
JUGEL and the Coordinating Committee but which include in any case appointment
of a Project coordinator (if possible, the technical representative on the
Coordinating Committee) in each Republic. Supervision by qualified consulting
firms would be encouraged. The JUGEL Project Unit chief of his representative
would visit the Project coordinator in each Republic at least once every 4-6
weeks for discussions and additional collection of information. JUGEL would
revise critical path information accordingly and initiate actions with con-
tractors in close cooperation with the local Project coordinator(s) to speed
up work in the critical path itself.
16.       Commissioning.  In general, arrangements for testing and commis-
sioning of the facilities would be devised by JUGEL and the Coordinating
Committee and, as each phase of the Project became ready for commissioning,
arrangements in accordance therewith made with the respective enterprise,
which would be responsible for actually carrying them out assisted by a
JUGEL representative. The use of consultants to assist in this work would
be encouraged.
Studies
17.       Execution.  The execution and timing of all studies would be the
responsibility of JUGEL, in close cooperation with the Coordinating Committee,
for which it would contract with experienced consulting firms or individuals
under agreed terms of reference. 1/
1/   Subject to Bank concurrence in the case of Bank financed studies and
subject to Bank review in the case of other studies bearing on the
Project.



ANNEX 3
Attachment 10
Page 5 of 5
18.       Implementation.  Any completed study (or part of it constituting
comprehensive treatment of a subject) and the recommendations in it would
be reviewed by the enterprises immediately and opinions should reach JUGEL
within two months or less, depending on the urgency of reaching decisions.
Within three months (or less) after completion of a study JUGEL would present
to the Coordinating Committee members a report outlining the concensus reached
and the outstanding issues to be solved, at the same time calling for a
special decision meeting. During this meeting the Coordinating Committee
would decide on issues within its decision making prerogatives. For general
studies (e.g., Management, Least-Cost Development, Tariffs) or their parts,
outside its decision making prerogatives, the Committee would transmit the
study, together with its conclusions and recommendations, to the Composite
Organization of Associated Labor in each Republic for final action.
Relations with the Bank
19.       Progress Reporting.  Within one month after the closing of each
quarter, all enterprises would provide such information to JUGEL as is required
under the Bank's reporting instructions so that the relevant reports reach the
Bank not later than two months after the closing of each quarter.
20.       Communications.  JUGEL would be fully responsible for the dialogue
with the Bank under both loans on all aspects of the projects, diligently
keeping the Bank informed of all changes, decisions and other matters af-
fecting the Project, the agreements with the Bank and the organizational
framework within which the entities operate.
May 1977

Details of Republican Organizations
2.        The basic management structure is common to all power organizations
and is illustrated in Attachment 1, which would apply to an organization
covering both generation and transmission. Depending on the size of the BOAL
or the work organization several departments are either not required or are
combined within the general scheme. Organizational differences between BOAL
may be considerable, but, of course, less so for work organizations. In all
cases, however, the Workers' Council (from BOAL through work organization to
composite organization elected in accordance with the relevant Self-management
Agreements by workers), acts as the top policy-making body in much the same
fashion as the board of directors of a corporation in a non-socialistic
country. The Workers' Council appoints the General Manager, who is chief
executive. It should be realized however, that a basic difference exists:
the Yugoslav orgnization rather takes the form of an inverted pyramid with
the workers in the upper level and the executives at the lower point. Each



ANNEX 4
Page 3 of 4
organization, depending on its size, is subdivided into working units, each
of which has its own Workers' Council and Manager, to assure workers' partici-
pation in management throughout the organization.
3.        In Attachment 2 an effort has been made to represent the sometimes
extremely complicated organization of the sector in each republic in common
terms. Summarizing this attachment shows the following:

4.        Although management and staff of the 18 organizations (and the con-
sultants assisting them) which would participate in the Project are competent
to design, construct and operate the facilities for which they are responsible,
the problems of coordination and cooperation on a country level have only been
partially solved, which is illustrated by the following:
(a) The construction of the 380-kV system had a hesitant start
in 1972/73, as expected, but momentum did not pick up until
about 1975 and the objective of completing related facili-
ties at the same date, is not being met, e.g., timing for
lines and substations for what presently is probably the
most important connection in the country, is as follows:
Subst
Line
1         1.6.76        1.1.76          1.6.77          1.1.77
Obrenovac   , v          |              |                  l           I
.I            I               llII

_  I  I        l               l~~~~~I        I
Tuzla                    I                                             i
Mostar                   i              l                              I
l             l              l               l



ANNEX 4
Page 4 of 4
(b) A Bank loan of US$70 million, made in 1975 for the 450-MW Buk
Bijela hydropower station in Bosnia-Herzegovina, failed to
become effective because the Federal Government could not
obtain ratification of the Guarantee Agreement due to an
inter-republican riparian dispute over the use of the waters
at the proposed station site, which arose subsequent to the
Bank's appraisal and consideration of the loan by the Board.
A power component (34-MW hydro) is also included, in the Ibar
multipurpose water project (Loan 777-YU; 1971) which is sched-
uled for completion in 1977. Also, a Bank sector mission
carried out an extensive review of the Yugoslav energy sector
in October 1975.
(c) Although an agreement has been reached between republics on
the power development program until 1981 (Federal Gazette of
March 21, 1975) canvassing for financing is still largely an
individual affair (with little or no common policy for raising
consumers' contribution for local costs) and only recently
an internationally oriented effort has been undertaken for
cofinancing for the second 380-kV project.
(d) In the field of dispatch which - technically and financially -
is extremely sensitive to compatibility and standardization
of equipment, each republic was proceeding independently
(although coordinated in respect of basic requirements) for
procuring equipment.
(e) Management Consultants to advise JUGEL and the borrowers under
the first power transmission project were only engaged in mid
1976, some 3 years late, thus not providing the republics
amongst themselves and together vis-a-vis the Bank, an oppor-
tunity to open a dialogue on implementation of recommendations,
well in advance of the appraisal of the present Project.
JUGEL's Organization
5.        JUGEL's organizational chart is shown in Attachment 3.  For a des-
cription of its operations reference is made to Annex 1 para 17. One of its
weaknesses has been lack of highly qualified and experienced staff that could
act in an authoritative manner as counterpart to republican experts, local and
foreign consultants, and the Bank.
6.        Because it is not the policy of the power organizations to adjust
annually the expansion program as stipulated in the National Plan, in order
to monitor the financial requirements, annual information would be useful as
shown in Attachment 4 as well as information that would allow future selection
(lack of historic data does not allow making the selection now) of monitoring
indicators appropriately representing measures of performance with respect to
efficiency and progress (Attachment 5).

Plant and Investment Information to be
Included in the Last Quarterly Progress
Report of Each Year
(a)       Existing generating capacity (at year end) of members of JUGEL
(similar to Annex 1, Attachment 7).
(b)       Detailed generating and transmission expansion program and cost as
prepared during appraisal (resulting in a summary similar to Annex 2, Attach-
ment 5) covering 5 years.
N.B.      The "scheduled year" should not reflect the existing republican plan
if, by the end of the relevant year, the financing plan has not been rounded
off or main contracts have not been signed and it is obvious that the plant
cannot be completed as scheduled previously.
(c)       Forecast (5 years) balance of energy and balance of capacities
(similar to Annex 2, Attachments 2 and 3) together with the summary for the
transmission pool (similar to Annex 2, Attachment 4).
(d)       Each republic's and autonomous province's investment program,
(reflecting (a)(b) and (c) above) in a format as prepared during appraisal,
and summarized for the country as a whole (similar to Annex 2, Attachment 6).
Information on investments in distribution should also be included, if
available.
