A.   For Each Republic
1.        Combined Balances of Energy and Capacities
See Annex 2, Attachments 1, 2 and 3, suitably amended and combined
for each republic, showing:
-    Sales to direct consumers (GWh)
-    Sales to distribution enterprises (GWh)
-    Sales (or transfers) to other republics (including jointly
owned plant) (GWh)
-    Sales to other countries (export) (GWh)
-    Transmission losses 380-kV, 220-kV, 110-kV (GWh and % of generation)
-    Station requirements, hydro and thermal (GWh and % of generation)
-    Gross own generation, hydro and thermal (GWh)
-    Purchases (or transfers) from other republics (including jointly
owned plant) (GWh)
-    Total production (gross generation plus purchases) (GWh)
-    Load factor (%)
-    Maximum demand (MW hourly average)
-    Installed capacity, hydro and thermal (MW)
-    Plant margin (installed capacity less maximum demand)
(MW and % of installed capacity)
At the bottom of the table should be shown in each column:
-    % growth rate over last year of sales to direct consumers and
to distributors
-    % growth or reduction over last year's total losses
-    % growth of total production
-    % growth of maximum demand
-    MW capacity added in the year, hydro and thermal
2.        Length (km) of transmission lines 380 kV, 220 kV, 110 kV, and
substation capacity (MVA) 380 kV, 220 kV, 110 kV.

YUGOSLAVIA
SECOND POWER TRANSMISSION PROJECT
The Yugoslav Power Sector Accounting System
1.        Each basic organization uses the chart of accoupts and financial
statement reporting forms used by all Yugoslav organizations. They have
adopted the double entry system of accounts and accrue income and expenditure
at the end of the accounting period.  The concept of profit (Dohodak) differs
in that it is derived before deduction of expenditure for salaries and wages,
interest on debt, insurance premiums, legal and contractual liabilities.
These expenditures are treated as appropriations of profit. Nevertheless,
the form of accounts used is readily adapted to the form of presentation
which is normally followed by the Bank when appraising public utility
projects.
2.        The appropriation of profit is aimed first to meet the organiza-
tion's social obligations both legal and contractual before meeting obliga-
tions to the employee for salary, housing, training and recreation. There
are social obligations within the Yugoslav socialist system which require
each basic organization to contribute to the cost of education at schools
and universities; the Federal Fund for Underdeveloped Regions for assistance
to the Republics of Macedonia, Montenegro, Bosnia-Herzegovina and the prov-
ince of Kosovo, loans to community funds, other enterprises, and Government
institutions.
3.        Salaries are based on social agreements reviewed annually by the
Republican Government, the Chamber of Commerce and the workers' organizations.
Rates of pay are set for each category of worker within each industry. The
agreements take into account the need for sufficient funds to maintain the
living standards of the workers and to provide sufficient funds for expanding
output.
4.        Assets are not considered to belong to an organization but rather
to be entrusted to a basic organization for their management and control.
Depreciation funds may be used first to meet debt service requirements, then
to finance modernization or replacement of fixed assets, to meet social
obligations, and the remainder may be used to finance new construction.
5.        All cash and payments of accounts are regulated and supervised
by the Social Accounting Service (SAS) who are also responsible for auditing
in Yugoslavia. Each basic organization has its own account (referred to as
the Giro account) at the local bank. All business funds, reserve funds etc.
are maintained in this one account and all payments are verified by the
SAS before checks are sent.



ANNEX 5
Attachment 1
Page 2 of 2
6.        Each basic or work organization has its own internal control unit
appointed by the Worker's Council from its members to monitor the implementa-
tion of the decisions of the Council. These units do not exercise internal
control neither do the appointees have to have any special qualifications for
the tasks, nor do they have any particular program of work. Consequently
they could not be construed to perform an internal audit function.
7,.       The accounting methods and procedures are not prescribed (with the
exception of the chart of accounts and reporting forms mentioned in para 1)
and thus each organization is free to develop its own internal systems and
apply accounting procedures as it. sees fit. Financial statements are
prepared quarterly and closed off about the 20th of the"following month.
Annual accounts are closed off about January 31 and are required to be
submitted to the SDK by the end of February.
8.        Financial planning is developed in the form of 5-year plans.  The
present plan is for the period 1976-1980.
May 1977

YUGOSLAVIA
SECOND POWER TRANSMISSION PROJECT
REPUBLIC OF BOSNIA - HERZEGOVINA
ELEKTROPRIVREDA BIH
Organization
Elektroprivreda is a Work Organization composed of 17 BOALs (6 hydro-
plants, 2 thermal-plants, a transmission enterprise, a switchyard, 5 capital
investment and/or support service units, a fish farm and a worker's cafeteria)
from the power sector of the Republic of Bosnia-Herzegovina. It was estab-
lished when the 17 BOALs signed a Self-Management Agreement on December 21,
1973 by which it is governed. Statutes enacted in July 1974 provide for its
organization, self-management, conduct of business, use of resources and
distribution of income.
Elektroprivreda was established for the following principal purposes:
(i) preparation of major power development programs for
Bosnia-Herzegovina;
(ii) pooling of all free funds to be used for capital expenditure
in the power sector;
(iii) determination of the annual and long-term power balance;
(iv) collection and distribution of revenues to its members; and
(v) determination of electricity tariffs for the purchase and
sale of power.
Elektroprivreda operates under an Electricity Law enacted by the
Republic of Bosnia-Herzegovina on April 26, 1974. The law regulates power
industry organization, generation, transmission and distribution in Bosnia-
Herzegovina within the framework of the Yugoslav constitution. Under the
law a self-managing community of interest has been created by agreement
between the power producers and consumers in Bosnia-Herzegovina charged
with the responsibility for:
(i) coordination of plans for generation and transmission
throughout Bosnia-Herzegovina, and with JUGEL:
(ii) financing of power programs;



ANNEX 5
Attachment 2
Page 2 of 4
(iii) determination of bulk supply tariffs; and
(iv) conditions of supply.
Under Elektroprivreda's self-management agreement, the seventeen
basic organizations signatory to the agreement have adopted a 1976-80 power
development program. They have agreed to use all available free funds in
their business (equity) and depreciation funds to help finance its program.
Further, they have agreed to make available their free foreign exchange to
meet debt service payments on foreign loans. Contracts for foreign borrowing
must be signed by Elektroprivreda and the agreements approved by its Workers
Council. Other provisions require Elektroprivreda and its basic organizations
to sign self-management agreements setting out the methods to be followed by
Elektroprivreda when distributing the income from electricity sales and for
determining the internal prices which would be paid to each basic organiza-
tion for generation or transmission of power. The last mentioned agreements
became law on October 16, 1974.
Financing Plan
Tariffs and Special Levies
The tariffs in Bosnia and Herzegovina were increased about 18%
effective July 15, 1976. The tariff structure was also changed to eliminate
the two steps previously existing, thus giving consumers only one choice of
tariffs. The new tariffs also penalize consumers who do not modify their
demand in peak periods and who do not make efficient use of power.
The following surcharges have also been introduced in order to
raise funds for construction of electric power facilities; these are paid
monthly by the consumers together with their electricity bills. The final
amount of surcharge is determined annually based on the total consumption:
(a) Users of social funds: Din .05 per kWh.
(b) Citizens who perform economic services: Din .05 per
kWh plus Din 10 per month installed meter.
(c) Households: Din .02 per kWh plus Din 5 per month per
meter.
The collections of surcharge by BOALs engaged in distribution activities are
required to be paid into a special account with the Social Accounting Service
operated for the exclusive use of construction of power facilities; the col-
lections are then allocated by agreement between Elektroprivreda and the dis-
tribution organizations. In addition to the above surcharge, a law provides
for making special loans for development of the electricity power industry
through levies on economic organizations.



ANNEX 5
Attachment 2
Page 3 of 4
Based on these sources of funds and Elektroprivreda's internal gen-
eration of cash Elektroprivreda and the Community of Interest have drawn up
the power investment program for 1976-80. The financial forecasts assume
continuance of the special measures in 1981-82 also, and tariff increases of
5% in September 1977, 20% in January 1978, 16% in January 1979 and 20% in
January 1980. A summary of the financing plan for 1976-82 is given below:

It is seen that internal cash generation including customer contributions for
construction would cover 33% of the construction program. Thus, provided the
above tariff increases are obtained, the forecasts show that Elektroprivreda
should be able to meet the Bank's revised financial covenant in the years
1978 to 1982. The rate of return on revalued assets for 1976-1982 taking

Long-Term Debt
The long-term debt at the end of 1975 amounted to Din 3,770 million
repayable over an average term of about 35 years.  About 30% of this amount
was obtained from the Yugoslavia Investment Bank prior to 1962 at rates of
interest of 1-2% repayable over 50 years. Since 1962, long-term loans have
been provided by local banks and Federal Funds on less attractive but still
very reasonable terms (15-30 years at 2-4% interest). Future lending is
expected to be at higher rates of interest and generally to have shorter
payment periods. A statement of suppliers credits and other loans, local
and foreign, including loans from the Federal Funds for Underdeveloped
Regions, which Elektroprivreda hopes to secure for financing its capital
investment program in 1976-82 is given in Appendix A.
Present and Future Financial Position
The level of debt in Elektroprivreda's capitalization as of
December 31, 1976 (42%) was low but with the steadily increasing construction
program rising in 1982 to about 2.5 times the 1976 program, largely financed
by borrowings, the debt level is expected to rise steadily to a high 68% in
1982. In view of the heavy borrowing program resulting in sharply rising
debt service charges the annual debt service would be covered by internal
cash generation only 1.8 times in 1982 as against a comfortable 3.1 times
in 1976.
Performance Under Loan 836-YU
The two borrowers in Bosnia-Herzegovina under Loan 836-YU (Trebinje
and Sarajevo) were required to achieve a cash generation level of 35%. Trebinje
having achieved an average level of 50% in 1971-75 and Sarajevo 47%, their per-
formance was more than satisfactory. They would achieve the required self-
financing level of 35% in 1976 and 1977 also. Elektroprivreda, the composite
organization, which is not subject to the agreement under Loan 836-YU, would
achieve a cash generation level of 34% in 1976 and 32% in 1977.
May 1977

YUGOSLAVIA
SECOND POWER TRANSMISSION PROJECT
REPUBLIC OF CROATIA
ZAJEDNICA ELEKTROPRIVREDNIH ORGANIZACIJA HRVATSKE
Organization
During 1950-1965 Zajednica existed as a single enterprise with cen-
tralized management function responsible for the planning, development and
operation of all the generation and transmission enterprises in Croatia. In
1965 with the decentralization of the electric power industry in Yugoslavia,
all of Zajednica's functions were transferred to the individual enterprises
except that of maintaining relations with other republics. With the signing
of the Self-Management Agreement of January 30, 1975 Zajednica became a
composite organization of all BOALs in the electric power sector in Croatia,
effective January 31, 1975. The main principle underlying the Self-Management
Agreement is that each BOAL is enabled to operate under the same financial
conditions irrespective of its inherent advantages or disadvantages and this
is achieved by internal unit prices being fixed each year (in November) by
Zajednica for each BOAL and by pooling all surpluses and deficits of the
BOALs, the common pool of funds being operated by Zajednica, i.e. by all the
BOALs acting together. A measure of uniformity is thus achieved in opera-
tional matters. Planning and implementation are also greatly facilitated by
this centralization of activities.
Financing Plan
To finance the large capital expansion program during the period
1976-1980, the Community of Interest of Electricity Consumers of Croatia and
the Association of Electric Power Industry Organizations of Croatia have in
a Self-Management Agreement of 1976 agreed to the following special measures
for raising funds. The financial forecasts assume continuance of these
measures in 1981-1982 also.

Zajednica's financing plan aims at 40% of the total funds for expansion being
generated by the electricity consumers through internal cash generation in-
cluding the connection charge (naknade) and customer contributions (doprinos).
Given below is a summary of the financing plan for the period 1976-1982. The
plan assumes tariff increases of an average 9% from September 1977, 10% from
January 1978, 16% from January 1979, 13% from January 1980 and about 4% each
from January 1981 and 1982 over and above the 18% increase implemented with
effect from June 8, 1976. Provided these tariff increases are obtained, the
forecasts show that Zajednica would be able to meet not only the Bank's
revised financial covenant (minimum 30% cash generation) but also its own
objective of a 40% cash generation for each year through 1982. The rate of
return on revalued assets for 1976-1982 taking into account also the customer
contributions (doprinos) which are based on electricity consumption and which
could therefore be considered a surrogate for tariffs is as follows. The table
also gives the rate of return taking into account the connection charge which
really is also a surrogate for tariffs:

Although Zajednica's long-term debt as of December 31, 1975 was on
relatively soft terms (12% of total for 50 years at 1% interest or less, 4%
of total for 20-30 years at 2% interest and 84% of total for 10-15 years at
6.5-7.5% interest), future commercial loans from local Banks are estimated
to have a maturity of 10-15 years at interest rate of 6.5-8%. Out of the
foreign funds requirements, a USSR credit of US$10.4 million equivalent (2%
interest; 10 years), an Eximbank credit of about US$194 million for the Krsko
nuclear station (50% Zajednica's) and loans from European Banks totalling
about US$217 million, also for the nuclear station, have been arranged
leaving still a significant gap in the financing plan.



ANNEX 5
Attachment 3
Page 4 of 4
Present and Future Financial Position
The level of debt in Zajednica's capitalization as of December 31,
1975 (46%) was low. With the large borrowings during the period 1976-1982
to support the heavy investment program the debt/equity ratio is expected
to rise to 55/45 in 1980 and then drop slightly to 52/48 in 1982. The in-
creasing debt service burden is reflected in the fact that the annual debt
service is covered only 1.8 times by the internal cash generation in 1982
as against 2.8 times in 1976.
Performance under Loan 836-YU
As against the 35% contribution to investment required under Loan
836-YU during the years 1971-1975, the self-financing level actually achieved
by the four borrowers in Croatia was as follows:
Osijek          29%
Rijeka          35%
Split           38%
Zagreb          11%
Zagreb has argued that the covenant would be substantially fulfilled if all
the borrowers in Croatia including Zagreb were considered together. There is
merit in this argument since the additional measures necessary for achieving
the 35% contribution to investment (e.g. connection charge introduced in early
1973) were determined by the republic on the basis of their impact on the
entire sector and not on one particular enterprise. A contributing reason for
the non-fulfillment of the covenant was the enormous increase in the construc-
tion program owing to the construction of the Krsko nuclear plant. With the
proposed tariff increase from September 1, 1977 these borrowers would meet the
covenant in 1977.

YUGOSLAVIA
SECOND POWER TRANSMISSION PROJECT
REPUBLIC OF MACEDONIA
ELEKTROSTOPANSTVO
Organization
Following the constitutional changes of 1974, the constituent units
of Elektrostopanstvo, the borrower under Loan 836-YU, reorganized themselves
into BOALs, thus acquiring more operational autonomy than before. In order
to realize the Social Plan of Macedonia from 1976 through 1980, the five BOALs
engaged in the generation of electricity in Macedonia and the BOAL engaged in
the transmission of electricity from January 1, 1974 (Elektroprenos) entered
into a Self Management Agreement establishing with effect from January 1,
1976, Elektrostopanstvo, a work organization charged with the responsibility
of coordinating the planning, construction and financing of the new power
investment program in the republic. Elektrostopanstvo therefore remained
practically unaffected by the organizational changes. All the capital invest-
ment funds are pooled together in Elektrostopanstvo which (i.e. the BOALs
collectively) determines the allocation of funds amongst the BOALs for the
realization of the agreed investment program. Elektrostopanstvo sells power
to the only distribution company in Macedonia and six large direct consumers
(one ferrochrome industry, two chemical industries, one cement industry, the
railways and one smelting factory).
Financing Plan
To finance the large capital expansion program for 1976-1980 the
Self Management Agreement has determined the following sources of funds
apart from the free funds generated by Elektrostopanstvo from operations,
foreign credits which would be necessary for financing the foreign costs
and local commercial Bank loans. The financial forecasts assume continuance
of the measures for raising funds during 1981-1982 also.

1976-1982

(a) Customer contributions for new construction - All

Assuming tariff increases averaging 10% effective September 1, 1977, 20% in
January 1978, 3% in January 1979 and 5% in January 1980, Elektrostopanstvo is
estimated to achieve a self-financing level of 27% in 1977, and ranging from
30-52% during 1978-1982. The average cash generation during 1976-1982 would
be 34%. Provided the above tariff increases are obtained, the forecasts
show that Elektrostopanstvo would meet the Bank's revised financial covenant
(minimum 30% cash generation) through 1982. The rate of return on revalued
assets for 1976-1982 taking into account also the customer contributions
which are based on electricity consumption and which could therefore be
considered a surrogate for tariffs is as follows:

It is seen that internal cash generation (including customer contributions)
would cover 34% of the construction program thus enabling Elektrostopanstvo to
meet the proposed financial covenant during 1976-1982. However, this is con-
tingent upon significant tariff increases in the immediate future (10% in
September 1977 and a further 20% in January 1978) and minor tariff increases
in January 1979 and January 1980.
Present and Future Financial Position
The level of debt in the capitalization at December 31, 1976 (38%)
is satisfactory; the current ratio (2.4:1) is also adequate. However, with
the heavy borrowings necessitated by the large investment program, the
debt/equity ratio is estimated to rise steadily from 38/62 in 1976 to 59/41
in 1980 and then drop slightly to 57/43 in 1982. The adverse impact of this
on the organization's financial position is evident from the steady drop in.



ANNEX 5
Attachment 4
Page 4 of 4
the debt service coverage from 3.6 in 1977 to 1.9 in 1982. After 1982, when
the full impact of the new loans at more stringent terms (e.g. local Bank
loans are for 15 years plus grace period with interest at 6.5%) than the old
ones (which often carried a rate of interest as low as 2% and a maturity
period as long as 50 years) begins to be felt after the grace periods are
over, the debt service coverage would become increasingly precarious. Since
the scope for significant additional revenues through tariff increases is
limited, both Elektrostopanstvo and the Bank would keep the organization's
earnings and investment program under close watch through the proposed
annual review in January of each year.
Performance Under Loan 836-YU
Elektrostopantsvo made a contribution to investment of an average
66% in 1971-1975 as against the 35% required under Loan 836-YU.   During
1976-77 also, it would meet the covenant which permits funds raised outside
the sector for power sector development also to be included in internal cash
generation.

ANNEX 5

Appendix A
Page 1 of 1
NOTES TO FINANCIAL STATEMENTS
Income Statement
Tariffs. In addition to an average tariff increase of 15% effective
August 1, 1976, the forecasts assume average tariff increases of 10% effective
September 1, 1977, 20% effective January 1, 1978, and 3% effective January 1,
1979 and 5% effective January 1, 1980.
Salaries and Wages. Salaries and wages were raised about 8% effec-
tive January 1, 1976. Although the trade union for all industry is of the
view that electric power sector salaries are higher than those of other
sectors, and may not need to be revised significantly in future, Elektro-
stopanstvo considers that wage increases of at least 10% would in fact be
inevitable from 1977 onwards. The estimates therefore provide for a 10%
increase in the wages of workers in 1977, 1978 and 1979 and slightly lower
percentage increases in 1980-82.
Balance Sheets
Revaluation of Assets. As of December 31, 1975 all fixed assets
and accumulated depreciation were revalued in accordance with the coefficients
established by the Federal Government raising the net value of fixed assets
by 93%. The Business Fund is also increased by the amount of revaluation
surplus. The law also requires future revaluations when prices of equipment
and construction costs increase more than 10%. The forecasts reflect re-
valuation of fixed assets every two years beginning with 1977 assuming a 6%
increase in the price level every year.
Accounts Receivable. All electricity bills are issued on the 1st
of each month and required to be paid by the 15th of the month. Penalty
for late payment is at 12% interest per annum. Since even court action
is permitted for continued delay in payment, Elektrostopanstvo does not
anticipate any delay in the payment of electricity bills.
Long-Term Debt. Elektrostopanstvo's long-term debt as of December
31, 1975 was on relatively soft terms (bulk of it at 2-3% interest with
maturity ranging up to 50 years). However such soft terms are no longer
available and future borrowings are assumed to be at rates of interest at
6.5% or more with maturities of around 15 years plus construction period
as grace period for local Bank loans and 6-8% with maturities from 8 to 10
years for foreign suppliers credits. Loans from the Fund for Less Developed
Regions would be for 18 years at 4% interest.
May 1977

Organization
Elektrocrnagora, the transmission BOAL in Montenegro associated with
three other BOALs (Perucica hydroplant, Piva hydroplant and Elektrogradna for
construction) as from January 1, 1976 to form Elektroprivreda Crne Gore. The
Community of Interest was established on April 1, 1976. Since July 1976
Montenegro also has had a "work organization in formation" for the construc-
tion of Plevla thermal plant. The total number of staff at December 31, 1976
in the four BOALs and Elektroprivreda, the associated enterprise, was distri-
buted as follows:
No. of Staff

Piva                                                   110
Elektrogradna                                          170
Elektroprivreda                                         55

Financing Plan
Tariffs
Tariffs in Montenegro have been significantly raised every year
since 1971 as shown below:
July 1, 1972                                           15%
May 1, 1973                                            12%
August 15, 1973                                         8%
June 15, 1974                                          21%
May 15, 1975                                           32%
August 1, 1976                                         15%
Tariffs are generally expected only to cover costs, including depreciation
and interest and not intended to generate funds for investment. The forecasts
assume a 10% increase in August 1977, a 5% increase in January 1978, a 6% in-
crease in January 1979, a 5% increase each in January 1980 and 1981 and a 21%
increase in January 1982.
The Social Plan for 1976-80 does not contemplate any levies or taxes
designed to raise funds for new investment, nor is deferral of existing debt
service permitted for making funds available for investment. However, article
64 of the law of the electric power industry mentions the possibility of
raising funds for investment in various ways and so surcharges could be intro-
duced as in other republics for this purpose. The financing plan assumes the
levy of customer contributions for construction' from 1978 so as to cover
about 13% of the investment requirements in 1976-82. The Social Plan visual-
izes that the Federal Fund for Undeveloped Regions would finance 18.7% of
investment (12-15 years at 7-8%) and the pooled resources 23.7% of investment
(loans for 20 years at 6%). A summary of the financing plan for 1976-1982 is
given below:

Present and Future Financial Position
The level of debt in the capitalization was estimated to be 62% as at
December 31, 1976 and is expected to remain in the range of 56-62% in 1977-82.
The annual debt service coverage of 3.3 times in 1976 falls steadily to 1.5
times in 1982, a reflection of the fast growing debt service burden.
Performance Under Loan 836-YU
Titograd's cash generation level during 1971-75 was 42% as against
35% required under the agreement under Loan 836-YU.
May 1977

SECOND POWER TRANSMISSION PROJECT
REPUBLIC OF SERBIA
ZDRUZENO ELEKTROPRIVREDNO PREDUZECE
Organization
Elektroistok became a work organization coordinating all trans-
mission in Serbia under a Self Management Agreement as of December 31, 1973.
It has 6 BOALs namely:
(1) Transmission/Belgrade
(2)        "     /Srbobran
(3)        "     /Valjevo
(4)        it    /Krushevac
(5) Design
(6) Construction and Maintenance
ZEP, the associated enterprise in Serbia, will finance 10% of
Elektroistok's investment plans during 1976-80 through loans from pooled
sources on the following terms:
25 years at 3% - the grace period will expire 6 months after
new plant is put into service. Repayment of loans will then
commence - during grace period interest is deferred and
included as part of the loan.
5% of Business Funds of commercial organizations is to be
pooled by ZEP for financing investment in Serbia (see law
on financing 1976-80 Plan).
35% of investment plan will be financed by foreign loans -
assume 15 years at 7%.
Deferred loans - interest and principal payments on some
old loans have been deferred and therefore are a source of
funds.
ZEP sells power to 11 distribution enterprises in Serbia and nine
direct industrial consumers. The internal prices are set in accordance with
the methodology given in the Self Management Agreement.

Present and Future Financial Position
The level of debt in ZEP's capitalization at end of 1982 is esti-
mated to remain the same as at end of 1976 (57%). The annual debt service
coverage falls from 2.3 in 1976 to 1.9 in 1982, a reflection of the assumption
of debt on more and more stringent terms and of the growing debt service
burden.

YUGOSLAVIA
SECOND POWER TRANSMISSION PROJECT
REPUBLIC OF SLOVENIA
ZDRUZENA ELEKTROGOSPODARSKA PODJETJA SLOVENIJE
(EGS)
Organization
The composite organization in Slovenia, EGS, includes all work
organizations for generation and transmission and also mining enterprises
engaged in producing lignite for power generation. Distribution companies
are also associated with EGS but do not pool their resources for generation
and transmission projects. There is a clear trend towards unification of
transmission enterprises in Slovenia, as in other republics, perhaps a
stronger trend here than elsewhere. The change will commence in Slovenia
with the Project being constructed by EGS.
Financial Arrangements
In Slovenia, the Community of Interest has more powers than in most
other republics; it approves the investment program and is empowered to raise
funds through other measures. These measures provide the bulk of the pooled
sources of funds for new investment and are made available as grants. A
business committee makes a detailed plan each October for the following year;
the plan determines the amount of pooled funds (created by special measures)
to be allocated to each investment project.
EGS does not plan for generation of significant funds from its
operations. Tariffs are expected to cover operating costs including interest,
depreciation statutory commitments and transfers to Reserve Fund, Collective
Consumption Fund, etc., but are not set with a view to generating any signi-
ficicant portion of the investment funds. These investment funds are gener-
ated through special measures (see below). In Slovenia, depreciation is
normally charged only at minimum rates unlike in other republics where
accelerated depreciation can be applied. However at year-end, Slovenia
could use higher depreciation to absorb any operational surplus. Deprecia-
tion is used first to meet repayment of debt and up to about 20% of deprecia-
tion funds can be used for planned investment maintenance, i.e. renewal of
existing assets, the expenditure on which is not part of the investment
program.

Attachment 7

Present and Future Financial Position
The present level of debt in the capitalization as of December 31,
1976 (56%) is acceptable. This rises to 61% at the end of 1977 owing to
heavy borrowings to meet the substantial capital expenditures.  However, with
more and more funds accruing from customers and other enterprises amounting
to Dinars 5,726 million in 1982, the organization would be able to liquidate
a substantial portion of its debts at that time. The debt/equity ratio is
therefore expected to be only 28/72 in 1982.
Since tariffs are so designed that operational surpluses are kept
low, the internal cash generation is only slightly more than adequate to cover
the annual debt service during the period 1976-1982. The terms of the long-
term debt at the end of 1975 amounting to Din 5,952 million are summarized
below:

YUGOSLAVIA
SECOND POWER TRANSMISSION PROJECT
Justification of Project in the Context of the Power Sector Development
General
1.        This Annex is in 4 parts:
A.   Justification of Project.
B.   Nature and relative size of economic distortion that may
have been caused by the Yugoslav planning system.
C.   Rate of return on Yugoslavia's power development program
1977-1982 in generation and transmission (lack of informa-
tion on expansion in distribution and retail supply re-
venue does not allow calculating the rate of return at the
retail level).
D.   Comparison of Slovenia's tariffs and rates with the marginal
cost of supply in that republic (only for Slovenia is in-
formation sufficiently complete to allow the comparison)
with the objective of reaching general conclusions for
Yugoslavia's tariffs and rates (which are similar through-
out the country).
2.        By studying the above aspects in the context of Yugoslavia's power
development procedures and policies, a reasonable assessment can be made of
shortcomings and problems that require solution in the future.
A.   Justification of Project
3.        Economic justification of a project rests on the ability to identify
feasible alternatives and demonstrate that each will impose higher economic
cost on the society than the project selected. The alternatives are defined
in terms of the technical objective which is being sought. Economic assess-
ment determines the alternative to be selected. For example, in the case of
a transmission line linking a generation plant to a load center, the objec-
tive is to supply power to the load center subject to a set of engineering
constraints such as reliability, stability and optimum power flow. Technically,
site generation is identified as the only alternative to a transmission line.
The choice between the two falls in the domain of economics, where the lower
cost alternative is selected. Execution of the transmission line at various
voltage levels constitute further sub-alternatives for the transmission line.



ANNEX 6
Page 2 of 22
4.        It is obvious that the benefits of scale (lower capital and personnel
cost per kW, higher efficiency - i.e. lower kWh cost per kWh, lower transport
cost of primary energy) require concentration of larger units in power stations.
These benefits are then in part offset by declining reliability (or avail-
ability) of plant and by the necessity to transmit power to the load centers.
It is equally obvious that as a consequence, local generation - although it
avoids substantial transmission facilities - can only compete with larger plant
(plus transmission) on a limited scale. Its capital cost per kW is up to double
(say $500-700) that of large stations (say $250-500) while the cost of trans-
mission line is in the order of $0.5/kW.km (i.e. 100 km of lines would add only
some $50/kW to the cost of a larger station).
5.        Two aspects, one of general and one of particular importance in
Yugoslavia, make the requirements for an extensive transmission system even
more marked:
(a) Site generation reduces the potential for international and
inter-republican power exchange and optimum dispatching,
further increasing the operational cost.
(b) Yugoslavia has unique opportunities to locate power stations
near or on the sources of primary energy, thus reducing the
transport cost of primary energy to a minimum. These sources,
however, are not evenly distributed among the republics and
even not within republics. This requires both transmission to
the load centers, and between supply centers (to enhance the
benefits of scale further).
6.        From the above it follows that, in particular for Yugoslavia, the
optimum "mix" of larger - and concentrated - generation plant, transmission
facilities, and smaller local generating plant, inclines to be biased heavily
in favor of large stations and an extensive transmission system. Site gener-
ation has only limited justification (appropriate spread of reserves through
the country, peaking benefits, see para. 26) and does not constitute a sub-
stantial alternative to a transmission system, particularly not in Yugoslavia.
7.        Since a least-cost development plan for the Yugoslav system devel-
opment is not available, the justification of the entire 380 kV network
(Phase I and II) will be carried out in two dimensions, economic and
engineering. The last section will discuss the impact of the least-cost
development program on transmission networks.
Economic Justification
8.        The large system and international interconnections study committee
of the International Union of Producers and Distributors of Electric Energy



ANNEX 6
Page 3 of 22
state that new transmission voltages are introduced to an existing grid
because of one or more of the following reasons. 1/
(a) transmission over long distance;
(b) transmission of power generated by very large power
stations or by a group of power stations concentrated
in some geographical areas;
(c) the limitation or reduction of fault currents;
(d) improvement of system stability;
(e) better utilization of line corridors and protection of
environment requirements; and
(f) international connection.
9.        It is possible to test the networks of kV rating higher than 380-kV
but this will not be needed here because JUGEL's technical feasibility study
has identified the 380-kV network as the optimum required network to meet the
Yugoslav system's high voltage transmission requirement until 1985. The 380-
kV network represents the highest capacity installed in any of Yugoslavia's
neighboring countries. Among these neighbors, Greece is still at the planning
stage, Italy does not anticipate a transmission higher than its existing 380-
kV until 1990, Austria is planning to upgrade its grid to 380-kV by early
1980, Hungary is in the process of constructing its 380-kV network, while
Romania has an existing 380-kV network. Since Yugoslavia is forecasting trade
in power with its neighbors, the compatibility of its high voltage network
with the neighboring networks will contribute to the efficient exchange of
power. Therefore, the highest network capacity required for Yugoslavia's
domestic use and/or international trade of power with its neighbors is the
380-kV network.
10.       For all practical purposes, this would leave an upgraded 220-kV
network as the only possible alternative to the proposed 380-kV transmission
grid. Some parts of the existing 220-kV network had already reached their
capacity. Meanwhile, a new 220-kV network would have required the acquisition
of rights of way considerably in excess of that required for the 380-kV net-
work. In addition, multiple double circuit lines would be required for
220-kV, while in most cases a single circuit lines suffice for 380-kV.
1/   The sub-group of experts for the choice of interconnection voltage,
choice of voltage level above 420-kV in Europe, International Union
of Producers and Distributors of Electric Energy, Vienna Congress,
May 23-28, 1976, p.3.



AMNEX 6
Page 4 of 22
II.       Transmission losses in a 220-kV network, equivalent to some 5,000
km (1985) would be considerable relative to the 380-kV network. Meanwhile,
the capital cost of constructing a 220-kV network that would render the same
service as a 380-kV network would be not less than the cost of developing the
latter.
12.       Therefore, on the basis of intuitive analysis it was demonstrated
that the alternative for the 380-kV network was another network of similar
configuration but different voltage rating. It was also established that the
380-kV grid constituted the upper limit for the Yugoslav power subsector's
required transmission capacity. Finally, because of optimum use of corridors
(that will also impose less cost on the environment), reduction in transmis-
sion losses and the capital and maintenance cost involved in the 220-kV
network, it was shown that the 380-kV network would represent the least
cost alternative.
Technical Justification
13.       The above analysis presented in favor of the 380-kV network leaves
two questions to be answered: (a) despite the economic desirability of the
380-kV grid, are the components of Phase II justified? (b) what is the
likely impact of a least-cost development study on the existing and proposed
configuration of the 380-kV network? The first question will be dealt with
by integrating the economic analysis presented above and the engineering
reasoning behind the justification of the components of Phase II. The answer
to the second question will then follow.
14.       The justification of the 13 lines and substations included in the
Project rests basically on: (a) the necessity to double 2 lines in view of
insufficient capacity (some 600-650 MVA) of the single line even under normal
conditions, (b) the creation of 5 rings (rather than doubling existing lines,
because this would be more expensive) since existing lines would be over-
loaded under certain outage conditions, and (c) 6 new connections.
15.       Items (a) and (b) are self-evident because they are dictated by
technical considerations which permit no alternative. Several lines pertain-
ing to item (b) are also similarly self-evident i.e.:
-    the line connecting Divaca to Italy should be at 380-kV
because this is the standard inter-European voltage level
and, although the magnitude of the load flows required is
unknown, the capacity should be as high as possible in
view of Yugoslavia's perennial difficulties in meeting
demand;
-    the line to the new plant (with a final capacity of 630 MW
or higher) at Bitola in Macedonia; although 220-kV could
be used the line should be at 380-kV because by 1983/84 a



ANNEX 6
Page 5 of 22
second connection with Greece would be completed, for which
similar considerations apply as for the connection with
Italy,
-    the line to the new aluminium plant at Obrenovac B should
be at 380-kV because this large plant (first phase 500 MW)
under construction is near the line (15 km),
-    the line to Banja Luka should be at 380-kV (although
demand would probably remain below line capacity until
1985) because in the future Banja Luka would constitute
the node of 3 meshes providing more adequate reliability
for the connection between the eastern and western halves
of the country (until 1985 the two wide-apart existing lines
will provide only limited reliability).
16.       For the two remaining connections, to Pancevo (near Belgrade) and
to Kranj (near Ljubljana), the technical justification may appear somewhat
doubtful. The Pancevo line, however, which is only 30 km long, would be
the first tranche of a 380-kV ring around the capital, to be completed by
1985.  By this time, the demand of Pancevo, which is an industrial centre,
is expected to be about 420-450 MW. Adding a 220-kV double circuit line
rather than a single circuit 380-kV line (which would be of about equal cost)
over 30 km appears not justified taking into account that within 4-5 years
the latter would have to be added anyway at unquantifiable additional cost
to the environment. The line to the industrial center of Kranj (present
supply at 110 kV) will be 35 km long and demand would be about 400 MW by
1985. Again, a double circuit 220-kV line - for which the second circuit
would be mounted once demand passes some 200 MW in 1982/83 - would be possible
and it appears that a comparison of alternatives would show a preference for
the 220-kV connection (although additional cost of 220-kV transformers at
Ljubljana would largely offset the higher cost of the alternative 380-kV
transformers at Kranj). The difference, however, would be small in view
of the short length of the line. The technical benefit of direct supply
at 380-kV are evident but largely unquantifiable; on the other hand reli-
ability of the single circuit 380-kV line would probably be somewhat less.
17.       Comparing total installed transformer capacity (at all transmission
levels) with maximum demand in MVA (assuming an average power factor of 0.85)
shows the following:



ANNEX 6
Page 6 of 22

MVA     _           MVA    %_
110-kv                            10,000    57        17,000   36
220-kV                             6,200    35        11,000   24
380-kV                             1,500     8        19,000   40
17,700   100        47,000  100
Maximum Demand MVA                 7,900              16,600
Transformer MVA available
per MVA of maximum demand          2.24                2.83
The program appears reasonable because all transformers also directly trans-
form to the intermediate distribution level of 31.5 kV (in several cases
10--kV) and about 2.5 MVA/MVA of demand in such a system constitutes a normal
average. The individual substation transformer capacity (the unit size is
standardized at 300 MVA and 400 MVA at the primary side for 380-kV) have
been selected on the basis of the forecast 1985 demand, i.e. demand occurring
about 4-5 years after completion of the project, which is reasonable and
would largely explain the probably temporary increase to 2.83 MVA/MVA of
demand.
18.       Conclusion.  Given the network as to be completed in 1977, the
additions to it included in the Project are all dictated by technical require-
ments which are also justified on the basis of economic assessment. The 35 km
of line to Kranj may be an exception but the difference with its 220-kV alter-
native would be negligibly small in comparison with the overall cost of the
Project. The additional transformer capacity appears slightly in excess of
1982 requirements but technical considerations (transport difficulties of
these large transformers are major in the mountainous country, and adequate
reserves should be locally available in view of these difficulties) justify
the use of the 1985 demand forecast for their installation.
The Impact of the Least Cost Development Study and Transmission Networks
19.       The approach adopted for the justification of the Project does not
assert that the least-cost development program will not have any impact on
the transmission network, it only gives an indication that the network
selected was optimum. Optimality of network capacity does not automatically
imply that the investment program on the grid is also optimum. The optimality
of the investment program in transmission is determined by the optimum
generating plan devised. The least cost development (LCD) program gives the
stream of investment associated with the selected network that will satisfy
the generation and load center constraints while simultaneously minimizing
the overall economic cost of the entire development program.



ANNEX 6
Page 7 of 22
20.       The LCD program might dictate different patterns of growth for
the generation facilities which in turn will advance some and retard other
portions of the network. The movement of the investment over time, especially
when not required, results in cost savings to the society. The basic ques-
tion to be determined is whether the Yugoslav approach to planning produced a
system development program that is significantly different from an alternative
least-cost development program to the extent that the Project timing proposed
would impose economic costs on the society. The deviation of the plan from
what would have been intuitively suggested by the LCD program will be referred
to as distortion. The section below will assess possible sources of distortions.
B.   The 380-kV Network, the Yugoslav Systems Planning and the Least-Cost
Development Approach
21.       If the locations of the load centers -- for which adequate forecasts
of future power requirements have been prepared -- are accepted as given, an
LCD study would then address the alternative types of generating plants, their
location and timing, and the transmission networks needed to link those plants
to the load centers. The types of plants and their capacities depend largely
on the prices and location of the primary energy resources. In turn, prices
depend on the relative energy resource endowment of the respective areas.
Finally, the transmission network is determined by the cost of moving the
energy from the sources to the load centers. The higher the kV rating, the
lower the number of lines required (be it at higher cost but at lower rate
of expansion compared with lower voltage lines).
22.       The new generating capacities, as planned in the 1977-1982 devel-
opment program, are practically all located near the primary energy resources.
The exceptions are oil-fired plants, the majority of which are required also
for district heating. Lignite-fired plants can be used for heating purposes
but, in view of their operating difficulties and environmental problems, they
are undesirable. Oil-fired plants that are not intended for district heating
have been planned because: (a) adequate financing could be found for thermal
plants but not for hydro plants, and (b) construction time is least.
23.       If economic distortions of any magnitude exist, they would be
due to:
(a)  the unjustified use of a certain type of primary energy
source;
(b)  the unit size of thermal plants;
(c)  selection of hydro plants where thermal plants would have
been justified and vice versa;
(d)  wrong location in view of relative difference in the local
(economic) cost of fuel;



ANNEX 6
Page 8 of 22
(e) timing;
(f) use of an interest rate which differs from the opportunity
cost of capital in Yugoslavia; and
(g) financial constraints.
The above points are discussed below:
24.       Use of Primary Energy Sources.  The capacity and type of plants to
be added until 1982 can be summarized as follows:
Plant Type                                  MW                 %
Hydro                                     1,696              16.4
Pumped Storage                            1,114              10.8
Oil-fueled                                  740                7.2
Oil-fueled (also district heating)          645                6.2
Coal-fueled                                 325               3.1
Lignite-fueled                            4,922              47.6
Lignite-fueled (also district heating)       50                0.5
Gas Turbines                                183                1.8
Nuclear                                     664               6.4
10,339             100.0
25.       The first nuclear plant, constituting 7% of total additional
capacity, is a pilot plant for gaining experience and, whether justified
or not in the classical sense, it should be excluded from consideration;
its benefits are on such a long-term basis that their weight can hardly be
taken into account -- or properly expressed -- in an economic comparison.
It appears unlikely, however, that for some time to come nuclear plants will
be more economic than lignite-fired plants. The lignite is of too poor
quality to be transported and used elsewhere than at the mines for substitu-
tion purposes; consequently, its economic value is about equal to the cost
of producing it (varying between Din 69/Gcal and Din 85/Gcal or $3.83-4.72/
Gcal) and far below the economic value of oil (about Din 1452/Gcal or $7.90/
Gcal).
26.       The oil-fired plants not used for district heating may appear un-
economic at current world oil prices, although it should be noted that 420
MW of the 740 MW had been ordered prior to the 1973 rise in oil prices.
However, oil-fired capacity will constitute only 7% of total installed
capacity (even less beyond 1982). Oil-fired plants were primarily selected
because: (a) no other plants could be completed in time (hydro being the only
alternative in some cases) to allow for a certain measure of reserve capacity
in the eastern part of the country, and (b) the lack of plant with peaking
capabilities is a Yugoslav perennial problem, lignite plants being relatively
unsuitable for this purpose and hydro plants being limited under adverse
conditions (winter and low hydraulicity). Distortions, if any, through the



ANNEX 6
Page 9 of 22
use of oil plants, therefore, should be relatively minor because of the low
percentage of capacity involved, and their offsetting advantages (peaking
capabilities and short construction period).
27.       Similar considerations are valid for the few pumped storage plants
under construction and planned. Normally these can be justified only if
thermal efficiency differentials at various loads of existing thermal plants
during pumping operations (the pumping load allows thermal plant to be loaded
nearer to its maximum efficiencies) are appropriately large; for lignite
plants this is generally not the case. A direct comparison of the large
(594 MW) pumped storage plant at Bajina Basta with an equivalent capacity
of gas turbines - as probably the most appropriate alternative in the large
Yugoslav system - shows that with capital costs about equal (some US$250/kW)
operational costs of the gas turbines are about double those of the incre-
mental generation to be provided by existing lignite plant during pumping
hours of Bajina Basta. Although a full system study would be required to
prove the assumption adequately, it appears that in view of the existing
lack of peaking plant, the introduction of the pumped storage plant would
not cause appreciable economic distortions, provided appropriate country-wide
least-cost dispatching takes place.
28.       Unit Size.  Operation of poor quality lignite plants--as predomi-
nantly used in the Yugoslav system (some 6,400 MW, or 71% of non-nuclear
thermal plant by 1982) -- is difficult and the Yugoslavs have followed the
prudent policy of modest incremental steps in unit size (proven to have been
also a wise policy in view of the fact that lack of capacity arose in the
early seventies and will continue at least until the early eighties requiring
optimum reliability of the largest units). The largest unit size is now 310 MW
(4% of peak demand) and the next step would be 500 MW (1980) or still 4% of
peak demand (excepting the 630 MW nuclear unit at Krsko to be shared 50/50 by
Slovenia and Croatia). Although there is a lack of a national least cost
investment plan for power system development in Yugoslavia, republics are
increasingly becoming aware of the benefits of large size generating plants
(economies of scale). This is reflected in the investment plans of the
republics where two republics join forces to construct larger size plants
that are geographically located in one of the partner republics. The nuclear
plant at Krsko is an example of a joint investment aimed at capturing the
benefits of larger size generating facilities. This demonstrates that al-
though republics seek independence in the development of their power system,
they cross geographical boundaries when rational economic analysis dictates it.
29.       Hydro Plant Versus Thermal Plant Selection:  There is no doubt
that hydro plant development would have been more rapid in Yugoslavia if
(a) financing had been available, and (b) agreements could have been reached
between republics on basin water use (e.g. the Drina). Financial constraints
have in most cases played a deciding role (para. 33). Riparian issues are
as strong between republics as between countries, particularly in view of
their possible impact on the economies of the undeveloped republics. They
must have caused some distortions with respect to power but it may also be
argued that the wait-and-see attitude and the consequential delays may benefit
the country as a whole in view of the opportunities of optimal economic use
of hydro resources, of which power is only a part.



ANNEX 6
Page 10 of 22
30.       Economic Location of Mines (and Power Plants).  The present prices
of fuel in the main mining areas are shown in Attachment 13. These prices, as
far as is known, now reasonably represent actual costs. Future prices would
depend largely on the scale of mining in each area, not substantially on the
location. No distortions are apparent because the richest areas (quantita-
tively and qualitatively) are being mined first. In the longer term, of
course, cost can be expected to rise because less rich fields would have
to be mined.
31.       Timing.  Timing of plants has largely been dec.ided by the procedures
of plant selection on an incremental (financial) cost basis (para. 32) but
modified due to financing constraints (para. 33). The procedures are expected
to be changed in the future (para. 32) but financing constraints -- which by
their nature conflict with the economic decisions -- are not expected to be
alleviated substantially in, say, the next 10 years. Distortions in timing
due mainly to financing difficulties have occurred in the past and can be
expected in the future.
32.       Opportunity Cost of Capital for Selection of Plant.  Use of other
decision parameter than Yugoslav power planning, up to the present, has been
on a financial basis, using incremental operational cost (including deprecia-
tion rather than annuities) as a benchmark for selection of plants in each
republic rather than opportunity cost of capital. For this reason distortions
must have occurred in the past. It is hoped, however, that with the introduc-
tion of LCD studies, whether on a country-wide or republican basis, the ranking
of plant will be economic rather than financial.
33.       Financial Constraints.  Resource constraints, particularly financial
constraints, have not played an important role in most of the Bank's LCD power
studies for making loans. They cannot be neglected in the case of Yugoslavia.
Borrowing for thermal plants has been relatively easy but for hydro plants
(with their high local cost component) it has been difficult, to say the least.
Consequently, construction of thermal plants -- with relatively low capital
costs -- whether least-cost or not, has surged in order to meet future re-
quirements on time. Some improvement, however, appears possible if, tied
to regularly updated LCD studies, the search for funds is coordinated on a
country basis rather than fragmented on a republican basis. Some experience
in this report may be gained through the Project if the policy presently
being followed for the 380-kV transmission is extended to generating plants.
Although unknown in quantity, the distortion due to financial constraints
may have been large and may remain relatively large in the future, upsetting
many of the LCD studies' recommendations.
34.       Transmission.  The difference between an appropriate LCD study
investment program and the current program would only lead to a change in
timing of the various plants to be added and the magnitude of the capacities.
The location of these plants might shift from one location to another but
the shift will be between the same locations identified by the sites of the
primary energy resources. These are also the same locations as used in the
Yugoslav plan. Given the load centers and given the location of the primary



ANNEX 6
Page 11 of 22
energy resources, it is concluded that the 380-kV transmission network would
be substantially invariant to the outcome of an LCD study; the present 380-kV
network configuration would not be significantly altered by the result of such
a study.
C.   Rate of Return on Yugoslavia's Power Development Program
35.       The internal economic rate of return (IERR) associated with a proj-
ect is defined as the discount rate which equates the present value of the
yearly net receipts (benefits-costs) over the life of the project. Benefits
(willingness to pay) are measured by revenues. The benefits resulting from
the Project cannot be determined. Therefore, the cost and revenues associated
with the entire Yugoslav development program for generation and transmission
for the years between 1977 and 1982 are used to determine the return on invest-
ment. This excludes all investments in distribution facilities. The average
tariff at the bulk level is used as a proxy for the benefits associated with
the program because tariffs at the distribution level are not available except
for Croatia. In the latter case tariffs at the bulk level are not available,
thus benefits may be somewhat overestimated. Attachment 4 summarizes the
average revenue per kWh between 1977 and 1982 for each republic. 1977 prices
have been used for calculation of costs and benefits.
36.       The assumptions for capital cost and operating and maintenance cost
are presented in Attachment 3, which also shows the details of the relevant
incremental cost and revenue streams. All values are expressed in 1977 prices.
37.       The calculation assumes a discount period of 25 years, equal to
the assumed economic life of 25 years of thermal plant. Economic life of the
hydro plant was assumed at 40 years and 30 years for both transmission and dis-
tribution. Book values at the end of the period considered for the analysis
(25 years) were calculated using a sinking fund approach to determine deprecia-
tion.
38.       Local cost components of the capital cost associated with the various
facilities in the development program were expressed in terms of border prices
by using a construction and equipment correction factor for Yugoslavia of 0.86.
Expected oil and uranium consumption was expressed in terms of international
prices. Domestic prices (and border price factor) were used for lignite and
(brown) coal used for generation because they are of poor quality not suitable
for export. The maintenance cost is assumed to be 1% of the total capital cost,
corrected appropriately to reflect border prices for the assumed foreign com-
ponent, for the hydro plant and transmission and distribution facilities, and
2% of the capital cost for thermal plant (similarly corrected). A standard
conversion factor of 0.93 was used to express the yearly incremental revenue
in terms of border prices. As can be observed in Attachment 1, the effective
conversion factor for total cost approaches 0.91, compared with 0.93 for the
benefits.



ANNEX 6
Page 12 of 22
39.       Sensitivity Analysis.  The IER is shown (Attachment 1) to be 13.55%
on the basis of the average bulk rates that would enable each borrower to meet
the financial covenant. This compares with the opportunity cost of capital of
9.6% estimated for Yugoslavia. Assuming an increase of 18% in the cost of the
program, the IER would be 9.25%. An increase in rates of 10% would raise the
IER to 16.4%. The IER is more sensitive to changes in capital cost than to
changes in rates. It is essential to bear in mind that the calculated IER
excludes the contribution of all customers to system development costs. The
contributions are non-returnable and in essence constitute a prepayment for
future capacity demand. The IER on the program is therefore likely to be
understated.
40.       Croatia is the only republic in Yugoslavia whose generation, trans-
mission and distribution organizations are integrated. As a result the
revenues at the distribution level were available and the IER for the entire
program was calculated. The IER (Attachment 2) is 3.8% using only revenues
from tariffs as a measure of benefits. Croatia is not representative of
Yugoslavia because, among other differences, generating plant is almost
exclusively oil-fueled type and fuel costs are therefore high in comparison
with other republics. For these reasons no meaningful conclusions can be
drawn from the present data; when further information on distribution costs
and revenues from other republics is available it would be useful to complete
the analysis.
D.   Evaluation of the Yugoslav Tariff System
General
41.       Because the tariff structure in the various republics is the same
for bulk tariffs and approximately the same for the distribution tariffs,
while rate levels are not too far apart (particularly not for bulk supply,
see Annex 1, Attachments 8 and 9) preliminary conclusions can be drawn in
advance of the outcome of the ongoing tariff study, by comparing present
tariffs and rates with marginal costing of supply for 1977/1982 in a single
republic. Slovenia was studied for this purpose because information and data
obtained for its power subsector were the most complete of all republics.
42.       The present tariff structure was introduced in Yugoslavia some
10 years ago and has not basically changed since. Although there have been
numerous rate increases, these have taken the form of percentage additions
across the board, thus retaining the original relativities. Consequently, it
is not expected that the rates noted reflect marginal costs of supply, even if
they did so 10 years ago. The use of the expedient of relatively high levies
(repayable and non-repayable, see Annex 1, para 29) to meet financing require-
ments for expansion indicates the existing of distortions.
43.       The present analysis uses the information as provided by Slovenia,
not as somewhat modified (see Annex 2) during appraisal, i.e. it would re-
flect a similar marginal cost study if undertaken by Slovenia on the basis
of its own assumptions. Some simplifications were made since the objective
is to draw general conclusions without going into unnecessary detail; an
in-depth study should be left to the Yugoslavs.



ANNEX 6
Page 13 of 22

Dependency on thermal plant would grow (including jointly owned plant and
nuclear plant) from 60% to 68%. The capacity balance (including jointly owned
plant) shows (Attachment 5) a plant margin of 28% (291 MW) for 1975 and 31%
(752 MW) for 1982, which appears reasonable in view of the declining depen-
dence on hydro and the large size of the nuclear unit. The energy balance
(Attachment 6) would remain fairly level at an excess of 350-400 GWh/a from
1975 through 1979 (in practice, on a "pool" level, Slovenia showed a deficit
in 1975, see Annex 2, para 8, indicating lack of network (and dispatch) capa-
biLities to other republics) except for 1977 (-89 GWh), rising to 1,129 GWh
by 1982, or 11% of own requirements - assuming average hydraulicity. This
indicates that meeting energy requirements will probably not be much of a
problem until 1982.
Average Incremental Capacity Costs for Generation, Transmission
and Distribution
46.       The Slovenian weekday load duration curve (Attachment 12) can be
divided into a night-time period of low demand (off-peak) which lasts from
11 h to 6 h and a broad peak period occurring between 6 h and 23 h. The peak
period is characterized by a broad plateau with two peaks at about mid-day and
19 h, the later peak being the higher. If the tariffs are not based on hourly
demand, the two extremes during the peak period are of no consequence for the
final outcome of the tariff design exercise. The summer daily peaks are
lower than their preceding winter counterparts with a minimum occurring in
July. The monthly peak demands, of course, grow from year to year; there is
a marked seasonal variation in both the daily load curve and the magnitude of
their monthly peak. In addition, there are seasonal variations in output
capacity because of the large hydro component. The Drava River is the prin-
cipal source of the variation during the year in the available hydro capacity
which, during the winter months, falls to 50%-60% of the summer capacity.
Thus, during the winter, when demand reaches its annual peak, available
capacity is at its lowest levels.
47.       Average incremental capacity costs for generation, transmission and
distribution have been calculated on the basis of Slovenia's 1976-1982 invest-
ment program (Attachment 7). The plan divides the investment stream into its
domestic and foreign components; to obtain border price estimates of local
costs, conversion factors for the various types of expenditures were used as
described in the footnotes to Attachment 7. 1/
1/   Conversion factors were derived from data supplied in the draft paper
"Yugoslavia: Preliminary Estimates of Country Parameters for Economic
Analysis of Projects", Initial Draft, January 10, 1975. This paper ex-
plains the reasons for the use of "border values" (or prices) rather
than "domestic values": the latter are converted to border prices by
multiplying them with the appropriate conversion factor.



ANNEX 6
Page 15 of 22
These expenditures are discounted at 10% interest rate, which is the estimated
opportunity cost of capital for Yugoslavia (the document referred to in the
footnote on the previous page calculates 9.6%, which has been rounded to 10%).
The discounted cost streams are summed to obtain the total present values of
investments at the generation, transmission and distribution levels respec-
tively. The average incremental capacity cost associated with generation,
transmission and distribution is obtained by dividing the discounted yearly
investments in each of the three processes by the sum of the discounted values
for the yearly peak demand during the planning period. The yearly average
incremental capacity cost (at both levels: main transmission (bulk) and dis-
tribution (retail) is obtained by annuitizing 1/ the discounted average incre-
mental cost for the system development program over 25 years at a discount
rate of 10% (because the objective is to reach general conclusions the cal-
culation was simplified by assuming a 25 year average for the economic life
of facilities). The results of these calculations are summarized as follows:
Present worth of the               Annuitized overall average
average overall incremental        incremental capacity cost
capacity cost associated with    associated with the plan-
the planning period                    ning period
(Din/kW)                       (Din/kW Year)
Unadjusted              Border    Unadjusted           Border
Prices                Prices        Prices           Prices
Generation           26,050                24,649        2,870             2,716
Transmission          7,583                 6,588          835               726
Distribution /1       7,646                 6,659          842               734
/1   For the average incremental cost at the transmission level, the dis-
counted value of the investments in generation and transmission is
divided by the sum of the discounted values of the incremental yearly
maximum demand at the transmission level. The system's average incre-
mental cost at the distribution level is obtained by determining the
ratio of the discounted development costs of the three processes and
the sum of the discounted values of the incremental yearly maximum
demand at the distribution level.
Overhead Costs Associated with the Incremental Capacity
48.       These costs include the salaries of administrative, operation and
maintenance staff and insurance costs. In the short run (a period of one year
or less) the number of personnel required to operate and maintain generation,
transmission and distribution facilities is determined by system installed
capacity rather than variations in energy production. Since thermal unit
size in the system increases, the personnel that will be required to operate
and maintain these facilities per kW of capacity decreases. In the absence
1/   Annuity factor = 0.110168.



ANNEX 6
Page 16 of 22
of data on future overhead costs, a fixed amount per kW was used (see Attach-
ment 8, note 1). Transmission and distribution 0 & M costs are predominantly
related to the total length of lines and partly to transmission and distribu-
tion system peak loads. In the absence of data on these quantities, overhead
costs for transmission and distribution were assumed to be a function of
installed generating capacity (see Attachment 8, note 1).

Allocation of Incremental Capacity-Related Costs
50.       Available net system capacity is shown in Attachment 9 for winter
and summer months respectively. In order to determine the seasonal peak and
off-peak periods, the system load data for December and July were taken as
the two representative months that represent the extreme variations of demand
on the system. In reality, available capacity varies throughout the year,
with the maximum in May-August and the minimum in December-March. In the
absence of more detailed data on seasonal capacity variations, the analysis
has been simplified by assuming two seasons.
51.       To appreciate the implications of the daily variations in demand,
approximate values have been calculated for capacity demand during the 13 h-
16 h "low" period of the daytime load plateau (shown as "Daytime Off-peak)
and for the night-time off-peak demand. Demand periods are listed below in
decreasing order of importance; the order is determined by the pressure that
the demand exerts on the system's maximum seasonal available capacity:
(a) Winter Peak demand is always above system winter assured
capacity, except in 1980 when the two are about equal.
(b) Winter Daytime Off-Peak demand is also above system winter
assured capacity, except in 1980 when it is 6% below
assured capacity.
(c) Summer Peak demand is, after 1977, always less than system
assured (summer) capacity, with assured capacity leading
by 0 to 1 year.



ANNEX 6
Page 17 of 22
(d) Summer Daytime Off-Peak demand 1/ is, after 1977, always
below assured capacity, the latter leading to 1 to 2 years.
(e) Winter Night-Time Off-Peak demand is, after 1976, always
below system assured capacity, the latter leading by about
3 years.
(f) Summer Night-Time Off-Peak demand lags system assured capacity
by about 5 years.
52.       Based on the above list, it would seem reasonable to allocate
capacity-related costs to the customer in the periods (a), (b) and (c) re-
spectively. Meanwhile, the pressure of consumption on capacity during the
latter three periods is sufficiently low relative to the system's assured
capacity to justify the exclusion of the capacity-related charges.
According to marginal-cost-pricing principles, the marginal tariff
would set the peak hours as follows:
Winter:
March-August inclusive: 2/
Peak    :   7 - 23 h (17 hours) weekdays and Saturdays
Off-peak: 11 - 6 h (7 hours) weekdays and Saturdays and all day
Sundays (24 hours)
Summer:
March-August inclusive:
Peak    : 18 - 22 h (4 hours) weekdays and Saturdays
Off-peak: 22 - 6 h (20 hours) weekdays and Saturdays and
all day Sundays (24 hours)
Total peak hours in one year              :  3,276 hours
Total off-peak hours in one year          : 5,484 hours
1/   In the analysis, summer demands have been assumed to be equal to winter
demands. In reality, they will be less, and summer peak demand will play
a minor role in determining system installed capacity.
2/   The definition of seasons, which is based on supply conditions, requires
further study. It appears that summer tariffs could apply to the month
of September too, if the hydraulicity of the Drava River is still at its
summer level. If this is so, then the level of demand in September in
relation to system installed capacity would not justify the application
of capacity changes during 6-18 h.



ANNEX 6
Page 18 of 22
Marginal Energy Costs
53.       The forecast of the energy-related costs associated with the exist-
ing and new plants in the system development program are presented in Attach-
ment 10. These costs include fuel costs as well as the proportion of the
cost of materials and maintenance that are directly energy-related. The cost
of supplying an increment of energy at different levels of demand is deter-
mined after making an approximation of the order of merit by which the future
generating plants in the system will be loaded in the different seasons. In-
dividual generating plants in Slovenia are listed in order of incremental
operating costs in Attachment 9, with cumulative capacity column shows the
net available system capacity if generating plants are brought into operation
by the republican dispatch center so as to minimize system average operating
costs. Peak demand during the winter and summer seasons (the latter estimated
at 90% of the winter peak) is shown in Attachment 9, with two rows which show
80% and 60% of peak demand for each year and both seasons. Defining a peak-
loading situation as one in which demand is between 80% and 100% of the peak
demand for that year and season, and an off-peak situation as one in which
demand varies between 60% and 80% of the peak, the generating plants that meet
the last MW of demand (the incremental plants) are as shown at the bottom of
Attachment 9.
54.       In a winter peak situation, the incremental plants are four of the
more expensive lignite plants and a gas-turbine plant (Brestanica-1). The
system marginal operating cost has been calculated by taking weighted average
of the operating costs of incremental plants. During winter off-peak periods
the incremental plants are the lower cost Sostanj Units 1 to 3 (lignite) and
Trbovlje 2 (lignite). During the summer, the increased availability of hydro-
power shifts thermal plant further up the loading schedule so that it is
possible to meet incremental peak demands using the more efficient Sostanj
Units 1-3 alone. During summer off-peak periods a combination of Sostanj
Units 3 and 4 are able to meet incremental demand. The similar operating
costs of Sostanj Units 1, 2, 3 and 4 result in a very small difference between
calculated system incremental operating costs for summer peak load and off-
peak load periods and consequently no distinction between energy costs asso-
ciated with these two periods will be made in this study. A composite peak
and off-peak system incremental operating cost has been estimated by forming a
weighted average of operating costs for Sostanj Units 1 to 4. The definition
of winter and summer peak and off-peak periods employed to calculate marginal
energy costs coincides fairly well with the definition adopted for the alloca-
tion of capacity costs. That is, during winter weekdays and Saturdays, demand
is usually above 80% of the peak during the 6 a.m.-11 p.m. period, and between
60% and 80% of the peak at other times. During the summer, the average
incremental energy cost remains constant throughout the day.
55.       The table below summarizes the average incremental energy costs
applicable to the various seasons:
