Cost Adjustments for System Losses
56.       The estimates of capacity and energy costs presented must be modi-
fied to account for energy losses at different levels of the system. Average
loss levels in Yugoslavia during 1970-75 have been 4.8%, 5.1% and 7.1% respec-
tively for generation, transmission and distribution. Transmission and dis-
tribution loss levels are about proportional to the square of the current flow
at any time, and hence are higher under peak load conditions. To take these
loss variations into account the average figures quoted above have been multi-
plied by the ratio of the mean square power level during the period in ques-
tion (peak or off-peak) to the daily mean square power level. These ratios
were derived from working-day load duration curves for December (winter) and
July (summer) of 1974, for Yugoslavia as a whole. Generating station losses
(consisting of power consumption by the stations themselves) are a roughly
constant fraction of power output, and in some cases (such as hydro stations)
may even be a decreasing fraction of gross station generated power as output
increases. The share of hydro-generated power in total generated power in-
creases by about 10% from winter to summer during 1977-1979. This seasonal
change in the relative shares of thermal and hydro power results in a 0.6%
difference between winter and summer generation losses. The table below
shows loss levels for peak and off-peak periods during the winter and
summer.
Power System Losses During the Winter and Summer Seasons
Winter                   Summer
Loss Level                        Peak     Off-Peak       Peak        Off-Peak
-------------% of Generation--------------
Generation                         5.1        5.1          4.5           4.5
Transmission (380-110kV)           5.8         3.4         5.9           4.9
Distribution (below 110 kV)        8.1         4.7         8.3           6.9

Marginal-Cost-Based Tariffs
57.       Based on the above discussion, a tariff structure and level can
be derived that reflects the average incremental costs of supplying electric
power in Slovenia. Tariffs are calculated for two customer classes. The
first customer class includes large industries and distribution enterprises
purchasing directly from the high voltage (110 kV and above) network; and the
second customer class includes the low voltage (400 V and below) consumers
that are connected to the distribution networks. The rates derived consist
of a capacity component related to the maximum monthly kW demand during the
peak hours, plus a kilowatt hour-related energy component.
Comparison of the Existing Tariffs with Marginal-Cost-Based Tariffs
58..      Attachment 11 presents the present tariff structure and rates
in Slovenia, as well as a marginal structure and rates as calculated above,
covering the period 1977-82, for the two most important types of supply:
bulk supply in the 110-kV, 220-kV, and 380-kV transmission system and retail
supply in distribution networks. It is necessary to make the following ob-
servations before reaching preliminary conclusions:
(a) there are large non-repayable levies on each bill as contribu-
tions towards expansion which could or could not be taken into
account; if the latter, the question arises, how?
(b) no check can be made without a statistical analysis (using
historical information on consumer classes and individual
bills) confirming that the marginal costs as calculated,
if applied, would meet the overall financial requirements
of the power sector in Slovenia (i.e. that levels should
either be set below or above marginal costs as calculated,
retaining relativities to meet these requirements);
(c) modifications could be made in order to adjust to the fact
that:
(i) metering facilities do not allow excluding Sunday -
even if contemplated - from peak charges, for several
years to come,
(ii) the assumption that in the future a noon peak will
not occur may be debatable in view of the Yugoslav
working hours, generally from 6-14.30 h. The neces-
sary calculations have not been made because the
difference would not modify the conclusions sub-
stantially; and
(d) the so-called "second step" rates that can be selected as
an alternative (at a lower demand rate but a higher kWh
rate) by the consumers is expected to disappear (in



ANNEX 6
Page 21 of 22
Bosnia-Herzegovina and Croatia they have already been
eliminated). This second step is therefore not included
in a comparison of present rates and marginal costs.
Conclusions
Bulk Sales
59.       (a)  Marginal capacity costs are Din 414/kW month applicable only
during the 6 winter months, compared with the present rate of Din 199.95/kW
month for 6 months and Din 40.10/kW month for the rest of the year. Assuming
that the average summer peak is 80% of the winter peak (MW), average revenue
per month from the capacity charge would in both cases, for the same hours of
application, be in the order of:
200 x 6 x MW + 40.10 x 6 x 0.8 MW             1
12
(i) ~ ~ ~ 1 xrsn   --         2=Dn16 x MW
(ii)  Marginal          12         = Din 207 x MW
Slight adjustments are necessary, of course, because the hours of application
are different in both cases (lower peak-hour rate from 13-16 h and 22 through
23 h for (i) and no Sunday capacity rate for (ii) but this does not materially
affect the conclusion: present capacity charges are about 50% of marginal
costs.
(b) Bulk supply kWh rates are substantially below marginal costs
(particularly because higher marginal rate levels would apply longer each day
except Sunday):
Marginal Costs                Present Rates
Time             para/kWh              Para/kWh    % of M.C.
Winter:        Peak               61.30                 30.10          65
Off-Peak           31.60                 14.-          44
Summer:        Peak               28.80                 24.05          84
Off-Peak           28.50                  8.-          28
It appears that present average energy charges are some 30-40% below marginal
cost.  The levy on each bill is 24% for the bulk sales (Annex 1, Attachment
10) as a contribution towards expansion. Although a precise calculation is
impossible without statistical data, it appears that the deficit in the capac-
ity charge of 50% would largely be removed if these contributions were accepted
as capacity charges. The deficit in the energy charge would remain, however.
In any event, the present rates are (i) on the average below marginal costs,
and (ii) do not provide, together with the levy, the correct message to the
user to adjust his demand in accordance with the costs of supply.



ANNEX 6
Page 22 of 22
Retail Sales
(c) While present energy charges for domestic supply approximate
marginal costs (higher winter usage can be expected to show average marginal
revenue per kWh not too far from the present charge of 57 para/kWh), the
capacity charge appears far below requirements. Assuming 3,276 hours of
application, at marginal prices (Din 609/kW month, see Attachment 11) a
small single phase consumer would pay an average of 609 x 3,276/8,760 Din/kW
month = 227 Din/kW month, compared with Din 19-35 (fixed). per month presently
(in Slovenia the fixed domestic charges are based on fuse ratings of 25, 35
and 50A).
(d)  For non-domestic supply, although the energy charges are in
excess of marginal costs (see last lines for both existing rates and marginal
rates in Attachment 11), the capacity charge is equal to that of domestic
supply.
(e) A surcharge of 60% applies for domestic and 48% for non-domestic
supplies (Annex 1, Attachment 10). Treating this as a capacity contribution
it appears that average domestic charges approximate marginal cost, but that
average non-domestic charges are in excess of marginal costs.
(f) While bulk supply is under-charged (see above), on the average,
retail supply is similarly overcharged.

ANNEX 6
Attachment 3
Page 4 of 5
YUGOSLAVIA
SECOND POWER TRANSMISSION PROJECT
Assumptions for Rate of Return Calculations
Notes
Page 1
1.        The estimates for plant generation are shown in Annex 2, Attachment 2
(Forecast Balance of Energy).
2.        Fuel prices are shown in Annex 6, Attachment 13.
3.        The figures for the plant heat rates have been provided by JUGEL.
4.        Plant costs are shown in Annex 2, Attachment 5 (Generating Expansion
Program and Costs) and the estimated expenditure schedules were provided by JUGEL.
5.        In order to obtain border prices, the present values of the local
costs were multiplied by the standard equipment conversion factor for Yugoslavia
of 0.86. All present values are shown both "inconverted" and "converted" because
the summary rate of return calculations indicates that for power in Yugoslavia
the complication of using border prices is of little value: the effective border
price factor for total cost is shown to be about 0.91 and, since the standard
conversion factor for the benefits is 0.93, the differences are marginal. For
this reason, the rate of return calculation for Croatia's program, was executed
without using border prices.
Page 2
6.        Net incremental thermal generation per republic is shown on the above
page 1.
7.        New incremental hydro generation per republic is shown in Annex 2,
Attachment 2 (Forecast Balance of Energy).
8.        For the calculation of generation and fuel cost per type of plant it
was assumed that the savings shown on the above page 1 for Bosnia-Herzegovina
and Croatia are to be divided as follows:



ANNEX 6
Attachment 3
Page 5 of 5
Bosnia-Herzegovina              Croatia
GWh           MDin          GWh           NDin
Coal            -250            - 60
Lignite         -120            - 29
Oil                -               -         -200           - 67
Total     -370            - 89         -200          - 67
Croatia has only one Plant (Plomin, see Annex 1, Attachment 7) of 125 MW
operating on coal and this was neglected.
9.        It was assumed (see loss factor) that losses would grow from the
present 7.5% to 10% by 1982, in steps of 0.5%. This is probably too high at
the bulk supply level for Croatia (because Croatia's oil plant requirements
for station supply would be 5 - 6%), which however makes little difference in
the sector calculation. For the calculation of Croatia's rate of return on
its program the loss figures were retained and may be somewhat on the low side;
no information, however, is available on distribution losses.
10.       For revenue calculations total annual generation per republic was
multiplied by the loss factor for the year and multiplied by the average repub-
lican revenue per GWh. It was assumed that the 1977 average revenue per GWh
would remain constant throughout the period. It should be noted, however that
the figures for 1977 are based on the rates set in mid-1976 and, because a
further rate increase can be expected in 1977, may therefore be rather low.
The overall average was calculated for calculating beyond 1982, because the share
of each republic in generation and sales is not known. Croatia's average revenue
per GWh is at the retail level. Because no information is available at the bulk
level, this may slightly overstate total revenue (Croatia constitutes about 20%
of total revenue and an overstatement of some 20% overstates total revenue by
about 5%).
Page 3
11.       The detailed information has been obtained from JUGEL; for the summary
see Annex 2, Attachment 6. Excluded are the costs related to the development
program of both Kosovo and Vojvodina because no information is available on their
revenues (consequently these are not included in the benefits). Also excluded
are small amounts for "various", and the cost of Croatia's distribution program.
Because the latter is required for the rate of return calculation on Croatia's
program, the information is shown in the relevant column of Annex 6, Attachment 2.
Croatia did not provide, however, an estimate for 1981 and 1982, and a cost of
MDin 2,700 has been assumed for both years.
May 1977
